Prophetic Dreams as Sources of Knowledge in the Quran
Author cites Quranic verses to argue that prophetic dreams convey truthful knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how the author uses Quranic examples to argue that prophetic dreams are valid sources of knowledge.

Core Idea
Prophetic dreams are not mere symbols; they transmit objective truth from Allah.

In Islamic epistemology, dreams of prophets hold a special status. This lesson examines a passage where the author cites four Quranic narratives – from Ibrahim, the Battle of Badr, the conquest of Mecca, and Yusuf – to demonstrate that these dreams convey truthful knowledge. Let's explore how each example supports the claim and what logical pattern emerges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's central claim
Read the passage and find the implicit thesis: that prophetic dreams (ru'ya) are a valid source of knowledge about future events. The author does not state it directly but proves it through examples.
The claim is epistemological: dreams can be truthful.
2
Extract each Quranic example
List the four examples in order: (1) Ibrahim's dream to sacrifice Ishmael, (2) the Prophet's dream at Badr showing the enemy as few, (3) the dream of entering Mecca, (4) Yusuf's dream of celestial bodies prostrating.
Each example is a distinct case from different prophets.
3
For each example, note how it demonstrates truthfulness
For each dream, identify the evidence of truth: (1) Ibrahim's son accepted it as a command; (2) the dream matched reality and prevented despair; (3) the dream was fulfilled with the conquest; (4) Yusuf's dream came true decades later.
The truth is shown by fulfillment or immediate acceptance.
4
Recognize the inductive argument pattern
The author does not use a formal syllogism but an inductive argument: multiple instances of truthful prophetic dreams lead to the general principle that such dreams are reliable sources of knowledge. This is a form of istiqra' (induction).
The argument gains strength from the number and variety of examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلام عندما أمر بذبح ولده اسماعيل خلال النوم (فَلَمَّا بَلَغَ مَعَهُ السِّعْيَ قَالَ يا بني إني أرى فِي الْمَنَامِ أنّي أَذْبَحُكَ فَانْظُرْ مَاذَا تَرَى فبادر الغلام الحليم اسماعيل (قَالَ يَا أَبَتِ افْعَلْ مَا تُؤْمَرُ سَتَجِدُنِي إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مِنَ الصَّابِرِينَ) وفي وصف معركة بدر الكبرى قال تعالى مخاطبا النبي محمد صلوات الله عليه (إذْ يُرِيكَهُمُ اللهُ فِي مَنَامِكَ قَلِيلًا وَلَوْ أَرَاكَهُمْ كَثِيرًا لَفَشِلْتُمْ وَلَتَنَازَعْتُمْ فِي الْأَمْرِ ) وكذلك رؤيا النبي الله المنامية لدخول جيش المسلمين لفتح مكة قبل حدوثه ( لَقَدْ صَدَقَ اللهُ رَسُولَهُ الرُّؤيَا بِالْحَقِّ لَتَدْخُلُنَّ الْمَسْجِدَ الْحَرَامَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللهُ أمنينَ)؛ وفي قصة يوسف عليه السلام رؤيا تخبر عن احداث مستقبلية قبل تحققها فقد رأى سجود والديه واخوته قبل مدة طويلة من تحققه (إِذْ قَالَ يُوسُفُ لأبيهِ يَا أَبَتِ إِنِّي رَأَيْتُ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ كَوْكَبًا وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ رَأَيْتُهُمْ لِي ساجدين وبعد ان تحققت رؤيا يوسف عليه السلام التي شاهدها في طفولته (فَلَمَّا دَخَلُوا عَلَى يُوسُفَ أَوَى إِلَيْه أبويه وقَالَ ادْخُلُوا مصرَ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ أَمِنينَ . ورفع أبويهِ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ وَخَرُوا لَهُ سُجَّدًا وَقَالَ يَا أَبَتِ هَذَا تَأْوِيلُ رُؤْيَايَ مِنْ قَبل قَدْ جَعَلَهَا رَبِّي حَقًّا)
Peace when he was commanded to slaughter his son Ishmael during sleep: (When he reached the age of striving with him, he said, 'O my son, I see in a dream that I am slaughtering you; so see what you think.' So the forbearing boy Ishmael rushed: 'O my father, do as you are commanded; you will find me, if Allah wills, of the patient.') And in the description of the great Battle of Badr, the Exalted said, addressing the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him: (When Allah showed them to you in your dream as few; and if He had shown them to you as many, you would have lost courage and disputed over the matter.) And likewise the Prophet's dream of the Muslim army's entry to conquer Mecca before it happened: (Certainly Allah has fulfilled for His Messenger the vision in truth: you will surely enter the Sacred Mosque, if Allah wills, secure.) And in the story of Joseph, peace be upon him, a dream informing of future events before their fulfillment: he saw his parents and brothers prostrating to him long before it occurred: (When Joseph said to his father, 'O my father, I saw eleven stars and the sun and moon; I saw them prostrating to me.') And after the fulfillment of Joseph's dream which he had seen in his childhood: (When they entered upon Joseph, he drew his parents to him and said, 'Enter Egypt, if Allah wills, secure.' And he raised his parents upon the throne, and they fell down before him prostrate. And he said, 'O my father, this is the interpretation of my dream from before; my Lord has made it true.')
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument by example. The author presents Quranic evidence to establish that prophetic dreams (ru'ya) are a valid source of true knowledge about future events. The author begins with the story of Prophet Ibrahim's dream commanding the sacrifice of his son Ishmael, which is accepted as a divine command even though it was a dream. The son's response shows immediate assent (tasdiq) to the truth of the dream. Next, the author cites the Battle of Badr where Allah showed the enemy as few in the Prophet's dream, a knowledge that prevented despair. Then, the dream of entering Mecca (conquest) is mentioned as a truthful prediction. Finally, the detailed story of Prophet Joseph's childhood dream of celestial bodies prostrating, which came true decades later, is given as the clearest proof. The author emphasizes that the fulfillment of the dream is called 'ta'wil' (interpretation/fulfillment). The logical point is that these dreams are not mere subjective experiences but convey objective truths, thus serving as an epistemological source alongside revelation (wahy).

Key Terms
رؤيا
منام
تأويل
صدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which event from the Prophet Muhammad's life is cited as a dream that showed the enemy as few?
A) The conquest of Mecca
B) The Battle of Badr
C) The Night Journey
D) The Treaty of Hudaybiyya

Key Takeaway
Prophetic dreams are truthful and serve as an epistemological proof in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another Quranic verse that mentions a dream (e.g., the dream of the Prophet to enter Mecca) and explain how it fits the pattern of truthful dreams taught in this lesson.
?
Compare and contrast a prophetic dream (like Yusuf's) with a non-prophetic dream mentioned in the Quran (e.g., the dream of the king in Surah Yusuf). Why is one considered knowledge and the other not?

Truthful Dreams of Non-Prophets: Evidence from the Quran
Author provides Quranic examples of truthful dreams experienced by non-prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Quranic examples of truthful dreams experienced by non-prophets and explain their epistemological significance.

Core Idea
Truthful dreams are not limited to prophets; they are a source of knowledge for ordinary people.

The author presents two Quranic stories of truthful dreams (ru'ya sadiqa) experienced by non-prophets: the two prison companions of Joseph and the king of Egypt. These examples serve to expand the epistemological argument that dreams can convey true knowledge about future events, even to those who are not prophets. By examining these narratives, we will see how dream interpretation (ta'bir) functions as an epistemic tool and how the fulfillment of the dream confirms its truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dreamers and their status
Notice that the dreamers are not prophets: the two men in prison are a cupbearer and a baker; the king is a ruler. The author deliberately chooses non-prophets to show that truthful dreams are accessible to common people.
Non-prophets include even a polytheist king, broadening the scope of ru'ya sadiqa.
2
Describe the dream content
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The cupbearer dreamed of pressing wine, the baker of birds eating from his head, and the king of fat cows and lean cows, and ears of grain. Each dream contains symbolic imagery that corresponds to real events.
The dreams are vivid and detailed, not merely random images.
3
Identify the interpreter and the interpretation
Joseph (Yusuf) interprets both dreams. For the prisoners, he gives immediate interpretations that come true. For the king, he provides a detailed interpretation along with a practical plan for the years ahead.
Joseph's interpretation is not guesswork; he attributes it to divine teaching.
4
Confirm the fulfillment in the text
The narrative explicitly states that the dreams unfolded exactly as Joseph interpreted: the cupbearer served wine, the baker was crucified and birds ate from his head; the seven years of plenty and famine occurred as predicted.
Fulfillment is the key epistemological marker that the dream was truthful.
5
Conclude the epistemological implication
These examples demonstrate that truthful dreams are not exclusive to prophets. Non-prophets can receive true knowledge through dreams, though proper interpretation (often by a divinely guided interpreter) is necessary to extract that knowledge.
This supports the broader claim that dreams are a legitimate source of certain knowledge (tasdiq) in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاء في القرأن الكريم ذكر رؤى صادقة رآها غير الانبياء من عامة الناس منها رؤيا صاحبي يوسف في السجن (وَدَخَلَ مَعَهُ السِّجْنَ فَتَيَان قَالَ أَحَدُهُمَا إِنِّي أرَانِي أعْصِرُ خَمْرًا وَقَالَ الْآخَرُ إِنِّي أَرَانِي أَحْمِلُ فَوْقَ رَأْسِي خَبْرًا تَأْكُلُ الطَّيرُ مِنْهُ نَبئنَا بِتَأْوِيلِهِ إِنا نَرَاكَ مِنَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ . قَالَ لَا يَأْتِيكُمَا طَعَامَ تُرزَقَانِهِ إِلا نبأتكما بتأويله قبلَ أَنْ يَأْتِيَكُمَا ذَلِكُما مِمَّا عَلَّمَنِي رَبِّي إِنِّي تَرَكْتُ مِلَّةَ قَوْمٍ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللهِ وَهُمْ بِالْآخِرَةِ هُمْ كَافِرُونَ ... يَا صَاحِبَي كَافِرُونَ ... يَا صَاحِبي السجن أما أحَدُكُمَا فَيَسْقِي رَبَّهُ خَمْرًا وأما الآخَرُ فَيُصْلَبُ فَتَأكُلُ الطَّيرُ مِنْ رَأْسِهِ قُضِيَ الْأَمْرُ الذي فيه تستفتيان ( ( تقول الرواية ان الفتيين هما ساقي الملك والآخر خباز الملك قد سجنهما الملك بعد ان اتهمهما في بعض الامور . فلما رأيا يوسف في السجن أعجبهما قوله وفعله وهديه فطلبا منه ان يفسر رؤياهما فأخبرهما بتاويل رؤياهما فكان الامر كما فسره يوسف عليه السلام، كما حدثت رؤيا صادقة لملك مصر في عهد نبي الله يوسف ﴿ وَقَالَ الْمَلِكُ إِنِّي أَرَى بَقَرَاتِ سِمَان يَأكلهن سبع عجاف وسبع سنبلات خُضْرٍ وَأَخَرَ يَابِسَاتِ يَا أَيُّهَا الْمَلَأُ أَفْتُونِي فِي رُؤيَايَ إِن كُنتُم لِلرؤيا تعبرُونَ . قَالُوا أَصْغَاثُ أَحْلَامِ وَمَا نَحْنُ بِتَأْوِيلِ الْأَحْلَام بِعَالِمِينَ ، وَقَالَ الَّذِي نَجَا مِنْهُمَا وَاذْكر بعدَ أُمَّةٍ أَنَا أُنَبِّئُكُم بِتَأْوِيلِه فَأَرْسِلُونَ . يُوسف أيُّهَا الصِّدِّيقَ أَفْتِنَا فِي سَبْع بَقَرَاتِ سِمَانِ يَأْكُلُهُنَّ سبع عجاف وسبع سنبلَاتِ خَضْرٍ وَآخَر يَابِسَاتِ لَعَلِّي أَرْجِعُ إِلَى النَّاسِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ . قَالَ تَزْرَعُونَ سبع سنين دأبًا فَمَا حَصَدتُمْ فَذَرُوهُ فِي سُنْبُلِهِ إِلَّا قَلِيلا مِمَّا تَأْكُلُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِن بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ سبع شِدَادٌ يَأْكُلْنَ مَا قَدَّمْتُمْ لَهُنَّ إِلَّا قَلِيلًا مِمَّا تُحْصِنُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِن بَعدِ ذَلِكَ عَامَ فِيهِ يُغَاثُ النَّاسُ وَفِيهِ يَعْصِرُونَ)
In the Noble Quran, mention is made of truthful dreams seen by non-prophets among the common people, among them the dream of Joseph's two companions in prison: (And two young men entered the prison with him. One of them said, 'I see myself pressing wine.' The other said, 'I see myself carrying bread on my head, from which birds are eating. Inform us of its interpretation; indeed, we see you among the doers of good.' He said, 'No food will come to you as your provision except that I will inform you of its interpretation before it comes. That is from what my Lord has taught me. Indeed, I have left the religion of a people who do not believe in Allah and are disbelievers in the Hereafter... O my two companions of prison, as for one of you, he will give drink to his master of wine; and as for the other, he will be crucified, and birds will eat from his head. The matter has been decreed concerning which you both inquire.') The narrative says the two young men were the king's cupbearer and the king's baker, imprisoned after the king accused them of some matters. When they saw Joseph in prison, his speech, actions, and guidance pleased them, so they asked him to interpret their dreams. He informed them of the interpretation of their dreams, and it happened as Joseph interpreted. Similarly, a truthful dream occurred to the king of Egypt during the time of the prophet Joseph: (And the king said, 'I see seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones, and seven green ears of grain and others dry. O eminent ones, explain to me my vision, if you can interpret visions.' They said, 'Jumbled dreams, and we are not knowledgeable of the interpretation of dreams.' And the one who survived of the two said, after an interval of time, 'I will inform you of its interpretation, so send me. Joseph, O truthful one, explain to us seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones, and seven green ears and others dry, that I may return to the people that they may know.' He said, 'You will plant for seven years consecutively; what you harvest, leave in its ear, except a little from which you will eat. Then will come after that seven hard years which will consume what you saved for them, except a little from which you will store. Then will come after that a year in which the people will be granted relief and in which they will press [oil and wine].')
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage continues the author's epistemological argument by presenting examples of truthful dreams from non-prophets. The author first recounts the dream of Joseph's prison companions: the cupbearer dreamed of pressing wine, the baker of birds eating from his head. Joseph correctly interpreted these dreams, and their fulfillment confirmed the truth of the dream and the interpretation. The author then describes the dream of the king of Egypt (seven fat cows eaten by lean ones, seven green ears and dry ears). The court interpreters could not explain it, but Joseph, upon being summoned, provided not only the interpretation (seven years of plenty followed by seven of famine) but also a practical plan. The author uses these examples to show that truthful dreams are not limited to prophets but can occur to ordinary people. This supports the epistemological claim that dreams can be a source of certain knowledge (tasdiq) when interpreted correctly by those divinely taught, like Joseph.

Key Terms
رؤيا صادقة (ru'ya sadiqa): A truthful dream that corresponds to future reality, as experienced by non-prophets.
تعبير (ta'bir): Interpretation of a dream, synonymous with ta'wil in this context. [Note: In broader usage, ta'bir is the standard term for dream interpretation; ta'wil often refers to deeper interpretation.]
صاحب السجن (sahib al-sijn): Companion in prison, referring to the two men who were with Joseph.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the king of Egypt dream about according to the Quran?
A) Seven fat cows and seven lean cows
B) A ladder reaching heaven
C) A great flood
D) A burning bush

Key Takeaway
Truthful dreams can occur to non-prophets, as demonstrated by the Quranic stories of Joseph's prison companions and the king of Egypt, and their fulfillment confirms the truth of the dream and the interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another example of a truthful dream in the Quran (e.g., the dream of Ibrahim sacrificing his son) and analyze the dreamer, the dream content, the interpretation, and the fulfillment, noting whether the dreamer is a prophet or not.

The Epistemology of Dream Interpretation: Ta'bir as an Epistemic Tool
Author provides Quranic examples of truthful dreams experienced by non-prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a truthful dream (ru'ya sadiqa) and a jumbled dream (adghath ahlam), and explain the role of divinely taught interpretation in converting dreams into certain knowledge.

Core Idea
A truthful dream requires correct interpretation to yield knowledge; that interpretation is a gift from Allah.

The passage not only presents truthful dreams of non-prophets but also highlights the critical role of interpretation (ta'bir). The court interpreters dismiss the king's dream as 'jumbled dreams' (adghath ahlam), showing their inability to extract meaning. In contrast, Joseph interprets both his companions' and the king's dreams with precision and clarity, attributing his knowledge to divine teaching. This lesson examines the epistemological distinction between a truthful dream and mere confused dreams, and the necessity of a qualified interpreter to unlock the knowledge they contain.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between ru'ya sadiqa and adghath ahlam
The court interpreters call the king's dream 'adghath ahlam' (jumbled dreams), meaning they cannot make sense of it. A truthful dream (ru'ya sadiqa) is coherent, symbolic, and corresponds to reality when interpreted correctly.
The same dream could be considered truthful if properly interpreted; the interpreters' failure does not make the dream false.
2
Identify the source of Joseph's interpretive knowledge
Joseph explicitly says his knowledge comes from his Lord: 'That is from what my Lord has taught me.' His interpretation is not based on human guesswork but on divine instruction.
This underscores that true dream interpretation is a spiritual gift, not just a skill.
3
Compare the court interpreters' failure with Joseph's success
The court interpreters are knowledgeable in their field but cannot interpret the king's dream. Joseph, though in prison, provides a complete interpretation and a practical plan. The contrast shows that correct interpretation requires more than worldly knowledge.
The failure of the court interpreters highlights the limitations of human intellect without divine guidance.
4
Understand that ta'bir is an epistemic tool
Ta'bir (interpretation) is the process of extracting knowledge from a dream. A truthful dream, without proper interpretation, remains ambiguous. With correct ta'bir, it becomes a source of certain knowledge (tasdiq) about the future.
The fulfillment of the dream confirms both the truth of the dream and the correctness of the interpretation.
5
Apply the epistemological lesson
Recognize that in Islamic epistemology, dreams can be a legitimate source of knowledge, but their epistemic value depends on the dream's nature (truthful vs. jumbled) and the interpreter's qualification. This example shows that non-prophets can have truthful dreams, but the interpretation often requires a divinely guided figure.
This lesson is relevant for understanding the role of dreams in Islamic spirituality and knowledge theory.

جاء في القرأن الكريم ذكر رؤى صادقة رآها غير الانبياء من عامة الناس منها رؤيا صاحبي يوسف في السجن (وَدَخَلَ مَعَهُ السِّجْنَ فَتَيَان قَالَ أَحَدُهُمَا إِنِّي أرَانِي أعْصِرُ خَمْرًا وَقَالَ الْآخَرُ إِنِّي أَرَانِي أَحْمِلُ فَوْقَ رَأْسِي خَبْرًا تَأْكُلُ الطَّيرُ مِنْهُ نَبئنَا بِتَأْوِيلِهِ إِنا نَرَاكَ مِنَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ . قَالَ لَا يَأْتِيكُمَا طَعَامَ تُرزَقَانِهِ إِلا نبأتكما بتأويله قبلَ أَنْ يَأْتِيَكُمَا ذَلِكُما مِمَّا عَلَّمَنِي رَبِّي إِنِّي تَرَكْتُ مِلَّةَ قَوْمٍ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللهِ وَهُمْ بِالْآخِرَةِ هُمْ كَافِرُونَ ... يَا صَاحِبَي كَافِرُونَ ... يَا صَاحِبي السجن أما أحَدُكُمَا فَيَسْقِي رَبَّهُ خَمْرًا وأما الآخَرُ فَيُصْلَبُ فَتَأكُلُ الطَّيرُ مِنْ رَأْسِهِ قُضِيَ الْأَمْرُ الذي فيه تستفتيان ( ( تقول الرواية ان الفتيين هما ساقي الملك والآخر خباز الملك قد سجنهما الملك بعد ان اتهمهما في بعض الامور . فلما رأيا يوسف في السجن أعجبهما قوله وفعله وهديه فطلبا منه ان يفسر رؤياهما فأخبرهما بتاويل رؤياهما فكان الامر كما فسره يوسف عليه السلام، كما حدثت رؤيا صادقة لملك مصر في عهد نبي الله يوسف ﴿ وَقَالَ الْمَلِكُ إِنِّي أَرَى بَقَرَاتِ سِمَان يَأكلهن سبع عجاف وسبع سنبلات خُضْرٍ وَأَخَرَ يَابِسَاتِ يَا أَيُّهَا الْمَلَأُ أَفْتُونِي فِي رُؤيَايَ إِن كُنتُم لِلرؤيا تعبرُونَ . قَالُوا أَصْغَاثُ أَحْلَامِ وَمَا نَحْنُ بِتَأْوِيلِ الْأَحْلَام بِعَالِمِينَ ، وَقَالَ الَّذِي نَجَا مِنْهُمَا وَاذْكر بعدَ أُمَّةٍ أَنَا أُنَبِّئُكُم بِتَأْوِيلِه فَأَرْسِلُونَ . يُوسف أيُّهَا الصِّدِّيقَ أَفْتِنَا فِي سَبْع بَقَرَاتِ سِمَانِ يَأْكُلُهُنَّ سبع عجاف وسبع سنبلَاتِ خَضْرٍ وَآخَر يَابِسَاتِ لَعَلِّي أَرْجِعُ إِلَى النَّاسِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ . قَالَ تَزْرَعُونَ سبع سنين دأبًا فَمَا حَصَدتُمْ فَذَرُوهُ فِي سُنْبُلِهِ إِلَّا قَلِيلا مِمَّا تَأْكُلُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِن بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ سبع شِدَادٌ يَأْكُلْنَ مَا قَدَّمْتُمْ لَهُنَّ إِلَّا قَلِيلًا مِمَّا تُحْصِنُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِن بَعدِ ذَلِكَ عَامَ فِيهِ يُغَاثُ النَّاسُ وَفِيهِ يَعْصِرُونَ)
In the Noble Quran, mention is made of truthful dreams seen by non-prophets among the common people, among them the dream of Joseph's two companions in prison: (And two young men entered the prison with him. One of them said, 'I see myself pressing wine.' The other said, 'I see myself carrying bread on my head, from which birds are eating. Inform us of its interpretation; indeed, we see you among the doers of good.' He said, 'No food will come to you as your provision except that I will inform you of its interpretation before it comes. That is from what my Lord has taught me. Indeed, I have left the religion of a people who do not believe in Allah and are disbelievers in the Hereafter... O my two companions of prison, as for one of you, he will give drink to his master of wine; and as for the other, he will be crucified, and birds will eat from his head. The matter has been decreed concerning which you both inquire.') The narrative says the two young men were the king's cupbearer and the king's baker, imprisoned after the king accused them of some matters. When they saw Joseph in prison, his speech, actions, and guidance pleased them, so they asked him to interpret their dreams. He informed them of the interpretation of their dreams, and it happened as Joseph interpreted. Similarly, a truthful dream occurred to the king of Egypt during the time of the prophet Joseph: (And the king said, 'I see seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones, and seven green ears of grain and others dry. O eminent ones, explain to me my vision, if you can interpret visions.' They said, 'Jumbled dreams, and we are not knowledgeable of the interpretation of dreams.' And the one who survived of the two said, after an interval of time, 'I will inform you of its interpretation, so send me. Joseph, O truthful one, explain to us seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones, and seven green ears and others dry, that I may return to the people that they may know.' He said, 'You will plant for seven years consecutively; what you harvest, leave in its ear, except a little from which you will eat. Then will come after that seven hard years which will consume what you saved for them, except a little from which you will store. Then will come after that a year in which the people will be granted relief and in which they will press [oil and wine].')
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage continues the author's epistemological argument by presenting examples of truthful dreams from non-prophets. The author first recounts the dream of Joseph's prison companions: the cupbearer dreamed of pressing wine, the baker of birds eating from his head. Joseph correctly interpreted these dreams, and their fulfillment confirmed the truth of the dream and the interpretation. The author then describes the dream of the king of Egypt (seven fat cows eaten by lean ones, seven green ears and dry ears). The court interpreters could not explain it, but Joseph, upon being summoned, provided not only the interpretation (seven years of plenty followed by seven of famine) but also a practical plan. The author uses these examples to show that truthful dreams are not limited to prophets but can occur to ordinary people. This supports the epistemological claim that dreams can be a source of certain knowledge (tasdiq) when interpreted correctly by those divinely taught, like Joseph.

Key Terms
رؤيا صادقة (ru'ya sadiqa): A truthful dream that corresponds to future reality, as experienced by non-prophets.
تعبير (ta'bir): Interpretation of a dream, synonymous with ta'wil in this context. [Note: In broader usage, ta'bir is the standard term for dream interpretation; ta'wil often refers to deeper interpretation.]
صاحب السجن (sahib al-sijn): Companion in prison, referring to the two men who were with Joseph.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How did the court interpreters describe the king's dream?
A) A truthful dream
B) A divine revelation
C) Jumbled dreams (adghath ahlam)
D) A nightmare

Key Takeaway
A truthful dream, even from a non-prophet, can yield certain knowledge only when interpreted correctly by someone with divinely granted insight, as exemplified by Joseph.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the court interpreters' response in the passage (قالوا أضغاث أحلام). Explain why they were unable to interpret the king's dream, and contrast that with Joseph's approach. What does this tell us about the prerequisites for valid dream interpretation?

Distinguishing True and False Dreams
Author categorizes dreams into true and false, citing Quranic stories and hadiths of Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria for true and false dreams as outlined by the Quranic story of Yusuf and the statements of the Imams, and apply them to evaluate a dream's epistemological value.

Core Idea
Dreams are a mixture of truth and falsehood; discernment comes from knowing their sources.

Dreams have been a source of guidance and warning throughout Islamic history. This lesson draws from the Quranic narrative of Prophet Yusuf and the teachings of the Ahl al-Bayt to establish a framework for distinguishing true dreams from false ones. You will learn the epistemological criteria that prevent both over-reliance and dismissal of dreams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dream content
Read the reported dream carefully. Note its elements: people, actions, symbols. In the Yusuf story, the prisoners' dreams involved pressing wine and carrying bread; the king's dream involved cows and grain.
The passage shows dreams can be symbolic or literal.
2
Check correspondence with reality
Determine if the dream's interpretation matches actual events. Yusuf's interpretations came true exactly, indicating a true dream. If a dream contradicts known truths (e.g., encourages sin), it is false.
Imam al-Sadiq says true dreams sometimes guide to benefit or warn of harm.
3
Identify the source of the dream
According to Imam Ali, true dreams come from angels, false from jinn. Imam al-Baqir adds that the spirit's ascent to the lowest heaven yields truth, while visions in the air are confused. Imam al-Sadiq notes both originate from the same place, but false ones occur under rebellious authority.
The metaphysical source determines the dream's truth value.
4
Apply the principle of mixture
Recall Imam al-Sadiq's statement: Allah mixed true and false dreams so that if all were true, everyone would be prophets; if all false, no benefit. Therefore, evaluate each dream with balanced expectation—neither total trust nor total dismissal.
This prevents epistemic overconfidence or neglect.

إِنِّي أَرَانِي أَعْصِرُ خَمْرًا وَقَالَ الْآخَرُ إِنِّي أَرَانِي أَحْمِلُ فَوْقَ رَأْسِي خَبْزًا تَأْكُلُ الطَّيْرُ مِنْهُ نَبِّئْنَا بِتَأْوِيلِهِ إِنَّا نَرَاكَ مِنَ الْمُحْسِنِينَ . قَالَ لَا يَأْتِيكُمَا طَعَامٌ تُرْزَقَانِهِ إِلَّا نَبَّأْتُكُمَا بِتَأْوِيلِهِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يَأْتِيَكُمَا ذَلِكُمَا مِمَّا عَلَّمَنِي رَبِّي إِنِّي تَرَكْتُ مِلَّةَ قَوْمٍ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ بِاللَّهِ وَهُمْ بِالْآخِرَةِ هُمْ كَافِرُونَ ... يَا صَاحِبَيِ السِّجْنِ أَمَّا أَحَدُكُمَا فَيَسْقِي رَبَّهُ خَمْرًا وَأَمَّا الْآخَرُ فَيُصْلَبُ فَتَأْكُلُ الطَّيْرُ مِنْ رَأْسِهِ قُضِيَ الْأَمْرُ الَّذِي فِيهِ تَسْتَفْتِيَانِ . تَقُولُ الرِّوَايَةُ إِنَّ الْفَتَيَيْنِ هُمَا سَاقِي الْمَلِكِ وَالْآخَرُ خَبَّازُ الْمَلِكِ قَدْ سَجَنَهُمَا الْمَلِكُ بَعْدَ أَنِ اتَّهَمَهُمَا فِي بَعْضِ الْأُمُورِ. فَلَمَّا رَأَيَا يُوسُفَ فِي السِّجْنِ أَعْجَبَهُ قَوْلُهُ وَفِعْلُهُ وَهَدْيُهُ فَطَلَبَا مِنْهُ أَنْ يُفَسِّرَ رُؤْيَاهُمَا فَأَخْبَرَهُمَا بِتَأْوِيلِ رُؤْيَاهُمَا فَكَانَ الْأَمْرُ كَمَا فَسَّرَ يُوسُفُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ، كَمَا حَدَثَتْ رُؤْيَا صَادِقَةٌ لِمَلِكِ مِصْرَ فِي عَهْدِ نَبِيِّ اللَّهِ يُوسُفَ ﷺ (وَقَالَ الْمَلِكُ إِنِّي أَرَى سَبْعَ بَقَرَاتٍ سِمَانٍ يَأْكُلُهُنَّ سَبْعٌ عِجَافٌ وَسَبْعَ سُنْبُلَاتٍ خُضْرٍ وَأُخَرَ يَابِسَاتٍ يَا أَيُّهَا الْمَلَأُ أَفْتُونِي فِي رُؤْيَايَ إِنْ كُنْتُمْ لِلرُّؤْيَا تَعْبُرُونَ . قَالُوا أَضْغَاثُ أَحْلَامٍ وَمَا نَحْنُ بِتَأْوِيلِ الْأَحْلَامِ بِعَالِمِينَ . وَقَالَ الَّذِي نَجَا مِنْهُمَا وَادَّكَرَ بَعْدَ أُمَّةٍ أَنَا أُنَبِّئُكُمْ بِتَأْوِيلِهِ فَأَرْسِلُونِ . يُوسُفُ أَيُّهَا الصِّدِّيقُ أَفْتِنَا فِي سَبْعِ بَقَرَاتٍ سِمَانٍ يَأْكُلُهُنَّ سَبْعٌ عِجَافٌ وَسَبْعِ سُنْبُلَاتٍ خُضْرٍ وَأُخَرَ يَابِسَاتٍ لَعَلِّي أَرْجِعُ إِلَى النَّاسِ لَعَلَّهُمْ يَعْلَمُونَ . قَالَ تَزْرَعُونَ سَبْعَ سِنِينَ دَأَبًا فَمَا حَصَدْتُمْ فَذَرُوهُ فِي سُنْبُلِهِ إِلَّا قَلِيلًا مِمَّا تَأْكُلُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِنْ بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ سَبْعٌ شِدَادٌ يَأْكُلْنَ مَا قَدَّمْتُمْ لَهُنَّ إِلَّا قَلِيلًا مِمَّا تُحْصِنُونَ . ثُمَّ يَأْتِي مِنْ بَعْدِ ذَلِكَ عَامٌ فِيهِ يُغَاثُ النَّاسُ وَفِيهِ يَعْصِرُونَ) وَهَكَذَا كَانَتْ مَجَارِي الْأُمُورِ كَمَا فَسَّرَهَا يُوسُفُ الصِّدِّيقُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ وَفِي هَذَا الْمَعْنَى قَالَ النَّبِيُّ مُحَمَّدٌ (ص) (إِذَا كَانَ الْعَبْدُ فِي مَعْصِيَةِ اللَّهِ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ وَأَرَادَ اللَّهُ بِهِ خَيْرًا أَرَاهُ فِي مَنَامِهِ رُؤْيَا تَرُوعُهُ فَيَنْزَجِرُ بِهَا عَنْ تِلْكَ الْمَعْصِيَةِ) عَنِ الرِّضَا عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ قَالَ: كَانَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ (ص) إِذَا أَصْبَحَ قَالَ لِأَصْحَابِهِ: هَلْ مِنْ مُبَشِّرَاتٍ؟ يَعْنِي بِهِ الرُّؤْيَا. الرُّؤْيَا الصَّادِقَةُ وَالرُّؤْيَا الْكَاذِبَةُ: يَقُولُ الْإِمَامُ الصَّادِقُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ: الْأَحْلَامُ مَزَجَ اللَّهُ صَادِقَهَا بِكَاذِبِهَا. فَإِنَّهَا لَوْ كَانَتْ كُلُّهَا تَصْدُقُ لَكَانَ النَّاسُ كُلُّهُمْ أَنْبِيَاءَ، وَلَوْ كَانَتْ كُلُّهَا تَكْذِبُ لَمْ يَكُنْ فِيهَا مَنْفَعَةٌ فَصَارَتْ تَصْدُقُ أَحْيَانًا فَيَنْتَفِعُ بِهَا النَّاسُ فِي مَصْلَحَةٍ يَهْتَدِي لَهَا أَوْ مَضَرَّةٍ يَتَحَذَّرُ مِنْهَا. وَتَكْذِبُ كَثِيرًا لِئَلَّا يُعْتَمَدَ عَلَيْهَا كُلَّ الِاعْتِمَادِ. أَمَّا أَمِيرُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ عَلِيٌّ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَيَقُولُ: إِنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى خَلَقَ الرُّوحَ وَجَعَلَ لَهَا سُلْطَانًا فَسُلْطَنُهَا النَّفْسُ فَإِذَا نَامَ الْعَبْدُ خَرَجَ الرُّوحُ وَبَقِيَ سُلْطَنُهُ، فَيَمُرُّ بِهِ جِيلٌ مِنَ الْمَلَائِكَةِ وَجِيلٌ مِنَ الْجِنِّ فَمَهْمَا كَانَ مِنَ الرُّؤْيَا الصَّادِقَةِ فَمِنَ الْمَلَائِكَةِ وَمَهْمَا كَانَ مِنَ الرُّؤْيَا الْكَاذِبَةِ فَمِنَ الْجِنِّ. أَمَّا الْإِمَامُ الْبَاقِرُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَيَقُولُ: إِنَّ الْعِبَادَ إِذَا نَامُوا ، خَرَجَتْ أَرْوَاحُهُمْ إِلَى السَّمَاءِ الدُّنْيَا ، فَمَا رَأَتِ الرُّوحُ فِي السَّمَاءِ الدُّنْيَا فَهُوَ الْحَقُّ ، وَمَا رَأَتْ فِي الْهَوَاءِ فَهُوَ الْأَضْغَاثُ. وَيَسْتَدْرِكُ الْإِمَامُ جَعْفَرٌ الصَّادِقُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ فَيَقُولُ: الرُّؤْيَا الصَّادِقَةُ وَالْكَاذِبَةُ مَخْرَجُهُمَا مِنْ مَوْضِعٍ وَاحِدٍ ، أَمَّا الْكَاذِبَةُ الْمُخْتَلِفَةُ، فَإِنَّ الرَّجُلَ يَرَاهَا فِي أَوَّلِ لَيْلَةٍ فِي سُلْطَنِ الْمَرَدَةِ
I see myself pressing wine, and the other said: I see myself carrying bread above my head from which birds eat; inform us of its interpretation; indeed we see you among the doers of good. He said: There will not come to you any food you are provided with except I will inform you of its interpretation before it comes to you. That is from what my Lord has taught me. Indeed I have left the religion of a people who do not believe in Allah and they, in the Hereafter, are disbelievers... O two companions of the prison: as for one of you, he will give drink to his lord of wine; and as for the other, he will be crucified and birds will eat from his head. The matter is decreed about which you both inquire. The narration says that the two youths were the king's cupbearer and the king's baker; the king had imprisoned them after accusing them of some matters. When they saw Yusuf in prison, they were impressed by his speech, action, and guidance, so they asked him to interpret their dreams. He informed them of the interpretation of their dreams, and the matter turned out as Yusuf, peace be upon him, interpreted. Likewise, a true dream occurred for the king of Egypt during the time of Prophet Yusuf, peace be upon him: (And the king said: Indeed I see seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones, and seven green ears of grain and others dry. O council, give me your opinion about my vision if you are interpreters of visions. They said: [These are] confused dreams, and we are not knowledgeable in the interpretation of dreams. And the one who was saved from the two and remembered after a time said: I will inform you of its interpretation, so send me. Yusuf, O truthful one, give us an opinion concerning seven fat cows being eaten by seven lean ones and seven green ears of grain and others dry, that I may return to the people so that they may know. He said: You will sow seven years consecutively; and what you harvest, leave in its ears, except a little from which you will eat. Then after that will come seven hard years which will consume what you have stored for them, except a little which you will keep. Then after that will come a year in which the people will be given rain and in which they will press [grapes].) And thus the affairs proceeded as Yusuf the truthful, peace be upon him, interpreted. And in this meaning, the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, said: (When the servant is in disobedience to Allah Almighty and Allah wants good for him, He shows him in his sleep a dream that frightens him, so he refrains from that disobedience.) From al-Rida, peace be upon him, he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, would say to his companions in the morning: Are there any glad tidings? meaning dreams. True dream and false dream: Imam al-Sadiq, peace be upon him, says: Dreams—Allah has mixed their true ones with false ones. For if all of them were true, then all people would be prophets; and if all of them were false, there would be no benefit in them. So they become true sometimes, and people benefit from them in a benefit they are guided to or a harm they guard against. And they become false often, so that one does not rely on them completely. As for the Commander of the Faithful Ali, peace be upon him, he says: Indeed Allah Almighty created the spirit and made for it an authority; its authority is the soul. When the servant sleeps, the spirit goes out and its authority remains. Then a group of angels and a group of jinn pass by it. Whatever is from the true dream is from the angels, and whatever is from the false dream is from the jinn. As for Imam al-Baqir, peace be upon him, he says: When the servants sleep, their spirits go out to the lowest heaven. What the spirit sees in the lowest heaven is the truth, and what it sees in the air is confused dreams. And Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq, peace be upon him, adds, saying: The true and false dreams both come from one place. As for the false contradictory dream, a man sees it in the first night in the authority of the rebellious ones.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature of dreams, using the Quranic story of Prophet Yusuf and sayings of Shia Imams to distinguish between true and false dreams. The author begins by quoting the Quranic verses (12:36-41) where Yusuf interprets the dreams of two prisoners in Egypt. The passage then transitions to the king's dream (12:43-49) and Yusuf's accurate interpretation, demonstrating an example of a true dream that provided practical guidance for agriculture and famine. The author then cites a hadith of Prophet Muhammad that dreams can serve as a warning to deter from sin, indicating that dreams have a functional role in moral guidance. The core of the passage is the classification of dreams into true (sadiqa) and false (kadhiba). Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq explains that Allah mixed true and false dreams to prevent people from either becoming prophets if all were true, or losing benefit if all were false. They are true sometimes for benefit or warning, false often to prevent total reliance. Imam Ali provides a metaphysical explanation: during sleep, the spirit leaves the body while its authority (sultan) remains; angels produce true dreams, jinn produce false ones. Imam al-Baqir adds that spirits ascend to the lowest heaven where they see truth, while visions in the air are confused (adghath). Finally, Imam al-Sadiq notes that both true and false dreams originate from the same place, but false ones occur during the first night under the authority of rebellious forces. The passage does not present a formal logical argument but rather collects authoritative sources to establish an epistemological framework for evaluating dreams as a source of knowledge.

كاذبة

Criteria for Truthful and False Dreams
Imams define truthful and false dreams and their sources (angels vs jinn).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish truthful dreams from false dreams using the conditions and sources described by the Imams.

Core Idea
Dreams are a divine mixture of truth and falsehood — only certain conditions reveal their true nature.

In Islamic tradition, dreams are not all alike. The Imams provide a clear classification based on timing, source, and spiritual state. This lesson unpacks those criteria so you can evaluate dream reports with precision, as scholars have done when interpreting dreams in hadith and spiritual guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dream types
From the passage, extract the definitions of truthful dream (al-ru'ya al-sadiqa) and false dream (al-ru'ya al-kadhiba). Note the different terms used: truthful, false, and confused dreams (adghath).
Truthful dreams are from angels; false dreams are from jinn/demons.
2
Determine the timing condition
According to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq, truthful dreams occur after two-thirds of the night, before dawn, while false dreams occur in the first part of the night. Check the passage for these time frames.
The descending of angels is associated with the pre-dawn period.
3
Examine the spiritual condition
Even a well-timed dream may be false if the dreamer is in a state of janabah or without purification and not remembering God. This adds a ritual purity condition.
The passage states: 'unless he is in a state of janaba or sleeps without purification and does not remember God.'
4
Compare the metaphysical sources
Imam Ali explains that during sleep, the spirit leaves the body and passes by angels and jinn. Truthful dreams come from angels, false dreams from jinn. Imam al-Baqir adds that spirits ascend to the nearest heaven; what they see there is truth, what they see in the air is confused dreams.
The source determines the reliability of the dream content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرؤيا الصادقة والرؤيا الكاذبة
يقول الامام الصادق عليه السلام الاحلام مزج الله صادقها بكاذبها. فانها لو كانت كلها تصدق لكان الناس كلهم انبياء، ولو كانت كلها تكذب لم يكن فيها منفعة فصارت تصدق أحيانا فينتفع بها الناس في مصلحة يهتدي لها أو مضرة يتحذر منها. وتكذب كثيرا لئلا يعتمد عليها كل الاعتماد. اما امير المؤمنين علي عليه السلام فيقول إن الله تعالى خلق الروح وجعل لها سلطانا فسلطانها النفس فإذا نام العبد خرج الروح وبقي سلطانه، فيمر به جيل من الملائكة وجبل من الجن فمهما كان من الرؤيا الصادقة فمن الملائكة ومهما كان من الرؤيا الكاذبة فمن الجن. اما الامام الباقر عليه السلام فيقول إن العباد إذا ناموا، خرجت أرواحهم إلى السماء الدنيا، فما رأت الروح في السماء الدنيا فهو الحق، وما رأت في الهواء فهو الاضغاث. ويستدرك الامام جعفر الصادق عليه السلام فيقول الرؤيا الصادقة والكاذبة مخرجهما من موضع واحد، أما الكاذبة المختلفة، فان الرجل يراها في أول ليلة في سلطان المردة الفسقة وانما هي شئ يخيل إلى الرجل وهي كاذبة مخالفة لا خير فيها. وأما الصادقة إذا رآها بعد الثلثين من الليل، مع حلول الملائكة وذلك قبل السحر، فهي صادقة لا تخلف إن شاء الله، إلا ان يكون جنبا أو ينام على غير طهور ولم يذكر الله عزوجل.
The truthful dream and the false dream. Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) says: Dreams are a mixture by God of their truth with their falsehood. For if all of them were true, all people would be prophets, and if all of them were false, there would be no benefit in them. So they become true sometimes, and people benefit from them in a beneficial matter they are guided to or a harm they are warned against. And they are false often, so that complete reliance is not placed upon them. As for Amir al-Mu'minin Ali (peace be upon him), he says: Indeed God the Exalted created the spirit and made it have a dominion; its dominion is the soul. When the servant sleeps, the spirit goes out and its dominion remains. Then a generation of angels and a mountain of jinn pass by it. So whatever is from the truthful dream is from the angels, and whatever is from the false dream is from the jinn. As for Imam al-Baqir (peace be upon him), he says: When the servants sleep, their spirits go out to the nearest heaven. So what the spirit sees in the nearest heaven is the truth, and what it sees in the air is confused dreams (adghath). And Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) adds: The truthful and false dream both come from one place. As for the false dream that is inconsistent, a man sees it in the first part of the night under the dominion of the wicked demons, and it is only something that appears to the man, and it is false, contradictory, no good in it. As for the truthful dream, if he sees it after two-thirds of the night, with the descent of the angels and that is before dawn, then it is truthful, not contradicting, God willing, unless he is in a state of janaba or sleeps without purification and does not remember God the Mighty and Majestic.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of dreams from Islamic sources, quoting several Imams. It opens with the title 'The truthful dream and the false dream,' establishing the topic. Imam al-Sadiq first explains that dreams are a mixture of truth and falsehood by divine wisdom: if all were true, everyone would be prophets (impossible), and if all false, they would be useless. Thus, some are true for benefit and guidance, while many are false to prevent total reliance. Imam Ali then provides a metaphysical explanation: the spirit has a 'dominion' (soul) that remains when the spirit leaves during sleep. As the spirit passes, it encounters angels and jinn; truthful dreams come from angels, false from jinn. Imam al-Baqir adds that spirits ascend to the nearest heaven during sleep: what they see there is truth, while what they see in the air is confused dreams (adghath). Imam al-Sadiq further clarifies that both types originate from the same place but differ in timing and source: false dreams occur early in the night under demonic influence and are deceptive, whereas truthful dreams happen after two-thirds of the night, just before dawn, when angels descend. However, even these are conditional upon the dreamer being in a state of ritual purity and remembering God. This classification underscores the epistemological status of dreams: they are not all reliable, but certain conditions increase their truthfulness.

الرؤيا الصادقة
الرؤيا الكاذبة
الأضغاث

Key Takeaway

Distinguishing Prophetic Dreams from Common Dreams
Author explains that prophets' dreams are clear and truthful, unlike common dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemological distinction between prophetic/saintly dreams and common dreams, and explain the hadith evidence supporting the truthfulness of righteous dreams.

Core Idea
Prophetic dreams are clear, truthful, and authoritative, unlike the confused dreams of common people.

In this passage, the author argues that dreams of prophets, imams, and saints are qualitatively different from ordinary dreams. You will learn the specific characteristics that make these dreams reliable sources of knowledge and how they are rooted in prophetic traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dreams
Find where the author contrasts 'al-ru'ya al-manamiyya' for common people versus for prophets, imams, and saints. Notice the difference in degree of clarity.
The term 'al-ru'ya al-saliha' is used synonymously with truthful dream.
2
List the characteristics of each type
For common dreams: accompanied by confused dreams (adghath), ambiguity, forgetfulness, devilish interference. For elite dreams: completely clear, inform of past or future according to divine will, free from confusion.
The clarity depends on purity, knowledge, and spiritual rank.
3
Extract the hadith evidence
The author cites two hadiths: 'Nothing remains of prophethood except mubashshirat' and 'The truthful dream is one part of seventy parts of prophethood.' These establish righteous dreams as a vestige of prophethood.
The term 'mubashshirat' is interpreted as righteous dreams (al-ru'ya al-saliha).
4
Understand the epistemological claim
The passage asserts that for spiritual elites, dreams are a reliable source of knowledge (similar to revelation in clarity), whereas for common people they are ambiguous. This has implications for how we evaluate dream-based knowledge in Islam.
This distinction is important in Islamic spirituality and jurisprudence regarding the status of dreams.

ان الرؤيا المنامية عند الانبياء والائمة والاولياء تختلف في درجة الوضوح عن الرؤيا عند عامة الناس فالأخيرة تكون عادة مصحوبة بأضغاث كثيرة تشوش تفسيرها فتكون عرضة للابهام والنسيان ممزوجة بتدخل الشياطين في حين تكون الرؤيا عند الخاصة من الانبياء والائمة والاولياء واضحة تماما تخبرهم بما كان او يكون حسب الارادة الالهية ودرجة الطهارة والعلم والسمو الروحي فقد كان رسول الله محمد صلى الله عليه وآله كثير الرؤيا، ولا يرى رؤيا إلا جاءت مثل فلق الصبح وفي خبر خروج الحسين إلى كربلاء ورد ثم سار حتى نزل العذيب فقال فيها قائلة الظهيرة ثم انتبه من نومه باكيا فقال له ابنه ما يبكيك يا أبة فقال يا بني إنها ساعة لا تكذب الرؤيا فيها وإنه عرض لي في منامي عارض فقال تسرعون السير والمنايا تسير بكم إلى الجنة. أما رسول الله وفخر الكائنات النبي محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فيقول لم يبق من النبوة الا المبشرات قالوا وما المبشرات قال الرؤيا الصالحة. ان الرؤيا الصادقة جزء من سبعين جزء من النبوة. خياركم أولو النهى قيل يارسول
Indeed, the dream during sleep for prophets, imams, and saints differs in degree of clarity from the dream of common people. The latter is usually accompanied by many confused dreams that confuse its interpretation, so it is subject to ambiguity and forgetfulness, mixed with the interference of devils. Whereas the dream of the elite among prophets, imams, and saints is completely clear, informing them of what was or what will be according to the divine will and the degree of purity, knowledge, and spiritual elevation. Indeed, the Messenger of God Muhammad (peace be upon him and his family) had many dreams, and he never saw a dream except that it came like the breaking of dawn. And in the report of the departure of al-Husayn to Karbala, it is reported: Then he traveled until he camped at al-Udhaib, and he slept there at noon, then he woke from his sleep crying. His son said to him: What makes you cry, father? He said: O my son, this is an hour in which dreams are not false, and a vision appeared to me in my dream, and it said: You hasten the journey, and deaths hasten with you to Paradise. As for the Messenger of God, the pride of creation, Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him and his family), he says: Nothing remains of prophethood except good tidings (mubashshirat). They said: What are good tidings? He said: The righteous dream. Indeed, the truthful dream is one part of seventy parts of prophethood. Your best are those with wisdom (ulu al-nuha). Commentary
This passage shifts from general classification to the unique status of prophetic and saintly dreams. The author states that dreams of prophets, imams, and saints are qualitatively different from those of ordinary people: they are clear, free from confusion, and reliably inform of past or future events according to divine will. The clarity depends on the person's purity, knowledge, and spiritual rank. Two examples support this: the Prophet Muhammad (s) never had a dream that did not come true 'like the breaking of dawn,' and Imam al-Husayn (a) had a foreboding dream at al-Udhaib before Karbala, which he interpreted as a sign of impending martyrdom. The passage then cites a hadith of the Prophet: 'Nothing remains of prophethood except mubashshirat'—explained as 'righteous dreams' (al-ru'ya al-saliha). Another hadith states that the truthful dream is one part of seventy parts of prophethood. The text ends abruptly with 'خياركم أولو النهى' (Your best are those with wisdom), a reference to a hadith about the qualities of believers. This lesson underscores that for spiritual elites, dreams are a reliable source of knowledge, whereas for common people they are ambiguous.

Dream Classification: Imam al-Sadiq and Psychology
Author presents Imam al-Sadiq's threefold classification and psychologists' classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of dreams according to Imam al-Sadiq and identify the psychological classification of adghath ahlam.

Core Idea
Dreams are not all prophetic; most are merely reflections of daily life.

This passage presents the famous hadith of Imam al-Sadiq dividing dreams into three categories, and then expands on one type—adghath ahlam—using both psychology and Quranic support. Understanding this classification helps you avoid over-interpreting ordinary dreams as divine messages and provides a balanced view of dream interpretation in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of dreams from the hadith
Locate the three types in the Arabic: بشارة (good news), تخدير (intimidation), and أضغاث أحلام (confused dreams).
2
Define each type based on the hadith and the author's explanation
The hadith gives brief labels. The author later expands on 'adghath ahlam' as dreams arising from daily worries.
The other two types are not elaborated in this passage.
3
Connect the psychological classification to the Islamic one
Psychologists describe adghath ahlam as reflections of waking thoughts and life problems, aligning with the Islamic concept of confused, meaningless dreams.
4
Verify the Quranic reference
The author cites Surah Yusuf verse 44 where the king's dream is dismissed as 'adghath ahlam'.

لنذكر قول الامام جعفر بن محمد الصادق السلام (الرؤيا على ثلاثة وجوه. بشارة من الله للمؤمن وتخدير من الشيطان وأضغاث أحلام) أما علماء النفس فقد قسموا الرؤيا على اقسام هي أ - أضغاث أحلام : وهي الاحلام التي تنشأ من هموم الانسان وأفكاره التي يعايشها في يقظته فهي تراوده عند النوم في صورة الاحلام والمنامات وهي لا تدل على شيء لانها ليست سوى انعكاسات لافكار اليقظة ومشكلات الحياة . وقد أشار القرآن الكريم الى ذلك بقوله (قالوا أضغاث أحلام)
Let us mention the saying of Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq, peace be upon him: 'Dreams are of three types: good news from God for the believer, intimidation from Satan, and confused dreams (adghath ahlam).' As for psychologists, they have divided dreams into categories: A - Confused dreams: these are dreams that arise from a person's worries and thoughts that he experiences in his wakefulness, so they haunt him during sleep in the form of dreams and visions, and they indicate nothing because they are only reflections of waking thoughts and life problems. And the Holy Quran has pointed to that in His statement: 'They said, confused dreams (adghath ahlam).'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of dreams. It begins by quoting Imam al-Sadiq's hadith, which divides dreams into three types: divine good news (bishara), satanic intimidation (takhdir), and confused dreams (adghath ahlam). The first type is a true vision from God, the second is a deceptive dream from Satan, and the third comprises meaningless or jumbled dreams. The author then mentions the psychological classification, which focuses on one of these types—confused dreams—as arising from the dreamer's daily worries and thoughts, called 'adghath ahlam.' This is supported by a Quranic reference from Surah Yusuf, where the king's dream is dismissed as 'adghath ahlam.' The passage thus aligns the Islamic and psychological perspectives on this category of dreams.

Three Possibilities for the Meaning of 'Heart' in the Quran
Author examines three possible interpretations of 'heart' in Quranic epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the three possibilities for the meaning of 'heart' (قلب) in the Quran as presented by the author, and explain which one is accepted and why.

Core Idea
The Quranic 'heart' is a spiritual entity that uses the physical heart as its locus.

The Quran frequently mentions the heart as a center of cognition, emotion, and faith. But what exactly is this 'heart'? The author examines three possible interpretations, using Quranic evidence and rational argument to determine which is compatible with both revelation and empirical reality. This lesson will guide you through his analysis and help you understand the Quranic epistemology of the heart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
The author asks: is the heart mentioned in the Quran the physical organ, a purely spiritual entity, or something that combines both? Recognize this as the starting point.
2
List the three possibilities
The author presents three possibilities: (1) a spiritual heart unrelated to the physical; (2) the physical pineal heart as the center of thought; (3) a spiritual heart that uses the physical heart as its place of action.
Memorize the three options.
3
Analyze the evidence for each
For possibility 1, the author cites Quran 22:46 (hearts in breasts) to argue against a purely spiritual view; he rejects a metaphorical interpretation. For possibility 2, he uses the example of artificial heart transplants: thought continues, so the physical heart cannot be the seat of thinking. For possibility 3, he explains that the spiritual heart is the true subject of reward and punishment, and the physical heart symbolizes it.
Pay attention to how each possibility is supported or refuted.
4
Determine the accepted possibility
The author accepts the third possibility: the heart is a spiritual reality that acts through the physical heart. He concludes that the Quranic heart is a metonymy for the conscience and is beyond mere material observation.
5
Reflect on the epistemological implication
The accepted view implies that human cognition and emotion are rooted in a non-material essence, which has consequences for how we understand Quranic statements about understanding, faith, and moral responsibility.

نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة وتقليب اليد عبارة عن الندم ذكرا لحال ما يوجد عليه النادم قال (فَأَصْبَحَ يُقَلْبُ كفيه) أي يصفق ندامة قال الشاعر: كمغبون يعض على يديه تبين غبنه بعد البيع مع العقل والتقلب التصرف قال وتَقَلُّبَكَ في الساجدين)) يمثل القلب اكبر مستشاري النفس وأعظمهم شأنا عند اتخاذ القرارات الادراكية وفي كثير من الاحيان يتسيد القلب على العقل في كثير من القرارات العاطفية . فما المراد من القلب ؟ قال تعالى (أَفَلَمْ يَسِيرُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ فَتَكُونَ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌ يَعْقِلُونَ بِهَا) (وَلَقَدْ ذَرَأَنَا لِجَهَنَّمَ كَثِيرًا مِنَ الْجِنِّ وَالْإِنْسِ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌ لَا يَفْقَهُونَ بها) (إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْبٌ أَوْ أَلْقَى السَّمْعَ وَهُوَ شَهِيدٌ) (وَلَكِنَّ اللهَ حَبَّبَ إِلَيْكُمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَزَيَّنَهُ فِي قُلُوبِكُمْ وَكَرَّهَ إِلَيْكُمُ الْكُفْرَ وَالْفُسُوقَ وَالْعِصْيَانَ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الرَّاشِدُونَ) نستنتج من نصوص الآيات الكريمة المذكورة آنفا ان القلب في منظور القرآن الكريم هو موطن للشعور والتفكير والتفقه وبهذا المضمون جاء في دعاء النبي محمد ( ص ) ( اللهم يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك ) لقد طرح مفهوم القلب كأداة معرفية مهمة وجوهرية في نظرية القرآن ولكن سنعيد طرح السؤال ما هو القلب ؟ هل المراد منه الجسم الصنوبري المادي العضلي الذي يضخ الدم الى البدن ؟ أم يراد منه الاشارة الى قلب معنوي كرمز للتفكير المنظم العميق ؟
الاحتمال الاول : ان القلب المقصود في القرآن هو قلب معنوي لا علاقة له بالقلب المادي وهذا الاحتمال غير ممكن لقوله تعالى (فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ) فالآية الكريمة تشير الى القلب الذي في الصدر وقيل ان هذا قول مجازي ومجازه قوله تعالى (جنات تجري من تحتها الأنهار) والانهار لا تجري وانما تجري المياه التي فيها والحال ان هذا القول غير مقبول لان الجريان حاصل .
الاحتمال الثاني : ان يكون القلب المادي الصنوبري الواقع شمال الصدر هو ذاته مركز تفكير وشعور وهذا الاحتمال غير ممكن لان الانسان اذا استبدل قلبه المادي بقلب اصطناعي يواصل التفكير والعمل.
الاحتمال الثالث : ان القلب المقصود في الآيات الكريمة هو قلب معنوي يختص بالعواطف والمشاعر اتخذ من القلب المادي الصنوبري مكانا للعمل وهذا هو الاحتمال الممكن فهناك قلب معنوي وراء هذا القلب المادي الصنوبري وذلك القلب هو المخاطب في حقيقة الانسان وهو الاصيل الذي يترتب عليه الثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم والماديات انما تحكي المعنويات وان الظاهر هو عنوان الباطن فالقلب المادي الجسمي يحكي ذلك القلب المجرد في جوهريته فبينهما علاقة وعلقة وثيقة " فيكون القلب هنا كناية عن الضمير المرهف الحس فاذا فتح الاطباء العقل لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم وكذلك اذا فتحوا القلب لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم لان التجريد الفكري خارج اطار الماديات
Theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. And the turning of the hand is an expression of regret, mentioning the state of the one who regrets. He said: (So he began to turn his hands over) i.e., he claps in regret. The poet said: Like one cheated who bites his hands, his loss becoming clear after the sale. With the intellect, and turning about is acting. He said: 'And your turning about among those who prostrate.' The heart represents the greatest advisor of the soul and the most significant of them in status when making perceptual decisions, and often the heart dominates the intellect in many emotional decisions. So what is meant by the heart? Allah said: (Have they not traveled through the land so that they have hearts with which to reason?) (And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind, they have hearts with which they do not understand) (Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart or gives ear while present) (But Allah has endeared to you faith and beautified it in your hearts and has made hateful to you disbelief, defiance, and disobedience. Those are the rightly guided.) We conclude from the texts of the noble verses mentioned above that the heart in the perspective of the Holy Quran is a locus of feeling, thinking, and comprehension. In this content came the supplication of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'O Allah, O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' The concept of the heart has been presented as an important and essential cognitive tool in the theory of the Quran, but we will repeat the question: What is the heart? Is what is meant the physical pineal muscular body that pumps blood to the body? Or is what is meant an indication to a spiritual heart as a symbol of organized deep thinking?
The first possibility: that the heart intended in the Quran is a spiritual heart with no relation to the physical heart, and this possibility is impossible because of His saying: (For indeed it is not the eyes that are blind, but it is the hearts within the breasts that are blind.) So the noble verse indicates the heart that is in the chest. And it was said that this is a metaphorical saying, and its metaphor is His saying: (Gardens beneath which rivers flow) and rivers do not flow; rather the water in them flows. However, this saying is unacceptable because flow actually occurs.
The second possibility: that the physical pineal heart located on the left side of the chest is itself the center of thinking and feeling, and this possibility is impossible because if a person replaces his physical heart with an artificial heart, he continues thinking and working.
The third possibility: that the heart intended in the noble verses is a spiritual heart specializing in emotions and feelings that has taken the physical pineal heart as a place of action, and this is the possible possibility. For there is a spiritual heart behind this physical pineal heart, and that heart is the addressee in the reality of man, and it is the original upon which reward and punishment, praise and blame depend. Material things only convey spiritual things, and the outward is the title of the inward. So the physical material heart conveys that abstract heart in its essence, and between them is a close relationship and connection. So the heart here is a metonymy for the sensitive conscience. If doctors open the mind, they find only flesh and blood, and likewise if they open the heart, they find only flesh and blood, because intellectual abstraction is outside the framework of material things.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis of the concept of 'heart' (قلب) in the Quran. The author begins by citing various Quranic verses and a poetic example to establish that the heart is depicted as a cognitive and emotional center, often dominating the intellect. He then poses a question: is the heart the physical organ or a spiritual entity? The author examines three possibilities:

1. The heart is purely spiritual, unrelated to the physical heart. He rejects this because Quran 22:46 mentions 'hearts within the breasts', implying a location. Some claim this is metaphorical, but the author argues the metaphor is flawed (using the example of rivers flowing).

2. The physical pineal heart is the center of thought. He rejects this because if replaced with an artificial heart, thought continues.

3. The heart is a spiritual entity that uses the physical heart as its locus. This is accepted. He explains that the spiritual heart is the true self, subject to reward and punishment. The physical heart symbolizes the spiritual one. He concludes that the heart is a metonymy for the conscience; doctors find only flesh, but the true essence is beyond material.

Thus, the author argues for a dualistic interpretation: a spiritual heart behind the physical, which is the real organ of cognition and emotion.

العقل

Checkpoint
Which possibility does the author accept regarding the nature of the heart in the Quran?
A) The heart is a purely spiritual entity unrelated to the physical heart.
B) The physical pineal heart is the center of thinking and feeling.
C) The heart is a spiritual entity that uses the physical heart as its place of action.
D) The heart is merely a metaphor for the mind.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic heart is a spiritual reality that acts through the physical heart; it is the true seat of cognition, emotion, and moral accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Disjunctive Syllogism Behind the Quranic Heart
Disjunctive argument for the heart's nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism used to determine the nature of the heart in the Quran and check its logical validity.

Core Idea
The heart's nature is decided by eliminating impossibilities.

In this passage, the author uses a disjunctive syllogism to argue that the Quranic heart is a spiritual entity attached to the physical heart. He presents three possibilities, rejects two with clear reasoning, and affirms the third. This lesson breaks down the logical structure so you can see how the argument works and test its soundness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Disjuncts
Find the three possibilities the author lists: purely spiritual heart, physical pineal heart as center of thought, and spiritual heart with physical locus. These are the alternatives being considered.
The author calls them al-ihtimal al-awwal, al-thani, al-thalith.
2
Check Exhaustiveness
Determine whether the three possibilities cover all logical options. The author assumes they are exhaustive. Ask yourself: is there a fourth possibility? If not, the disjunction is complete.
In a valid disjunctive syllogism, the disjuncts must be mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
3
Verify Rejection of First Two
The author gives reasons to reject the first possibility (Quran mentions heart in the chest) and the second (artificial heart preserves thought). Check if these rejections are logically sound and based on evidence.
The first is rejected by direct Quranic text; the second by empirical observation.
4
Confirm Conclusion
Since the first two are eliminated, the third remains as the only viable option. Confirm that the conclusion follows necessarily: the heart is a spiritual entity with a physical locus.
This is a classic disjunctive syllogism form: P or Q or R; not P; not Q; therefore R.

الاحتمال الاول : ان القلب المقصود في القرآن هو قلب معنوي لاعلاقة له بالقلب المادي وهذا الاحتمال غير ممكن لقوله تعالى (فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ فالآية الكريمة تشير الى القلب الذي في الصدر وقيل ان هذا قول مجازي ومجازه قوله تعالى جنات تجري من تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ والانهار لا تجري وانما تجري المياه التي فيها والحال ان هذا القول غير مقبول لان الجريان حاصل .

الاحتمال الثالث : ان القلب المقصود في الآيات الكريمة هو قلب معنوي يختص بالعواطف والمشاعر اتخذ من القلب المادي الصنوبري مكانا للعمل وهذا هو الاحتمال الممكن فهناك قلـب معنوي وراء هذا القلب المادي الصنوبري وذلك القلب هو المخاطب في حقيقة الانسان وهو الاصيل الذي يترتب عليه الثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم والماديات انما تحكي المعنويات وان الظاهر هو عنوان الباطن فالقلب المادي الجسمي يحكي ذلك القلب المجرد في جوهريته فبينهما علاقة وعلقة وثيقة " فيكون القلب هنا كناية عن الضمير المرهف الحس فاذا فتح الاطباء العقل لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم وكذلك اذا فتحوا القلب لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم لان التجريد الفكري خارج اطار الماديات كذلك نلاحظ في سياق الآية المباركة هذا المعنى (ولكن اللهَ حَبَّبَ إِلَيْكُمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَزَيَّنَهُ في قُلُوبِكُمْ وَكَره إلَيْكُمُ الْكُفْرَ وَالْفُسُوقَ وَالْعصْيَانَ) ومما يؤيد هذا الاتجاه في التفسير هو سياق الحديث الشريف (انما سمي القلب من تقلبه ) أما قوله لا اله الا ان في الجسد مضغة إذا صلحت صلح الجسد كله وإذا " فسدت فسد الجسد كله الا وهي القلب " فلا شك في انه ( ص) كان يقصد القلب المادي. ولعل من أوضح التعريفات لمعنى القلب في القرآن الكريم هو قول الامام جعفر بن محمد الصادق (ع ) (موضع العقل الدماغ والقسوة والرقة في القلب وعلى هذا السياق من الفهم درجت قصائد الشعراء وهكذا كانت رسائل العشاق فاذا قررنا أو أقررنا بتفسير ان القلب مركز العواطف يكون الانسان الذي استبدل قلبه بآخر اصطناعي قادر على التفكير ولكنه خال من العواطف وهذا ما ثبت في دراسات علم النفس حيث تبين ان القلب الجديد لاتكون فيه أية عواطف ولا انفعالات فان القلب الاصطناعي إذا قربت اليه خطرا بدا وكأنه لاشيء يهدده في حين ان القلب الطبيعي يرتعش في الحالة نفسها واذا قربت من القلب الاصطناعي شيئا كان يحبه الشخص قبل استبدال قلبه بدا كأنك لم تقدم إليه شيئا لانه قلب بارد غير متفاعل مع سائر الجسد وقد اكتشف الاطباء وجود هرمونات غير عاقلة ترسل رسائل عاقلة الى الجسم كله وان القلب مركز عقل وتعقل وليس مجرد مضغة أو مضخة وفي هذا الاتجاه ذكر الدكتور على الخطيب رئيس تحرير مجلة الأزهر السابق أن طبيبا للقلب مشهوراً قد اعتزل مهنة جراحة القلب ، ذلك لأن الأشخاص الذين يستبدلون قلوبهم يصبحون بلا مشاعر.
The first possibility: that the heart intended in the Quran is a spiritual heart with no connection to the physical heart, and this possibility is not possible because of His saying (Indeed, it is not the eyes that are blind, but the hearts within the breasts become blind). The noble verse points to the heart that is in the chest, and it is said that this is a metaphorical statement, and its metaphor is His saying (Gardens under which rivers flow) and rivers do not flow, but the water in them flows. However, this statement is not acceptable because the flowing actually occurs. The second possibility: that the physical pineal heart located in the left chest is itself the center of thought and feeling, and this possibility is not possible because if a person replaces his physical heart with an artificial heart, he continues thinking and working. The third possibility: that the heart intended in the noble verses is a spiritual heart specialized in emotions and feelings that has taken the physical pineal heart as a place of operation, and this is the possible possibility. There is a spiritual heart behind this physical pineal heart, and that heart is the addressee in the reality of the human being, and it is the original upon which reward and punishment, praise and blame are based. Physical matters only express spiritual matters, and the apparent is the title of the hidden. So the physical bodily heart expresses that abstract heart in its essence; between them is a close relationship and connection. Thus, the heart here is a metonym for the sensitive conscience. When doctors open the mind, they find only flesh and blood, and likewise when they open the heart, they find only flesh and blood, because intellectual abstraction is outside the framework of physical matters. Similarly, we observe in the context of the blessed verse this meaning: (But God has endeared to you faith and made it beautiful in your hearts and made hateful to you disbelief, wickedness, and disobedience). Supporting this direction in interpretation is the context of the noble hadith: (The heart is only named from its turning). As for his saying: (Beware, in the body there is a piece of flesh: if it is sound, the whole body is sound; if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Indeed it is the heart), there is no doubt that he (peace be upon him) meant the physical heart. Perhaps one of the clearest definitions of the meaning of the heart in the noble Quran is the saying of Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): (The place of intellect is the brain, and hardness and softness are in the heart). And on this understanding flowed the poems of poets, and such were the letters of lovers. So if we decide or affirm the interpretation that the heart is the center of emotions, then a person who replaced his heart with an artificial one would be capable of thinking but devoid of emotions. This has been established in psychological studies, where it was shown that the new heart has no emotions or reactions. If danger is brought close to the artificial heart, it appears as if nothing threatens it, while the natural heart trembles in the same situation. If something that the person loved before replacing his heart is brought close to the artificial heart, it seems as if you brought nothing to it, because it is a cold heart, not interactive with the rest of the body. Doctors have discovered that there are non-rational hormones that send rational messages to the whole body, and that the heart is a center of intellect and reasoning, not merely a piece of flesh or a pump. In this direction, Dr. Ali al-Khatib, former editor-in-chief of al-Azhar magazine, mentioned that a famous heart surgeon retired from the profession of heart surgery, because people who replace their hearts become without emotions.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage critically examines three possible interpretations of the heart (qalb) in the Quran. The author presents each possibility, then refutes the first two and adopts the third. The first possibility, that the Quranic heart is purely spiritual with no relation to the physical heart, is rejected because the verse explicitly talks about hearts 'in the breasts' and the metaphorical reading is invalid. The second possibility, that the physical pineal heart is the center of thought and feeling, is rejected because artificial heart transplants show that thinking continues without the original organ. The third possibility, that the Quranic heart is a spiritual entity that uses the physical heart as its locus, is accepted. The author supports this with Quranic verses, hadith, the saying of Imam al-Sadiq, and modern psychological findings to argue that the spiritual heart is real and responsible for emotions and moral accountability, while the physical heart merely reflects its state. The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: there are three possibilities; the first two are false, therefore the third is true.

Key Takeaway
The author uses a valid disjunctive syllogism to eliminate two possibilities and affirm the third: the Quranic heart is a spiritual entity with a physical locus.

The Spiritual Heart: Evidence from Quran, Hadith, and Science
Evidence for the third interpretation of the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the types of evidence the author uses to support the third interpretation of the heart and assess their contribution to the argument.

Core Idea
The spiritual heart is known through scripture, tradition, and empirical observation.

After establishing the third possibility through logical elimination, the author marshals a range of evidence to support the interpretation that the heart is a spiritual entity. From Qur'anic verses to hadith, the saying of Imam al-Sadiq, and modern psychological findings, he builds a cumulative case. This lesson dissects how each piece of evidence functions.

Guided Path
1
List the Evidence Types
Identify the categories: direct Quranic verse (Q. 49:7), hadith (the heart named from its turning, the 'mudgha' hadith), saying of Imam al-Sadiq, and modern medical/psychological observations.
Note that the author also mentions poetry and love letters as cultural evidence.
2
Analyze Each Evidence's Role
For each, determine what it supports. The Quranic verse shows emotional transformation in hearts; the hadith on 'turning' indicates changeability; the mudgha hadith links physical heart to overall health; Imam al-Sadiq distinguishes brain (intellect) from heart (emotions); modern studies show artificial hearts lack emotional response.
The modern evidence is used to confirm that emotions are tied to the natural heart, not just the brain.
3
Assess the Weight of Evidence
Evaluate whether each piece is conclusive or suggestive. The Quran and hadith are primary; the Imam's saying is authoritative; modern science is supportive but secondary. The author uses them together to form a strong cumulative case.
In Islamic methodology, scriptural evidence is primary, but empirical evidence can corroborate.
