⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال الاول : ان القلب المقصود في القرآن هو قلب معنوي لاعلاقة له بالقلب المادي وهذا الاحتمال غير ممكن لقوله تعالى (فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ فالآية الكريمة تشير الى القلب الذي في الصدر وقيل ان هذا قول مجازي ومجازه قوله تعالى جنات تجري من تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ والانهار لا تجري وانما تجري المياه التي فيها والحال ان هذا القول غير مقبول لان الجريان حاصل .
الاحتمال الثاني : ان يكون القلب المادي الصنوبري الواقع شمال الصدر هو ذاته مركز تفكير وشعور وهذا الاحتمال غير ممكن لان الانسان اذا استبدل قلبه المادي بقلب اصطناعي يواصل التفكير والعمل.
الاحتمال الثالث : ان القلب المقصود في الآيات الكريمة هو قلب معنوي يختص بالعواطف والمشاعر اتخذ من القلب المادي الصنوبري مكانا للعمل وهذا هو الاحتمال الممكن فهناك قلـب معنوي وراء هذا القلب المادي الصنوبري وذلك القلب هو المخاطب في حقيقة الانسان وهو الاصيل الذي يترتب عليه الثواب والعقاب والمدح والذم والماديات انما تحكي المعنويات وان الظاهر هو عنوان الباطن فالقلب المادي الجسمي يحكي ذلك القلب المجرد في جوهريته فبينهما علاقة وعلقة وثيقة " فيكون القلب هنا كناية عن الضمير المرهف الحس فاذا فتح الاطباء العقل لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم وكذلك اذا فتحوا القلب لا يجدون الا اللحم والدم لان التجريد الفكري خارج اطار الماديات كذلك نلاحظ في سياق الآية المباركة هذا المعنى (ولكن اللهَ حَبَّبَ إِلَيْكُمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَزَيَّنَهُ في قُلُوبِكُمْ وَكَره إلَيْكُمُ الْكُفْرَ وَالْفُسُوقَ وَالْعصْيَانَ) ومما يؤيد هذا الاتجاه في التفسير هو سياق الحديث الشريف (انما سمي القلب من تقلبه ) أما قوله لا اله الا ان في الجسد مضغة إذا صلحت صلح الجسد كله وإذا " فسدت فسد الجسد كله الا وهي القلب " فلا شك في انه ( ص) كان يقصد القلب المادي. ولعل من أوضح التعريفات لمعنى القلب في القرآن الكريم هو قول الامام جعفر بن محمد الصادق (ع ) (موضع العقل الدماغ والقسوة والرقة في القلب وعلى هذا السياق من الفهم درجت قصائد الشعراء وهكذا كانت رسائل العشاق فاذا قررنا أو أقررنا بتفسير ان القلب مركز العواطف يكون الانسان الذي استبدل قلبه بآخر اصطناعي قادر على التفكير ولكنه خال من العواطف وهذا ما ثبت في دراسات علم النفس حيث تبين ان القلب الجديد لاتكون فيه أية عواطف ولا انفعالات فان القلب الاصطناعي إذا قربت اليه خطرا بدا وكأنه لاشيء يهدده في حين ان القلب الطبيعي يرتعش في الحالة نفسها واذا قربت من القلب الاصطناعي شيئا كان يحبه الشخص قبل استبدال قلبه بدا كأنك لم تقدم إليه شيئا لانه قلب بارد غير متفاعل مع سائر الجسد وقد اكتشف الاطباء وجود هرمونات غير عاقلة ترسل رسائل عاقلة الى الجسم كله وان القلب مركز عقل وتعقل وليس مجرد مضغة أو مضخة وفي هذا الاتجاه ذكر الدكتور على الخطيب رئيس تحرير مجلة الأزهر السابق أن طبيبا للقلب مشهوراً قد اعتزل مهنة جراحة القلب ، ذلك لأن الأشخاص الذين يستبدلون قلوبهم يصبحون بلا مشاعر.
The first possibility: that the heart intended in the Quran is a spiritual heart with no connection to the physical heart, and this possibility is not possible because of His saying (Indeed, it is not the eyes that are blind, but the hearts within the breasts become blind). The noble verse points to the heart that is in the chest, and it is said that this is a metaphorical statement, and its metaphor is His saying (Gardens under which rivers flow) and rivers do not flow, but the water in them flows. However, this statement is not acceptable because the flowing actually occurs. The second possibility: that the physical pineal heart located in the left chest is itself the center of thought and feeling, and this possibility is not possible because if a person replaces his physical heart with an artificial heart, he continues thinking and working. The third possibility: that the heart intended in the noble verses is a spiritual heart specialized in emotions and feelings that has taken the physical pineal heart as a place of operation, and this is the possible possibility. There is a spiritual heart behind this physical pineal heart, and that heart is the addressee in the reality of the human being, and it is the original upon which reward and punishment, praise and blame are based. Physical matters only express spiritual matters, and the apparent is the title of the hidden. So the physical bodily heart expresses that abstract heart in its essence; between them is a close relationship and connection. Thus, the heart here is a metonym for the sensitive conscience. When doctors open the mind, they find only flesh and blood, and likewise when they open the heart, they find only flesh and blood, because intellectual abstraction is outside the framework of physical matters. Similarly, we observe in the context of the blessed verse this meaning: (But God has endeared to you faith and made it beautiful in your hearts and made hateful to you disbelief, wickedness, and disobedience). Supporting this direction in interpretation is the context of the noble hadith: (The heart is only named from its turning). As for his saying: (Beware, in the body there is a piece of flesh: if it is sound, the whole body is sound; if it is corrupt, the whole body is corrupt. Indeed it is the heart), there is no doubt that he (peace be upon him) meant the physical heart. Perhaps one of the clearest definitions of the meaning of the heart in the noble Quran is the saying of Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): (The place of intellect is the brain, and hardness and softness are in the heart). And on this understanding flowed the poems of poets, and such were the letters of lovers. So if we decide or affirm the interpretation that the heart is the center of emotions, then a person who replaced his heart with an artificial one would be capable of thinking but devoid of emotions. This has been established in psychological studies, where it was shown that the new heart has no emotions or reactions. If danger is brought close to the artificial heart, it appears as if nothing threatens it, while the natural heart trembles in the same situation. If something that the person loved before replacing his heart is brought close to the artificial heart, it seems as if you brought nothing to it, because it is a cold heart, not interactive with the rest of the body. Doctors have discovered that there are non-rational hormones that send rational messages to the whole body, and that the heart is a center of intellect and reasoning, not merely a piece of flesh or a pump. In this direction, Dr. Ali al-Khatib, former editor-in-chief of al-Azhar magazine, mentioned that a famous heart surgeon retired from the profession of heart surgery, because people who replace their hearts become without emotions.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage critically examines three possible interpretations of the heart (qalb) in the Quran. The author presents each possibility, then refutes the first two and adopts the third. The first possibility, that the Quranic heart is purely spiritual with no relation to the physical heart, is rejected because the verse explicitly talks about hearts 'in the breasts' and the metaphorical reading is invalid. The second possibility, that the physical pineal heart is the center of thought and feeling, is rejected because artificial heart transplants show that thinking continues without the original organ. The third possibility, that the Quranic heart is a spiritual entity that uses the physical heart as its locus, is accepted. The author supports this with Quranic verses, hadith, the saying of Imam al-Sadiq, and modern psychological findings to argue that the spiritual heart is real and responsible for emotions and moral accountability, while the physical heart merely reflects its state. The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: there are three possibilities; the first two are false, therefore the third is true.

Key Terms
قلب
احتمال
مجاز

Checkpoint

A) That the brain is the seat of intellect and the heart is the seat of emotions.
B) That the heart is purely physical.
C) That the heart is purely spiritual.
D) That the physical heart is the center of thought.

Key Takeaway
The author supports the spiritual heart interpretation with a convergence of Quranic, prophetic, Imami, and empirical evidence, showing that the heart is a spiritual entity with a physical locus.

The Sealing of the Heart: Definition and Implications
Author defines the sealing of the heart and explains its effects with Quranic verses and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic metaphor of heart sealing and identify its spiritual and epistemological consequences.

Core Idea
The seal on the heart is not a metaphor—it is a real spiritual state with consequences for knowledge and accountability.

In this passage, the author unpacks the concept of tab' al-qalb (sealing of the heart) by examining its linguistic roots and Quranic usage. You will learn how persistent sin and rejection of truth lead to a spiritual 'seal' that affects perception and makes one comfortable with falsehood. The lesson also addresses the phenomenon of waswasa (whisperings) and its cure, linking heart states to ethical and epistemological outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic root
The author states that the source meaning of 'khatama' and 'taba'a' is the effect of a thing, like the engraving of a seal. Understand that this sets up the metaphor: the seal leaves an impression.
2
Trace the causal sequence
From the passage: persistence in falsehood or sin without turning to truth → God gives a disposition → person finds evil good and approves sins → as if heart is sealed. This is a key causal chain.
This is not arbitrary; it is a consequence of one's choices.
3
Catalog the Quranic metaphors
The author lists four metaphors: tab' (seal), ighfal (heedlessness), akinnah (coverings), qasawah (hardness). Each metaphor illustrates a different aspect of spiritual blindness.
These metaphors are used elsewhere in Quran to describe disbelievers.
4
Evaluate al-Jubba'i's interpretation
Al-Jubba'i says the seal is a sign for angels, indicating disbelief so they do not intercede. This adds a metaphysical dimension: the seal has an external reality.
This is a theological, not a logical, claim.
5
Apply the hadith on waswasa
The hadith explains that waswasa can 'tear the veil of the heart'. If it does, the person becomes accountable for speaking it. The cure is saying 'I believe in Allah and His Messenger' thrice. This shows that heart states can be remedied.
The veil is presumably the seal? Possibly the seal is not permanent for believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصدر ختمت وطبعت وهو تأثير الشيء كنقش الخاتم والطابع والثاني الاثر الحاصل عن النقش . ان الانسان اذا تناهى في اعتقاد باطل او ارتکاب محظور ولا يكون منه تلفت بوجه الى الحق يورثه الله هيئة تمرنه على استحسان المعاصي وكأنما يختم بذلك على قلبه (أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ طَبَعَ اللهُ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِم وَسَمْعِهِمْ وَأَبْصَارِهِمْ) وعلى هذا النحو استعارة الاغفال في قوله عز وجل (وَلَا تُطعْ مَنْ أغْفَلْنَا قَلْبَهُ واستعارة الكن في قوله تعالى (وَجَعَلْنَا عَلَى قُلُوبِهِم أكنةً أنْ يَفْقَهُوهُ) ۳ واستعارة القساوة في قوله تعالى (وَجَعَلْنَا قُلُوبَهُمْ قَاسِيَةً ) قال الجبائي؛ يجعل الله ختما على قلوب الكفار ليكون دلالة للملائكة على كفرهم فلا يدعون لهم ، وفي هذا المورد نذكر قول النبي محمد عليه الصلاة والسلام شر العمى عمى القلب ) ( لكل قلب وسواس فاذا فتق الوسواس حجاب القلب نطق به اللسان واخذ به العبد واذا لم يفتق القلب ولم ينطق به اللسان فلا حرج ۷ ومن ( وجد من هذا الوسواس فليقل آمنت بالله ورسوله ثلاثا فان ذلك يذهب عنه )
Source of 'khatama' and 'taba'a': the effect of a thing like the engraving of the seal and the stamp, and the second is the impression resulting from the engraving. Indeed, when a person persists in a false belief or commits a forbidden act and does not turn at all towards the truth, God gives him a disposition that trains him to find good deeds evil and to approve of sins, as if his heart is sealed. (Those are the ones upon whose hearts and hearing and sight Allah has set a seal.) And in this manner is the metaphor of neglect in His statement (And do not obey one whose heart We have made heedless) and the metaphor of cover in His statement (And We have placed over their hearts coverings lest they understand it) and the metaphor of hardness in His statement (And We made their hearts hard). Al-Jubba'i said: God places a seal on the hearts of disbelievers to be a sign for the angels of their disbelief so that they do not pray for them. In this regard, we mention the saying of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'The worst blindness is blindness of the heart.' (Every heart has a whisper (waswas); if the whisper tears the veil of the heart, the tongue speaks it and the servant is held accountable; but if the heart is not torn and the tongue does not speak it, there is no sin.) And whoever experiences this whisper, let him say 'I believe in Allah and His Messenger' three times, for that will make it go away.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of the 'seal of the heart' (tab' al-qalb) as presented in the Quran and interpreted by theologians. The author begins by clarifying the linguistic origin of the words 'khatama' and 'taba'a', stating that the source meaning is the effect of a thing, like the engraving of a seal (khatam) or stamp (tabi'), and the second meaning is the impression left by that engraving. This sets up the metaphor: when a person persists in falsehood and sin without any regard for truth, God creates in him a disposition that makes him comfortable with disobedience, as if his heart is sealed. The author then cites Quranic verses that use metaphors of sealing (tab'), heedlessness (ighfal), covering (akinnah), and hardness (qasawah) to describe the state of disbelievers' hearts. He quotes al-Jubba'i, who explains that the seal is a sign for angels, indicating that such people are disbelievers so the angels do not intercede for them. The author then brings a hadith stating that the worst blindness is blindness of the heart, emphasizing the centrality of the heart's perception. Another hadith explains the phenomenon of 'waswas' (whisperings) that tear the veil of the heart; if the veil is torn and the tongue utters the whisper, the servant is accountable, otherwise not. The cure for such whisperings is to say 'I believe in Allah and His Messenger' three times. This lesson emphasizes that the heart's condition is not merely metaphorical but has real spiritual and epistemological consequences.

Key Terms
طباعة (tabā‘a): Here it refers to the spiritual sealing of the heart by God as a consequence of persistent falsehood and sin. [Note: In classical mantiq, tab' is not a term; here it is used in Quranic exegesis context.]
وسواس (waswās): Whisperings from Satan that affect the heart; if they break through the heart's veil, they lead to sinful speech or action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of the heart being sealed?
A) God's arbitrary will
B) Persistence in false belief and prohibited acts without turning to truth
C) Lack of intellectual capacity
D) Following desires

Key Takeaway
The sealing of the heart is a spiritual state resulting from persistent sin, which leads to a disposition that makes one comfortable with falsehood, with real consequences for accountability to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the causal chain from the passage (persistent sin → divine disposition → approval of sin → seal), analyse the following scenario: A person repeatedly lies and justifies it. How would you explain the eventual 'sealing' of his heart? Identify the point at which the seal would become effective.
?
Read the Quranic verse 'وَجَعَلْنَا عَلَىٰ قُلُوبِهِمْ أَكِنَّةً أَن يَفْقَهُوهُ' (Al-An'am 6:25). Using the author's method of identifying metaphors, explain what 'akinnah' (coverings) implies about the heart's ability to understand. Compare it to the seal metaphor.

The Purified Heart as the Seat of First Principles
Epistemological argument for heart-based knowledge of first principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the role of the heart in knowing first principles from the role of the intellect in deductive reasoning, using Pascal's framework.

Core Idea
The heart knows first principles directly; reason deduces from them.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier discussion of the sealed heart to present the purified heart's capacity for knowledge. The heart, when cleansed, can perceive metaphysical truths that lie beyond sensory experience. The author supports this with a Quranic verse, an analogy from Imam al-Sadiq, and a philosophical argument from Pascal. Your task is to unpack how this epistemological framework distinguishes between intuitive and discursive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim about the heart
Find the sentence where the author states that a purified heart can attain truths beyond the senses. Note that this is presented as a 'Quranic theory'.
2
Locate the Quranic and hadith evidence
Read the verse 'And know that Allah intervenes between a person and his heart' and the Imams saying that the heart is to the body as the Imam is to the people. Ask: What do these citations add to the argument?
The verse indicates divine control over the heart; the analogy establishes the heart as leader.
3
Extract Pascal's epistemological distinction
Pascal says we know truth by both heart and reason. The heart senses first principles; reason deduces propositions. Underline the key phrases: 'principles are sensed by the heart' and 'propositions are deduced by reason'.
Pascal's point is that each faculty has its proper domain.
4
Analyze the complementarity and critique
Notice Pascal's critique: it is ridiculous for reason to demand proofs from the heart for first principles, and for the heart to demand emotional validation from reason for conclusions. Why is this important for avoiding category errors in knowledge?
This prevents us from expecting rational proofs for intuitive truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي اتجاه مغاير فان القلب اذا تطهر من الاوساخ المعنوية وسما الى الخصال الروحية السامية يستطيع كما تقول نظرية القرآن ان ينال حقائق ما فوق الحس ولعل اولى تباشير هذا التكريم الالهي الله يمنع المرء من ارتكاب المعاصي الكبرى ( وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّ اللهَ يَحُولُ بَينَ الْمَرْءِ وَقَلْبِهِ ) ( يقول الإمام الصادق ( عليه السلام) (إن منزلة القلب من الجسد بمنزلة الإمام من الناس ) ومن الجميل والضروري هنا ان نعرض قول الدكتور بسكال (١٦٢٣ (١٦٦٢م في رسالته للدكتوراه حيث يقول ( نحن نعرف الحقيقة ليس فقط بوساطة العقل بل وأيضا بوساطة القلب. فبالقلب تعرف المباديء الاولى . وعبثا يحاول التعقل وهو لانصيب له في ذلك الامر. فالعقل خاضع لارادة القلب. ان المباديء تستشعر بالقلب والقضايا تستنتج بالعقل . ومن المضحك ان يطالب العقل القلب ببراهين على مبادئه الاولى ومن المضحك ان يطلب القلب من العقل ان يشعر كل القضايا المبرهن عليها)
In a different direction, when the heart is purified from moral impurities and rises to lofty spiritual qualities, it is able — as the Quranic theory says — to attain truths beyond the senses. Perhaps the first signs of this divine honor are that God prevents a person from committing major sins. (And know that Allah intervenes between a person and his heart.) Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) says: 'The position of the heart in the body is like the position of the Imam among the people.' It is beautiful and necessary here to present the statement of Dr. Pascal (1623-1662 CE) in his doctoral dissertation where he says: 'We know the truth not only by means of the intellect but also by means of the heart. By the heart we know first principles. Reason tries in vain to have no share in this matter. The intellect is subject to the will of the heart. Principles are sensed by the heart, and propositions are deduced by reason. It is ridiculous for reason to demand proofs from the heart for its first principles, and it is ridiculous for the heart to demand from reason to feel all proven propositions.'
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents a complementary perspective on the heart's role: while the previous lesson discussed the sealed heart, here the author describes the purified heart. When the heart is cleansed from moral impurities and elevated to spiritual excellence, it gains the ability to perceive truths that lie beyond the sensory world—this is what the author calls the 'Quranic theory.' The preliminary sign of such divine honor is God's protection from major sins, citing the verse 'And know that Allah intervenes between a person and his heart.' The author then quotes Imam al-Sadiq, who compares the heart's relation to the body as that of an Imam to his people—emphasizing leadership and guidance. To reinforce this, the author presents Pascal's view that truth is known both by reason and by the heart, with the heart being the seat of first principles. Reason, though valuable, is subject to the heart's will. Pascal illustrates the complementary roles: the heart senses principles, reason deduces conclusions. The passage ends with a critique of demanding proof from the heart for its principles or emotional validation from reason for conclusions—each faculty has its proper domain.

Key Terms
قلب
مبادئ أولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It deduces conclusions from premises
B) It knows first principles directly
C) It stores memorized information
D) It controls the body's actions

Key Takeaway
First principles are known directly by the purified heart, while reason deduces conclusions from those principles.

Desires (Aḥwā') as Factors Affecting the Heart: Positive and Negative
Author classifies desires into positive and negative types with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between positive and negative desires (hawā) using Quranic and hadith evidence, and explain how desires cloud the heart like dust.

Core Idea
Desires can incline the heart towards good or evil; they are like dust that clouds spiritual vision.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of hawā (desire/inclination) as a key factor influencing the heart's decision-making. The author classifies desires into two types: positive, which align with truth and goodness, and negative, which lead to falsehood. We will examine the Quranic and hadith evidence provided to understand this distinction and its spiritual implications.

Guided Path
1
Define hawā
Identify the meaning of hawā as inclination and the metaphor of dust clouding the mirror of the heart.
The author explicitly likens hawā to dust that clogs the mirror of the heart, impairing spiritual perception.
2
Recognize the two types
Distinguish between positive desire (inclination towards good) and negative desire (inclination towards falsehood) using the given Quranic verses.
Positive: Q. Ibrahim 14:37 (tahwi). Negative: Q. Al-Furqan 25:43 (man ittakhadha ilahahu hawahu).
3
Analyze the hadith
Understand why it is called hawā—because it causes its possessor to fall (yahwi) into destruction.
The hadith is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that Iblis uses desires to mislead.
4
Connect to spiritual perception
Explain how desires, like dust, obstruct the heart's clarity and ability to perceive truth.
This implies that managing desires is essential for clear spiritual vision.

العوامل المؤثرة على قرار القلب (۱) الاهواء { جمع هوى أي الميلان الهوى كالغبار الذي يلبد مرآة القلب وقد يكون الهوى ايجابيا كما في قوله تعالى (فَاجْعَلْ أَفْئِدَةً مِنَ النَّاسِ تهوي إليهم وارزقهم من الثمرات لعلهم يشكرون وقد يكون الهوى سلبيا يتجه نحو الباطل كما في قوله تعالى (أَرَأَيْتَ مَن اتَّخَذَ إِلَهَهُ هَوَاهُ أَفَأَنْتَ تَكُونُ عَلَيْه وكيلا وهذا النوع من الهوى هو المقصود بالحديث الشريف (إنما سمي الهوى لانه يهوي بصاحبه ورد عن النبي الاكرم ( ص ) ( ان ابليس
Factors influencing the heart's decision: (1) Desires (ahwa'): plural of hawa, meaning inclination. Hawa is like dust that clogs the mirror of the heart. Desire can be positive, as in His statement (So make the hearts of some people incline toward them and provide them with fruits so that they may be grateful). And it can be negative, tending toward falsehood, as in His statement (Have you seen the one who has taken his desire as his god? Then would you be responsible for him?). This type of desire is what is meant by the noble hadith: 'It is called hawa because it causes its possessor to fall (yahwi).' It is narrated from the noble Prophet (peace be upon him) that Iblis...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on factors that affect the heart's decision-making. The first factor is 'al-ahwa'' (desires/inclinations). The author defines hawa as inclination, and describes it as dust that clogs the mirror of the heart—implying that desires cloud spiritual perception. Importantly, desires are not always negative; the author distinguishes between positive and negative desires, citing Quranic verses. Positive desire is illustrated by Prophet Ibrahim's prayer for the hearts of people to incline (tahwi) towards his family, which is a commendable inclination. Negative desire is exemplified by those who take their own desires as their god, following falsehood. The author then connects this negative desire to the hadith that it is called 'hawa' because it causes its possessor to fall (yahwi) into destruction. The mention of Iblis at the end suggests the role of Satan in exploiting desires. This lesson shows that desires are a key factor influencing the heart, and they can either lead to good or evil depending on their direction.

Key Terms
هوى (hawā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it settles on the ground
B) Because it clouds the mirror of the heart
C) Because it is easily blown away
D) Because it is heavy and weighs down the heart

Key Takeaway
Desires (ahwā') are a double-edged sword: they can incline the heart towards truth or falsehood, and they require careful management to maintain spiritual clarity.

The Heart's Epistemological Role
Author presents the heart's epistemological role via imam analogy and Pascal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the heart's role in knowing first principles and explain the complementary relationship between heart and reason.

Core Idea
The heart, not reason alone, perceives the first principles of knowledge.

In this passage, the author presents a striking analogy: the heart is to the body as the Imam is to the people. Combined with Pascal's insight, we learn that the heart is the source of first principles, while reason builds upon them. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding the absurdity of demanding proof for self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
Compare the heart's centrality to an Imam's guidance. What does this imply about the heart's authority?
2
Extract Pascal's claim
Identify the heart's role in knowing first principles. Note the asymmetry: heart senses principles, reason deduces conclusions.
3
Analyze the complementarity
How do heart and reason work together? Why is it absurd for each to demand the other's function?
4
Apply to Islamic epistemology
Consider how this view aligns with Quranic mentions of the heart (e.g., Q. 22:46) and the necessity of sound heart for correct knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقلبه يقول الإمام الصادق (عليه السلام): "إن منزلة القلب من الجسد بمنزلة الإمام من الناس". ومن الجميل والضروري هنا أن نعرض قول الدكتور بسكال (1623-1662م) في رسالته للدكتوراه حيث يقول: "نحن نعرف الحقيقة ليس فقط بوساطة العقل بل وأيضا بوساطة القلب. فبالقلب تعرف المباديء الأولى. وعبثا يحاول التعقل وهو لا نصيب له في ذلك الأمر. فالعقل خاضع لإرادة القلب. إن المباديء تستشعر بالقلب والقضايا تستنتج بالعقل. ومن المضحك أن يطالب العقل القلب ببراهين على مبادئه الأولى، ومن المضحك أن يطلب القلب من العقل أن يشعر كل القضايا المبرهن عليها". العوامل المؤثرة على قرار القلب: (1) الأهواء: جمع هوى أي الميلان. الهوى كالغبار الذي يلبد مرآة القلب. وقد يكون الهوى إيجابيا كما في قوله تعالى: "فَاجْعَلْ أَفْئِدَةً مِنَ النَّاسِ تَهْوِي إِلَيْهِمْ". وقد يكون الهوى سلبيا يتجه نحو الباطل كما في قوله تعالى: "أَرَأَيْتَ مَنِ اتَّخَذَ إِلَهَهُ هَوَاهُ". وهذا النوع من الهوى هو المقصود بالحديث الشريف: "إنما سمي الهوى لأنه يهوي بصاحبه". ورد عن النبي الأكرم (ص): "إن إبليس قال أهلكتهم بالذنوب فأهلكوني بالاستغفار، فلما رأيت ذلك أهلكتهم بالأهواء فهم يحسبون أنهم مهتدون فلا يستغفرون". وروي عن الإمام علي (عليه السلام): "الهوى أس الفتن"، "إنما بدء وقوع الفتن أهواء تتبع وأحكام تبتدع". إن الهوى له تجليات عديدة: فهو يتجلى في العشق تارة وفي الغضب والحقد تارة أخرى. قال الشاعر: "فلما جعلت القلب تحت رحى الهوى ... ندمت وصار القلب في موضع صعب". (2) الطمع: قد يسيطر أحيانا ويؤثر على قرارات القلب. في عقيدة القرآن الكريم: "كَلَّا بَلْ رَانَ عَلَى قُلُوبِهِمْ مَا كَانُوا يَكْسِبُونَ". يقول: غلب على قلوبهم وغمرها وأحاطت بها الذنوب فغطتها.
And his heart: Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) said: 'The station of the heart in the body is like the station of the Imam among the people.' It is good and necessary here to present the statement of Dr. Pascal (1623-1662 CE) in his doctoral thesis where he says: 'We know the truth not only by means of reason but also by means of the heart. Through the heart, the first principles are known. Reason vainly attempts and has no share in that matter. The intellect is subject to the will of the heart. The first principles are sensed by the heart and the propositions are deduced by reason. It is absurd for reason to demand proofs from the heart for its first principles, and it is absurd for the heart to demand that reason sense all the proven propositions.' The factors influencing the decision of the heart: (1) Desires (ahwa'): plural of hawa, meaning inclination. Desire is like dust that befouls the mirror of the heart. And desire may be positive, as in His (the Exalted) statement: 'So make hearts of the people incline toward them.' And desire may be negative, heading toward falsehood, as in His (the Exalted) statement: 'Have you seen he who has taken his desire as his god?' And this type of desire is what is meant by the noble hadith: 'It is only called hawa because it causes its companion to fall.' It is reported from the Noble Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Iblis said: I destroyed them by sins, so they destroyed me by seeking forgiveness. When I saw that, I destroyed them by desires, so they think they are guided and do not seek forgiveness.' And it is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him): 'Desire is the foundation of trials,' 'The beginning of the occurrence of trials is desires followed and rulings innovated.' Indeed, desire has many manifestations: it appears sometimes in love, sometimes in anger and hatred. The poet said: 'When I placed the heart under the millstone of desire, I regretted, and the heart became in a difficult place.' (2) Greed: It may sometimes dominate and affect the decisions of the heart. In the creed of the Noble Quran: 'No! Rather, what they used to earn has covered their hearts.' He says: It overwhelmed their hearts, immersed them, and surrounded them with sins, covering them.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the role of the heart in acquiring knowledge and the factors that influence its decisions. The author begins by quoting Imam al-Sadiq (a.s.), who compares the heart's position in the body to that of an Imam among people, indicating the heart's central and guiding role. Then the author presents a statement from Blaise Pascal, who argues that truth is known not only through reason but also through the heart, with the heart being the source of first principles. Reason, according to Pascal, is subordinate to the heart's will and cannot access these principles on its own. The author then shifts to a more practical epistemological concern: the factors that affect the heart's decisions. Two factors are enumerated: desires (ahwa') and greed (tama'). Desires are described as an inclination that can be either positive or negative. The negative desire is the one that leads to falsehood and is condemned in the Quran and hadith. The author illustrates this with Quranic verses and prophetic traditions, showing how desires can cause one to fall into sin and misguidance. The second factor, greed, is explained as something that can dominate the heart and cover it with sins, as indicated in the Quranic verse about the covering (ran) over hearts due to their earnings. The passage thus presents an integrated view: the heart is a vital source of knowledge, but it is susceptible to corrupting influences (desires and greed) that can obscure the truth.

Key Terms
القلب
المبادئ الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Pascal as quoted in the passage, what does the heart know?
A) Sensory perceptions
B) First principles
C) Deduced propositions
D) Moral values

Key Takeaway
The heart is the source of first principles, and reason depends on it; demanding proof for first principles is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the heart-reason complementarity, explain why the first principles of theology (e.g., existence of God) are known through the heart (fitra) and not solely through rational proof.
?
Identify a Quranic verse that speaks about the heart's knowledge and show how it supports Pascal's claim as quoted.

Corrupting Influences on the Heart
Author enumerates factors influencing the heart's decisions: desires and greed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how desires and greed affect the heart's ability to perceive truth and cite Quranic and prophetic evidence.

Core Idea
Desires and greed are dust and rust that cloud the heart's mirror, obscuring the truth.

After establishing the heart's role, the author explains how negative desires (ahwa') and greed (tama') can corrupt it. These factors, if unchecked, cause the heart to fall into misguidance. This lesson unpacks the Quranic concept of 'ran' (covering) and the prophetic warnings against following hawa.

Guided Path
1
Define hawa and its dual nature
Distinguish positive (good inclination) from negative (destructive desire). Cite Quran 14:37 and 25:43.
2
Examine the effect of negative hawa
How does the hadith describe hawa causing one to fall? What is the role of Iblis in exploiting desires?
3
Understand the impact of greed (tama')
Connect to Q. 83:14: sins cover the heart. How does this relate to the heart's knowledge?
4
Synthesize the overall picture
How do these factors lead to trials (fitan) and what is the remedy?

Rust on the Heart: How Wealth Dulls Spiritual Perception
Author explains how wealth accumulation causes spiritual rust on the heart, citing Imam Ali's admonition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the spiritual condition of rayn and its connection to material indulgence, and analyze how Imam Ali's admonition illustrates the epistemological danger of lawful pleasures.

Core Idea
Wealth accumulation rusts the heart, blocking empathy and spiritual insight.

This passage explores a profound epistemological insight: material abundance and indulgence create a barrier (rayn) on the heart, numbing it to the suffering of others and to spiritual realities. The author uses Imam Ali's letter to his governor as a concrete example, showing that even lawful pleasures can corrupt a leader's sensitivity. Let us dissect this argument and see how it forms a tool for self-critique in Islamic spirituality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central thesis
Find the sentence that states the link between rayn and earning. What is the causal direction according to the author?
The author says 'rayn is linked to abundance of earning' – establish the claim.
2
Examine the observational evidence
Look at how the author supports the claim: he observes that most rich people suffer from spiritual and cardiac numbness due to delicious food and continuous pleasures. Identify the mechanism described.
The author uses empirical observation (نلاحظ) to ground the claim.
3
Analyze the authoritative example (Imam Ali)
Read the excerpt from Imam Ali’s letter. What specific action does he criticize? What effect does he warn against? Note how he distinguishes between doubtful and certain lawful food.
The letter reprimands attending a banquet of the rich while the poor are ignored, showing that even lawful enjoyment can desensitize the heart.
4
Trace the logical chain from indulgence to rust
Why does the author claim that lawful pleasures still cause harm? Trace how continuous immersion creates a layer like ice on the heart, blocking compassion and spiritual perception.
The mechanism is habituation to pleasure, which freezes empathy (جمود احساس القلب).
5
Synthesize the lesson with hadith and poetry
How do the quoted hadith 'love of money weakens religion' and the poetry about wealth owning the person reinforce the central thesis? What is the overall epistemological warning?
The hadith and poetry show that attachment to wealth corrupts certainty (اليقين) and enslaves the soul, confirming the rayn effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول ان الرين مرتبط بكثرة الكسب فاننا نلاحظ ان اغلب الاغنياء يصابون بالخدر الروحي والقلبي بسبب كثرة الطعام اللذيذ ومواصلة الانغماس باللذات فتغطى قلوبهم بطبقة اشبه بالثلج تمنعهم من الاحساس بالفقراء ومعاناتهم . لاحظ ان الامام علي عليه السلام قد وبخ عامله على البصرة توبيخا شديدا عندما حضر وليمة دسمة لانه يعرف آثارها القلبية في صناعة طبقة من الرين على القلب فمن كتاب له عليه السلام الى عثمان بن حنيف الانصاري عامله على البصرة ، و قد بلغه أنه دعي الى وليمة قوم من أهلها ، فمضى اليها قوله : أما بعد ، يا بن حنيف ، فقد بلغني أن رجلا من فتية أهل البصرة دعاك إلى مأدبة فأسرعت إليها . تستطاب لك الألوان ، و تنقل إليك الجفان و ما ظننت أنك تجيب إلى طعام قوم ، عائلهم مجفو ( أي محتاجهم مطرود ) وغنيهم مدعو . فانظر إلى ما تقضمه من هذا المقضم ( قضم اكل بطرف أسنانه ) . فما اشتبه عليك علمه فالفظه ، و ما أيقنت بطيب وجوهه فنل منه . ألا وإن لكل مأموم إماما يقتدي به و يستضيء بنور علمه ، ألا و إن إمامكم قد اكتفى من دنياه بطمريه ) أي ثوبيه الباليين ) ، و من طعمه بقرصيه ) أي رغيفيه ( ( ( لاحظ ان الامام علي ع السلام امتنع عن الطعام الدسم اللذيذ بالرغم من كونه حلال لاشك فيه لانه عرف تاثيره في جمود احساس القلب بمعاناة الفقراء . وفي الوقت نفسه وبخ عامله على حضوره الى الوليمة الدسمة (وهي حلال لاشك فيه لانه يعرف خطر الرين على الناس عموما وعلى الحكام والقادة بشكل خاص. روي عن الامام علي السلام (حب المال يوهن الدين ويفسد اليقين) كما ورد في الحديث ( اياكم واستشعار الطمع فإنه يشوب القلب شدة الحرص ويختم على القلوب بطابع حب الدنيا ) قال أبو العتاهية: إذا المرء لم يعتق من المال نفسه ... تملكه المال الذي هو مالكه
We say that rust is linked to abundance of earning, for we observe that most rich people suffer from spiritual and cardiac numbness due to much delicious food and continuous immersion in pleasures, so their hearts are covered by a layer like ice that prevents them from feeling the poor and their suffering. Note that Imam Ali (peace be upon him) severely reprimanded his governor of Basra when he attended a sumptuous banquet because he knew its cardiac effects in creating a layer of rust on the heart. From his letter to Uthman ibn Hanif al-Ansari, his governor of Basra, when it reached him that he was invited to a feast of some people of the city and went to it: 'Now then, O Ibn Hanif, it has reached me that a young man of the people of Basra invited you to a feast and you hurried to it. Delicious varieties were prepared for you and dishes were brought to you. I did not think that you would accept the food of a people whose poor are repelled and the rich invited. So look at what you chew from this mouthful. What is doubtful to you, discard it; and what you are certain is lawful, eat of it. Beware, every follower has an Imam whom he follows and from the light of whose knowledge he seeks illumination. Indeed, your Imam has satisfied himself with two worn-out garments and two loaves of bread.' (Note that Imam Ali refrained from delicious lawful food because he knew its effect in freezing the heart's feeling for the suffering of the poor. At the same time he reprimanded his governor for attending the sumptuous banquet, which was undoubtedly lawful, because he knew the danger of rust on people in general and on rulers and leaders in particular.) It is narrated from Imam Ali: 'Love of money weakens religion and corrupts certainty.' And it is reported in the hadith: 'Avoid feeling greed, for it mixes in the heart intense avarice and seals the hearts with the stamp of love of the world.' Abu al-Atahiyya said: 'If a person does not free himself from wealth, the wealth which is his owner possesses him.'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of how material wealth and indulgence create a barrier (رين) on the heart, preventing it from perceiving truth and empathizing with others. The author begins by stating the link between abundance of earning and spiritual numbness. He then provides a practical example from Imam Ali's letter to his governor, showing that even lawful pleasures can dull the heart if one becomes accustomed to them. The letter itself advises caution about food and emphasizes the Imam's own asceticism. The author comments on this, highlighting Imam Ali's awareness of the effect. Then a hadith about love of money and greed is cited, followed by poetry that illustrates the enslavement to wealth. The entire lesson argues that attachment to material goods rusts the heart's capacity for knowledge and compassion.

Key Terms
الرين
الخدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary cause of spiritual numbness and rust on the heart?
A) Committing major sins
B) Excessive earning and indulgence in pleasures
C) Lack of prayer
D) Keeping company with the wealthy

Key Takeaway
Spiritual rust (rayn) is a real condition caused by excessive lawful indulgence, blocking empathy and certainty; it must be counteracted by conscious simplicity and awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Tarhib and Targhib: Quranic Framework for Heart Decisions
Author discusses fear of punishment and hope of reward as factors influencing heart decisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the concepts of tarhib and targhib in Quranic passages and explain how they function as epistemological factors influencing moral decisions.

Core Idea
Fear and hope are Quranic tools that shape the heart's judgments.

The heart does not operate in a vacuum. The Quran presents two powerful emotional levers—fear of punishment (tarhib) and desire for reward (targhib)—that influence the heart's decisions. This lesson examines how these factors are grounded in revealed knowledge, including the recording of deeds by angels, and how they are used by the heart to guide the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factors
Extract the definitions of tarhib and targhib from the passage. Tarhib is associated with punishment (fire), targhib with reward (paradise). Note that these are not mere emotions but Quranically grounded motivations.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the three verses: (1) angels recording deeds, (2) punishment for disbelievers, (3) reward for believers. How does each verse contribute to tarhib or targhib?
The recording angels verse provides an epistemological basis: awareness of divine surveillance influences the heart.
3
Understand the mechanism
According to the passage, the knowledge that angels record all deeds and words influences the heart's decision, which then affects the soul. This is a cognitive-affective process: belief in the unseen motivates action.
4
Connect to the hadith
The hadith states hearts are created to love those who do good and hate those who do evil. This internal disposition works alongside external factors (tarhib/targhib) to shape decisions.
Together, external revelation and innate disposition form a complete moral psychology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الترهيب والترغيب : من العوامل المؤثرة في قرارات القلب الترهيب بالنار والعذاب والترغيب بالجنة وملذاتها وفي نظرية القرآن ان لكل انسان رقباء من الملائكة يسجلون اعماله واقواله الحسنة والسيئة في الليل والنهار وهذا ما يؤثر في طبيعة القرار الذي يتخذه القلب ويرفعه الى النفس قال تعالى (وَإِنْ عَلَيْكُمْ لَحَافِظِينَ كِرَامًا كَاتِبِينَ . يَعْلَمُونَ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ ) وفي مضمار الترهيب قال تعالى (وَالَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا وَكَذَّبُوا بِآيَاتِنَا أُولَئِكَ أَصْحَابُ النَّارِ هُم فِيهَا خَالِدُونَ ( ° وفي الترغيب قال تعالى (وَعَدَ اللهُ الْمُؤمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤمِنَاتِ جَنَّاتِ تَجْرِي مِنْ تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ خَالِدِينَ فِيهَا وَمَسَاكِنَ طَيِّبَةً فِي جَنَّاتِ عَدْنٍ وَرِضْوَان من الله أكبرُ ذَلِكَ هُوَ الْفَوْزُ الْعَظِيمُ ( * قال ابو تمام وكأنه يصف القلب: يا حساماً مهنداً وغماماً ... ديمتاه الترغيب والترهيب) حركات القلب وسكناته : روي عن النبي محمد (ص ) انه قال ( جبلت القلوب على حب من أحسن اليها وبغض من اساء اليها )
Intimidation and desire: Among the factors influencing the heart's decisions are intimidation by fire and punishment and desire for paradise and its pleasures. In the Quranic theory, every human has guardians from angels who record his good and evil deeds and words night and day, and this influences the nature of the decision that the heart takes and raises to the soul. Allah says: 'Indeed, over you are guardians, noble scribes, knowing what you do.' And in the realm of intimidation, He says: 'As for those who disbelieve and deny Our signs, they are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein forever.' And in desire, He says: 'Allah has promised the believing men and believing women gardens beneath which rivers flow, wherein they abide eternally, and pleasant dwellings in gardens of perpetual residence; but satisfaction from Allah is greater. That is the great attainment.' Abu Tammam said, as if describing the heart: 'O sharp sword and raincloud, whose two rains are intimidation and desire.' (Movements and stillnesses of the heart: It is narrated from the Prophet Muhammad that he said: 'Hearts are created to love those who do good to them and to hate those who do evil to them.')
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage classifies the factors that influence the heart's decisions into two categories: intimidation (al-tarhib) and desire (al-targhib). The author first states that these are major factors, then explains the Quranic basis that angels record all deeds, which affects decisions. He cites three Quranic verses: one about guardian angels, one threatening disbelievers with eternal fire, and one promising believers paradise and divine pleasure. He then quotes a couplet from Abu Tammam describing the heart as having two 'rains' – intimidation and desire. Finally, he reports a hadith that hearts are naturally inclined to love those who do good and hate those who do evil. The lesson demonstrates that the heart's epistemological and moral decisions are shaped by the interplay of fear and hope, which are grounded in revealed texts.

الترغيب

Key Takeaway
The Quranic framework of tarhib and targhib, supported by angelic recording, provides an epistemological basis for heart decisions that integrates emotion with divine revelation.

The Heart's Inclination and Disinclination: An Epistemological Insight
Cites hadiths about the heart's alternating states of attraction and aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the heart's states of inclination (iqbal) and disinclination (idbar) and explain how each state affects spiritual perception and understanding.

Core Idea
The heart's state determines its capacity for spiritual knowledge and action.

The heart is not merely a physical pump; in Islamic spirituality, it is the seat of understanding and the locus of divine knowledge. The Imams (peace be upon them) describe the heart as having alternating states of attraction and aversion, directly influencing how we perceive and act. This lesson unpacks the epistemological dimension of these states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms 'اقبال' and 'ادبار' in the Arabic. What do they mean in this context?
Note that these terms denote spiritual inclination and disinclination, not physical movement.
2
Extract the epistemological claim
According to the hadiths, what happens to the heart when it is inclined? What about when disinclined?
Imam al-Rida explicitly says 'بصرت وفهمت' (it perceives and understands) when inclined, and 'كلت و ملت' (weary and bored) when disinclined.
3
Compare the different imams' descriptions
How does Imam Ali's wording differ from Imam al-Rida's? Do they agree on the core idea?
All Imams agree on the alternating states; they use different metaphors (boredom, life/death, activity/languor).
4
Summarize the principle
Write a one-sentence summary of how heart state relates to perception.
Example: The heart's state directly impacts its ability to perceive and understand spiritual truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اقبال القلوب وادبارها : روي عن الامام علي (ع ) قوله (ان للقلوب شهوة واقبالا و ادبارا فآتوها من قبل شهوتها واقبالها فان القلب اذا اكره عمي ان للقلوب اقبالا و ادبارا فاذا اقبلت فاحملوها على النوافل واذا ادبرت فاقتصروا بها على الفرائض ) ان هذه القلوب تمل كما تمل الابدان فابتغوا لها طرائف الحكم وعن الامام الصادق عليه السلام ان القلب يحيا ويموت فاذا حيا فأدبه بالتطوع واذا مات فاقصره على الفرائض وعن الامام علي بن موسى الرضا عليه السلام ) ان للقلوب اقبالا و ادبارا ونشاطا وفتورا فاذا اقبلت بصرت وفهمت واذا ادبرت كلت و ملت فخذوها عند اقبالها ونشاطها واتركوها عند ادبارها وفتورها وعن الامام الحسن العسكري عليه السلام ( اذا نشطت القلوب فأودعوها . واذا نفرت فودعوها)
The inclination and disinclination of hearts: It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him) his saying: 'Indeed hearts have desire, inclination, and disinclination; so approach them from their desire and inclination, for if the heart is forced, it goes blind. Indeed hearts have inclination and disinclination; so if they are inclined, burden them with supererogatory acts, and if they are disinclined, restrict them to obligatory acts.' Indeed these hearts get bored as bodies get bored, so seek for them the delightful subtleties of wisdom. And from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): 'The heart lives and dies; so if it lives, discipline it with voluntary acts, and if it dies, restrict it to obligatory acts.' And from Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (peace be upon him): 'Indeed hearts have inclination, disinclination, activity, and languor; so if they are inclined, they perceive and understand, and if they are disinclined, they become weary and bored. So take them at their inclination and activity, and leave them at their disinclination and languor.' And from Imam al-Hasan al-Askari (peace be upon him): 'When hearts are active, entrust them; and when they are averse, leave them.'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of hadiths from the Ahl al-Bayt (peace be upon them) regarding the states of the heart (qalb) and the appropriate response to these states. The heart is portrayed as having spiritual inclinations (iqbal) and disinclinations (idbar), as well as desire (shahwa) and boredom (malal). The first hadith from Imam Ali advises that one should approach the heart from the side of its desire and inclination, avoiding force, because compulsion leads to blindness (عمى) of the heart. When the heart is inclined, it is capable of undertaking supererogatory acts (nawafil); when disinclined, it should be confined to obligatory acts (fara'id). The commentary then notes that hearts tire like bodies, so one should seek 'subtleties of wisdom' (tara'if al-hikam) to refresh them. Imam al-Sadiq adds the metaphor of life and death: when the heart is alive, it benefits from voluntary discipline; when dead (i.e., spiritually dull), it should be limited to obligations. Imam al-Rida elaborates on the states: inclination brings vision and understanding, disinclination brings weariness and boredom; action should follow the heart's state. Finally, Imam al-Askari advises to entrust hearts when they are active and leave them when they are averse. The lesson underscores the spiritual psychology of the heart as an epistemological organ—its state directly affects perception and understanding.

Key Terms
قلب
اقبال
ادبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Restrict it to obligatory acts
B) Burden it with supererogatory acts
C) Leave it alone completely
D) Force it to do more worship

Key Takeaway
The heart's inclination and disinclination are natural spiritual rhythms; wisdom lies in aligning actions with these states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the hadith from Imam Ali. Identify the two states (iqbal and idbar) and state what action is recommended for each.
?
Explain in your own words why the heart 'goes blind' when forced, using the commentary insight.

Practical Guidance: When to Increase and When to Reduce Worship
Cites hadiths about the heart's alternating states of attraction and aversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of restricting to obligatory acts when the heart is disinclined and engaging in supererogatory acts when inclined, based on the hadiths.

Core Idea
When the heart is active, increase voluntary worship; when languid, stick to obligations.

The hadiths from the Imams provide concrete guidance on how to manage worship according to the heart's condition. This lesson focuses on the practical application of these teachings in daily spiritual practice.

Guided Path
1
List practical recommendations from each hadith
What does Imam Ali say about the inclined and disinclined states? What about Imam al-Sadiq's life/death metaphor?
Write down the specific actions recommended for each condition.
2
Identify the condition and the response
For each recommendation, note the condition (e.g., heart inclined) and the recommended action (e.g., burden with nawafil).
Create a simple table: Condition | Action
3
Formulate a general rule
Combine all recommendations into one concise rule.
Example: When the heart is spiritually active/inclined, increase voluntary worship; when it is disinclined/languid, restrict to obligatory acts.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Imagine a day when you feel spiritually low; what should you do according to this rule?
Consider both the state of the heart and the recommended action.

Heart States and Pedagogical Timing
Hadith compilation on heart's states and their pedagogical implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the heart's states of inclination (iqbal) and aversion (idbar) and apply the principle of tailoring acts of worship and knowledge to the heart's receptivity.

Core Idea
Teach the heart when it is inclined; leave it when averse, or it goes blind.

This lesson draws on a series of hadith from the Ahl al-Bayt (a) that describe the heart as having fluctuating states. Understanding these states is key to effective spiritual and intellectual cultivation. The author compiles these narrations to show that knowledge and worship must be timed to the heart's readiness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the states mentioned
From the hadith of Imam Ali (a), note the pairs: iqbal/idbar, nashaat/futoor, hayat/mawt. Write down what each state entails.
2
Extract the pedagogical principle
All narrations advise: when the heart is active/inclined (iqbal, nashaat, hayat), engage it with supererogatory acts and knowledge; when it is averse/languid, restrict to obligatory acts only.
This principle is derived from statements like 'When they are inclined, burden them with supererogatory acts; when they are averse, restrict them to obligatory acts.'
3
Apply to the learning process
Consider how this principle applies to studying Islamic sciences. For example, when you feel enthusiasm, study deeper topics; when you feel bored, stick to essentials.
4
Recognize the psychological insight
The heart is not constant; forcing it when averse leads to blindness (عمي). This shows the importance of self-awareness in the pursuit of knowledge.
Imam Ali (a) said: 'If the heart is forced, it becomes blind.'

اقبال القلوب وادبارها : روي عن الامام علي (ع ) قوله (ان للقلوب شهوة واقبالا و ادبارا فآتوها من قبل شهوتها واقبالها فان القلب اذا اكره عمي ان للقلوب اقبالا و ادبارا فاذا اقبلت فاحملوها على النوافل واذا ادبرت فاقتصروا بها على الفرائض ) ان هذه القلوب تمل كما تمل الابدان فابتغوا لها طرائف الحكم وعن الامام الصادق عليه السلام ان القلب يحيا ويموت فاذا حيا فأدبه بالتطوع واذا مات فاقصره على الفرائض وعن الامام علي بن موسى الرضا عليه السلام ) ان للقلوب اقبالا و ادبارا ونشاطا وفتورا فاذا اقبلت بصرت وفهمت واذا ادبرت كلت و ملت فخذوها عند اقبالها ونشاطها واتركوها عند ادبارها وفتورها وعن الامام الحسن العسكري عليه السلام ( اذا نشطت القلوب فأودعوها . واذا نفرت فودعوها) ما يجلي القلب وينوره ؛ قال النبي الاكرم محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ) من زهد في الدنيا اثبت الله الحكمة في قلبه ( وانطق بها لسانه ) ان هذه القلوب تصدأ كما يصدأ الحديد إذا أصابه الماء . قيل وما جلاؤها ؟ قال كثرة ذكر الموت وتلاوة (القرآن) (ان للقلوب صداء كصداء النحاس فاجلوها بالاستغفار ) ( وعن الامام علي (ع ) (أحي قلبك بالموعظة ... ... ونوره بالحكمة (أحي قلبك بالموعظة وامته بالزهادة وقوه باليقين) " وعن الامام موسى بن جعفر عليه السلام) ان الزرع ينبت في السهل ولا ينبت في الصفا فكذلك الحكمة تعمر قلب المتواضع ولاتعمر قلب المتكبر الجبار لان الله تعالى جعل التواضع آلة العقل) ٤ ما يقسي القلب قال الرسول محمد (ص ) ( ثلاث يقسين القلب : استماع اللهو وطلب الصيد واتيان باب السلطان ( إياكم واستماع المعازف والغناء فانهما ينبتان النفاق في القلب كما ينبت الماء البقل) وعن أمير المؤمنين علي عليه السلام قال ( ما جفت الدموع الا لقسوة القلب وما قست القلوب الا لكثرة الذنوب ) ( من يأمل ان يعيش غدا فانه يأمل ان يعيش أبدا ومن يأمل ان يعيش أبدا يقسو (قلبه وقد لاحظنا ذلك الخلط الواضح بين مفاهيم الصدور والقلوب والنفوس عند جموع المفسرين وفي حقيقة الامر ان لكل مصطلح منها مقصد مختلف وغاية خاصة. والخلاصة : ان القلب في نظرية القرآن المعرفية أحد عظماء المستشارين للنفس وهو مختص بالامور العاطفية.
The inclination and aversion of hearts: It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him) his saying: 'Hearts have desire, inclination, and aversion. So approach them from [the direction of] their desire and inclination, for if the heart is forced, it becomes blind. Hearts have inclination and aversion. So when they are inclined, burden them with supererogatory acts; and when they are averse, restrict them to obligatory acts.' These hearts become weary just as bodies become weary, so seek for them the delightful sayings of wisdom. And from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): 'The heart lives and dies. So when it is alive, discipline it with voluntary acts; and when it is dead, restrict it to obligatory acts.' And from Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (peace be upon him): 'Hearts have inclination and aversion, activity and languor. So when they are inclined, they see and understand; and when they are averse, they become wearied and bored. So take them at their inclination and activity, and leave them at their aversion and languor.' And from Imam al-Hasan al-Askari (peace be upon him): 'When hearts are active, entrust them [with knowledge]; and when they are averse, leave them.' What polishes the heart and illuminates it? The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever abstains from the world, Allah plants wisdom in his heart and makes his tongue speak with it.' 'These hearts rust just as iron rusts when water touches it.' It was asked: 'What is their polish?' He said: 'Frequent remembrance of death and recitation of the Quran.' 'Hearts have a rust like the rust of copper, so polish them with seeking forgiveness.' And from Imam Ali (peace be upon him): 'Revive your heart with admonition... and illuminate it with wisdom.' 'Revive your heart with admonition, kill it with asceticism, and strengthen it with certainty.' And from Imam Musa ibn Ja'far (peace be upon him): 'The plant grows in soft earth, not in rock. So likewise wisdom inhabits the humble heart and does not inhabit the arrogant tyrant's heart, because Allah has made humility the tool of intellect.' What hardens the heart? The Messenger Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'Three things harden the heart: listening to amusement, hunting, and visiting the ruler.' 'Beware of listening to musical instruments and singing, for they cause hypocrisy to grow in the heart just as water causes the herbage to grow.' And from the Commander of the Faithful Ali (peace be upon him): 'Tears do not dry except from hardness of heart, and hearts do not become hard except from abundance of sins.' 'He who hopes to live tomorrow hopes to live forever, and he who hopes to live forever hardens his heart.' And we have observed that clear confusion among the masses of exegetes regarding the concepts of chests, hearts, and souls. And in reality, each of these terms has a different purpose and special aim. The conclusion: The heart, in the Quranic theory of knowledge, is one of the great advisors to the soul, and it is specialized in emotional matters.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the heart's states and its role in acquiring knowledge. The author compiles a series of hadith from the Prophet (s) and the Imams (a) to illustrate that the heart has varying dispositions: inclination (iqbal) and aversion (idbar), activity (nashaat) and languor (futoor), life and death. The key principle is that knowledge and discipline should be imparted when the heart is receptive—when it is inclined, active, or alive—and should be restricted to essentials when it is averse, languid, or dead. The author then addresses what polishes the heart (jalla'), listing abstention from the world (zuhd), remembrance of death, recitation of the Quran, and seeking forgiveness (istighfar). Polishing the heart removes its rust and allows wisdom to take root. Following that, the author discusses what hardens the heart (qaswah), such as listening to amusement, hunting, visiting rulers, listening to music and singing, and hope for long life. The author concludes by clarifying a common confusion among exegetes regarding the terms chest (sadr), heart (qalb), and soul (nafs). In the Quranic epistemology, the heart is specifically the seat of emotions and one of the great advisors to the soul. Thus, the heart's emotional state directly impacts the ability to receive and act upon knowledge. The author does not here present a logical argument but rather collects transmitted evidence (naql) to establish the epistemological principle that knowledge acquisition is tied to the heart's condition.

Key Terms
قلب
اقبال
ادبار
نشاط
فتور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith from Imam Ali (a), what should one do when the heart is inclined (muqbil)?
A) Restrict it to obligatory acts
B) Burden it with supererogatory acts
C) Leave it alone
D) Force it to learn

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and worship should be timed to the heart's receptivity; forcing a reluctant heart backfires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Based on the hadith, describe a scenario where a teacher should reduce a student's workload and one where the teacher should increase it, using the terms iqbal and idbar.
?
Reflect on your own heart's state today: is it inclined or averse? Plan your study or worship accordingly, applying the principle of 'burden with supererogatory when inclined, restrict to obligatory when averse'.

Polishing the Heart: Tools for Spiritual Clarity
Hadith compilation on polishing the heart and its connection to wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the means of polishing the heart (jalla') from the hadith and explain how each contributes to removing spiritual rust and enabling wisdom.

Core Idea
The heart rusts like iron; polish it with zuhd, death remembrance, Quran, and istighfar.

The author moves from the heart's states to its purification. The heart, like metal, can rust and lose its shine. The Prophet (s) and the Imams (a) give specific tools to polish the heart. This lesson focuses on those tools: zuhd (abstention), frequent remembrance of death, recitation of the Quran, and istighfar (seeking forgiveness). Each tool removes a layer of neglect and prepares the heart to receive wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphor of rust
The hadith say hearts rust like iron or copper. What does rust represent? It represents neglect, sins, and worldly attachments that cloud the heart's natural receptivity to truth.
The Prophet (s) said: 'These hearts rust just as iron rusts when water touches it.'
2
List the polishing agents
From the narrations, note: zuhd (abstention from the world), frequent remembrance of death, recitation of the Quran, and istighfar. Also mentioned: admonition (maw'idha) and asceticism (zuhd).
3
Understand how each works
Zuhd detaches the heart from worldly distractions; remembering death reduces attachment; Quran and istighfar cleanse spiritually. Each removes a different type of 'rust'.
The Quran describes itself as a healing (shifa') for the hearts.
4
Connect to knowledge acquisition
A polished heart is like soft earth (as in the hadith of Imam Musa al-Kazim) where wisdom grows. Without polishing, wisdom cannot take root.
Imam Musa al-Kazim (a): 'The plant grows in soft earth, not in rock. So likewise wisdom inhabits the humble heart.'

Hardness of Heart: Obstacles to Knowledge and Grace
Hadith compilation on causes of hardness of heart

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the causes of hardness of heart (qaswat al-qalb) from the hadith and explain why each prevents the heart from receiving wisdom.

Core Idea
Sins, worldly attachments, and heedlessness harden the heart, blocking knowledge and tears.

Just as the heart can be polished, it can also become hard. The author presents several causes of hardness: listening to amusement, hunting, visiting rulers, music and singing, and hope for long life. Hardness of heart is a serious spiritual ailment that prevents the heart from being moved by truth, making it impervious to wisdom. This lesson examines these causes and their effects.

Guided Path
1
List the causes from the hadith
From the Prophet (s): three things harden the heart: listening to amusement (lahw), hunting, and visiting the ruler (sultan). Also: music and singing, and hoping for long life.
The Prophet (s) also warned that music and singing cause hypocrisy to grow in the heart.
2
Understand the mechanism
These activities distract from remembrance of Allah, increase attachment to the world, and encourage heedlessness (ghaflah). Over time, the heart becomes insensitive to spiritual truths.
Imam Ali (a): 'Tears do not dry except from hardness of heart, and hearts do not become hard except from abundance of sins.'
3
Connect to knowledge acquisition
A hard heart is like rock (as in the earlier simile): wisdom cannot inhabit it. Therefore, avoiding these causes is essential for the student of knowledge.
4
Identify remedies implied
The opposite of what causes hardness: turning to dhikr, humility, and repentance. The author earlier listed polishing agents; these are the natural remedies.

The inclination and aversion of hearts: It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him) his saying: 'Hearts have desire, inclination, and aversion. So approach them from [the direction of] their desire and inclination, for if the heart is forced, it becomes blind. Hearts have inclination and aversion. So when they are inclined, burden them with supererogatory acts; and when they are averse, restrict them to obligatory acts.' These hearts become weary just as bodies become weary, so seek for them the delightful sayings of wisdom. And from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): 'The heart lives and dies. So when it is alive, discipline it with voluntary acts; and when it is dead, restrict it to obligatory acts.' And from Imam Ali ibn Musa al-Rida (peace be upon him): 'Hearts have inclination and aversion, activity and languor. So when they are inclined, they see and understand; and when they are averse, they become wearied and bored. So take them at their inclination and activity, and leave them at their aversion and languor.' And from Imam al-Hasan al-Askari (peace be upon him): 'When hearts are active, entrust them [with knowledge]; and when they are averse, leave them.' What polishes the heart and illuminates it? The Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever abstains from the world, Allah plants wisdom in his heart and makes his tongue speak with it.' 'These hearts rust just as iron rusts when water touches it.' It was asked: 'What is their polish?' He said: 'Frequent remembrance of death and recitation of the Quran.' 'Hearts have a rust like the rust of copper, so polish them with seeking forgiveness.' And from Imam Ali (peace be upon him): 'Revive your heart with admonition... and illuminate it with wisdom.' 'Revive your heart with admonition, kill it with asceticism, and strengthen it with certainty.' And from Imam Musa ibn Ja'far (peace be upon him): 'The plant grows in soft earth, not in rock. So likewise wisdom inhabits the humble heart and does not inhabit the arrogant tyrant's heart, because Allah has made humility the tool of intellect.' What hardens the heart? The Messenger Muhammad (peace be upon him) said: 'Three things harden the heart: listening to amusement, hunting, and visiting the ruler.' 'Beware of listening to musical instruments and singing, for they cause hypocrisy to grow in the heart just as water causes the herbage to grow.' And from the Commander of the Faithful Ali (peace be upon him): 'Tears do not dry except from hardness of heart, and hearts do not become hard except from abundance of sins.' 'He who hopes to live tomorrow hopes to live forever, and he who hopes to live forever hardens his heart.' And we have observed that clear confusion among the masses of exegetes regarding the concepts of chests, hearts, and souls. And in reality, each of these terms has a different purpose and special aim. The conclusion: The heart, in the Quranic theory of knowledge, is one of the great advisors to the soul, and it is specialized in emotional matters.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the heart's states and its role in acquiring knowledge. The author compiles a series of hadith from the Prophet (s) and the Imams (a) to illustrate that the heart has varying dispositions: inclination (iqbal) and aversion (idbar), activity (nashaat) and languor (futoor), life and death. The key principle is that knowledge and discipline should be imparted when the heart is receptive—when it is inclined, active, or alive—and should be restricted to essentials when it is averse, languid, or dead. The author then addresses what polishes the heart (jalla'), listing abstention from the world (zuhd), remembrance of death, recitation of the Quran, and seeking forgiveness (istighfar). Polishing the heart removes its rust and allows wisdom to take root. Following that, the author discusses what hardens the heart (qaswah), such as listening to amusement, hunting, visiting rulers, listening to music and singing, and hope for long life. The author concludes by clarifying a common confusion among exegetes regarding the terms chest (sadr), heart (qalb), and soul (nafs). In the Quranic epistemology, the heart is specifically the seat of emotions and one of the great advisors to the soul. Thus, the heart's emotional state directly impacts the ability to receive and act upon knowledge. The author does not here present a logical argument but rather collects transmitted evidence (naql) to establish the epistemological principle that knowledge acquisition is tied to the heart's condition.

Distinguishing Heart, Chest, and Soul in Quranic Epistemology
Author concludes by distinguishing qalb, sadr, and nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the terms qalb, sadr, and nafs as used in the Quranic theory of knowledge, and explain the heart's specialization as the emotional advisor to the soul.

Core Idea
The heart, chest, and soul have distinct roles; the heart is the emotional advisor in the Quranic epistemology.

In the concluding paragraph of this passage, the author highlights a common confusion among exegetes regarding the terms chest (sadr), heart (qalb), and soul (nafs). He asserts that each term has a distinct purpose and goal. The heart is specifically described as one of the 'great advisors' to the soul, specialized in emotional matters. This clarification is crucial for a proper understanding of Quranic psychology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms and their confusion
The author says there is clear confusion (khalit) among exegetes about مفاهيم الصدور والقلوب والنفوس. These are three different concepts.
The terms: صدر (sadr), قلب (qalb), نفس (nafs).
2
Extract the author's conclusion
The author states: 'In the Quranic theory of knowledge, the heart (qalb) is one of the great advisors to the soul, specialized in emotional matters.' So the heart is the seat of emotions.
This implies the chest (sadr) and soul (nafs) have other functions.
3
Connect to the earlier hadith
The hadith about heart states (iqbal/idbar) deal with emotions and receptivity, consistent with the heart being the emotional center. The chest might be related to broader consciousness, and the soul to desires and intellect.
4
Understand the implication for tafsir
Knowing these distinctions helps interpret Quranic verses correctly. For example, 'hearts are sealed' (khatama 'ala qulubihim) refers to emotional/spiritual sealing, while 'chests contract' refers to unease.

The inclination and aversion of hearts: It is narrated from Imam Ali (peace be upon him) his saying: 'Hearts have desire, inclination, and aversion. So approach them from [the direction of] their desire and inclination, for if the heart is forced, it becomes blind. Hearts have inclination and aversion. The Heart (Qalb) as an Emotion-Specialized Advisor in Quranic Epistemology
Definitional conclusion about the heart's specialized role in emotional guidance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the heart's epistemic role in the Quranic theory of knowledge as distinct from other faculties.

Core Idea
The heart is not the intellect but the emotional advisor to the soul.

In this passage, the author concludes that the heart functions as a grand advisor to the soul, specializing in emotional matters. This conclusion is drawn from a series of hadiths from Imam Ali, Imam Musa al-Kazim, and the Prophet. By parsing these hadiths, we will extract the precise definition of the heart as an epistemic tool in Quranic theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the final conclusion
Read the last sentence: 'the heart in the Quranic theory of knowledge is one of the great advisors to the soul and is specialized in emotional matters.' This is the core claim.
2
Analyze the supporting hadiths
Each hadith illustrates a condition or effect related to the heart: Imam Ali’s hadith mentions reviving, illuminating, and strengthening the heart; Imam Musa al-Kazim uses a simile about humility; the Prophet lists hardening factors. These examples elaborate on the heart's nature.
3
Extract the definition components
From the conclusion and hadiths, identify the genus (what kind of thing the heart is: an advisor to the soul) and the differentia (what distinguishes it: specialization in emotional matters).
4
Distinguish from other terms
Note that the heart is not the chest (sadr) or the intellect ('aql). The passage explicitly contrasts the heart's emotional role with other epistemic tools, guarding against conflation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الامام علي عليه السلام قال: أحي قلبك بالموعظة ونوره بالحكمة وقوه باليقين. وعن الامام موسى بن جعفر عليه السلام: إن الزرع ينبت في السهل ولا ينبت في الصفا فكذلك الحكمة تعمر قلب المتواضع ولا تعمر قلب المتكبر الجبار لأن الله جعل التواضع آلة العقل. ما يقسي القلب قال الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم: ثلاث يقسين القلب: استماع اللهو وطلب الصيد وإتيان باب السلطان. إياكم واستماع المعازف والغناء فإنهما ينبتان النفاق في القلب كما ينبت الماء البقل. وعن أمير المؤمنين علي عليه السلام قال: ما جفت الدموع إلا لقسوة القلب وما قست القلوب إلا لكثرة الذنوب. من يأمل أن يعيش غدا فإنه يأمل أن يعيش أبدا ومن يأمل أن يعيش أبدا يقسو قلبه. والخلاصة: إن القلب في نظرية القرآن المعرفية أحد عظماء المستشارين للنفس وهو مختص بالأمور العاطفية.
And from Imam Ali, peace be upon him, he said: Revive your heart with admonition, illuminate it with wisdom, and strengthen it with certainty. And from Imam Musa ibn Ja'far, peace be upon him: Indeed, vegetation grows in soft soil and does not grow on rock; likewise, wisdom inhabits the humble heart and does not inhabit the arrogant, oppressive heart, because God made humility the tool of intellect. What hardens the heart: the Messenger, peace be upon him, said: Three things harden the heart: listening to frivolity, hunting, and frequenting the door of the ruler. Beware of listening to musical instruments and singing, for they grow hypocrisy in the heart as water grows vegetation. And from the Commander of the Faithful Ali, peace be upon him, he said: Tears do not dry except due to hardness of heart, and hearts do not harden except due to abundance of sins. Whoever hopes to live tomorrow hopes to live forever, and whoever hopes to live forever, his heart hardens. And the conclusion: Indeed, the heart in the Quranic theory of knowledge is one of the great advisors to the soul and is specialized in emotional matters.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a definitional conclusion about the heart's role in the Quranic theory of knowledge, supported by a series of hadiths. The author first quotes Imam Ali instructing to revive the heart with admonition, illuminate it with wisdom, and strengthen it with certainty. Then Imam Musa al-Kazim gives a simile: wisdom grows in the humble heart like plants in soft soil, not on rock, and humility is the tool of the intellect. Next, the Prophet lists three things that harden the heart: frivolity, hunting, and visiting rulers. He also warns that music and singing breed hypocrisy in the heart. Imam Ali adds that dryness of tears and hardness of heart come from sins, and that hoping to live forever hardens the heart. The conclusion draws all together: the heart is a grand advisor to the soul, specialized in emotions. This contrasts with other epistemic tools like the chest (sadr) and soul (nafs). The passage guards against mixing these concepts.
