The Chest as Repository of Information in Quranic Theory
Author argues that the chest stores knowledge, citing Quran and medical transplants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic claim that the chest stores information and evaluate the empirical evidence the author presents.

Core Idea
The chest is the repository of information, supported by Quran and modern transplants.

This lesson examines a unique synthesis of Quranic epistemology and modern medical evidence. The author argues that the chest (sadr) is not merely a physical cavity but a storehouse for information, thoughts, and emotions. Through medical transplant anecdotes, he claims that recipients retain memories of organ donors, supporting the Quranic theory that the chest contains inner realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence where the author summarizes the Quranic theory: 'al-sadr fi nazariyyat al-Qur'an mukaan wa makhzan li-kathir min al-ma'lumat...' This confirms the chest is a repository.
2
Examine the medical evidence
Note the two case studies: lung transplant and heart transplant recipients reportedly recalling donor memories. Evaluate what these anecdotes are meant to prove.
The author uses them to empirically validate the Quranic view.
3
Analyze the extrapolation
Read the hypothetical head transplant argument. The author claims that changing the body would change thinking, implying the chest organs influence the mind. Consider if this follows logically from the evidence.
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
Assess whether the medical anecdotes are sufficient to support the Quranic claim. Consider alternative explanations (e.g., psychological suggestion).
This step practices critical thinking within an Islamic framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ازدحمت هموم الصدر قلنا عسى يوما يكون لها انفراج (لقد ثبت من خلال التجارب الطبية التي اجريت في الولايات المتحدة الامريكية والتي تم خلالها استبدال رئتين مريضتين لاحد الاشخاص برئتين سليمتين من شخص آخر تعرض للموت بحادث دهس حيث انتزعت رئتيه قبل موته ان الشخص الذي زرعت فيه الرئتان اخذ يتذكر معلومات خاصة بالميت المدهوس. وكذلك حدث الامر نفسه عند استبدال زراعة قلب من شخص لآخر بالطريقة نفسها ان الشخص الذي زرع له قلب سليم اخذ يتذكر قصائد رومانسية تعود للميت صاحب القلب المزروع بمجرد ان تقرأ الام البيت الاول من قصائد وجدتها في خزانة ولدها الميت ونصل من خلال الحقائق القرآنية التي ايدتها التجارب الطبية الحديثة الى القول الجازم وهو ان الطب لو تطور الى درج خلع رأس رجل وزراعته في جسد جديد لتغير تفكير الرأس البشري بشكل جوهري بتأثير القلب والرئتين والتصميم الفطري للجسد الجديد وكذلك اذا تم استبدال الرئتين أو استبدال القلب . والخلاصة : ان الصدر في نظرية القرآن مكمن ومخزن لكثير من المعلومات والوساوس والأقوال والأفعال والإرادات والأفكار.
If the worries of the chest crowd, we say: perhaps one day there will be relief for it. It has been established through medical experiments conducted in the United States of America, during which two diseased lungs of a person were replaced with two healthy lungs from another person who died in a hit-and-run accident, where his lungs were removed before his death, that the person in whom the lungs were transplanted began to recall information specific to the deceased person who was run over. Likewise, the same thing happened when replacing a heart transplant from one person to another in the same way: the person who received a healthy heart began to recall romantic poems belonging to the deceased owner of the transplanted heart, as soon as the mother read the first verse of poems she found in the wardrobe of her deceased son. We conclude, through Quranic facts supported by modern medical experiments, the definitive statement that if medicine develops to the point of removing a man's head and transplanting it into a new body, the thinking of the human head would change fundamentally under the influence of the heart, lungs, and the innate design of the new body, and also if the lungs or heart are replaced. The summary: that the chest, in the Quranic theory, is a repository and storehouse for much information, whispers, statements, actions, wills, and thoughts.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an argument from empirical evidence and Quranic facts. It begins with a poetic line about the chest's worries, then presents medical case studies of lung and heart transplants where recipients reportedly gained memories of the organ donors. The author uses these to support the Quranic view that the chest (sadr) is not merely a physical cavity but the repository of information, thoughts, and actions. The argument extrapolates to a hypothetical head transplant, claiming the new body's organs and design would alter the mind. The conclusion reaffirms the chest as a storehouse for diverse mental contents.

نظرية القرآن في المعرفة

The Sexual Instinct and Its Influence on Knowledge in Quranic Epistemology
Author analyzes sexual instinct as a strong motive and its impact on the heart and mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Quranic theory of knowledge accounts for the sexual instinct's influence on the heart and mind, and identify its implications for Islamic psychology.

Core Idea
The sexual instinct shapes cognitive and emotional decisions – an insight from the Quranic theory of knowledge.

The author presents a Quranic perspective on the sexual instinct as a powerful motive affecting not only behavior but also knowledge acquisition and rational decision-making. This lesson unpacks that claim and connects it to broader Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about the sexual instinct's scope
Find where the author defines the sexual instinct and its activation at puberty. Note that it includes all sexual inclinations and actions, not just acts.
2
Distinguish between secular and Quranic views
Compare the psychological descriptions (behavior, maturity, cognitive level) with the Quranic statement about its influence on heart and self, and being a gateway for Satan.
3
Analyze the mechanism of influence
How does the instinct affect 'decisions of the heart and self'? Consider the link between sexual dissatisfaction and psychological disorder, and the claim that romantic love is repressed instincts affecting rational decisions.
4
Evaluate the consequences
Summarize the author's conclusions: sexual dissatisfaction causes anxiety and stress, and romantic love reflects instincts that distort natural decision-making.
5
Reflect on epistemological relevance
Connect to Islamic epistemology: the heart (qalb) is the seat of knowledge and spiritual perception. How does the sexual instinct interfere with this?

الغريزة الجنسية تظهر الحاجة الجنسية لغالبية الناس على انها دافع قوي للسلوك يتحرك في سن البلوغ أي عندما تبدأ الغدد الجنسية بالنضج والقدرة على افراز الهرمونات ويرى الكثير من العلماء المختصين ان هذا الدافع يظهر أيام الطفولة وهم لا يحددون الدافع الجنسي بالعمليات الجنسية فقط وانما يدخلون في اطاره جميع الميول والافعال الجنسية ان خطورة هذا الدافع تكمن في الصعوبات التي تقف امام التربية الصحيحة ويطرح علماء النفس في عالم اليوم دراسات عديدة عن اثر الجانب الجنسي في السلوك ومستوى النضج والمستوى المعرفي ' وتقول نظرية القرآن في المعرفة ان الغريزة الجنسية لها تأثير أصيل وقوي على قرارات القلب والنفس وهي منفذ مهم من منافذ الشيطان في الوقت نفسه. ان عدم الاكتفاء الجنسي هو سبب رئيس للاضطراب النفسي والقلق والاجهاد الدائم وان مايسمى بالحب الغرامي هو غرائز ممنوعة من الصرف تنعكس داخل العقل وتؤثر في قراراته الطبيعية. وهناك من الشواهد الشعرية في هذا المضمار ما يجل منه مقام هذا البحث قال الشاعر السري بن احمد الكندي بلاني الحب منك بما بلاني فشأني أن تفيض غروب شاني أبيت الليل مرتفقاً أناجي بصدق الوجه كاذبة الأماني فتشهد لي على الأرق الثريا ويعلم ما أجنّ الفـــــــرقـدان فيا ولع العواذل خل عني وياكف الغرام خذي عنان
The sexual instinct: the sexual need appears for most people as a strong motive for behavior, activated at puberty, i.e., when the sexual glands begin to mature and the ability to secrete hormones. Many specialized scientists see that this motive appears in childhood, and they do not define the sexual motive only by sexual acts, but include within its framework all sexual inclinations and actions. The danger of this motive lies in the difficulties that stand in the way of proper education. Psychologists today present many studies on the effect of the sexual aspect on behavior, level of maturity, and cognitive level. And the Quranic theory of knowledge says that the sexual instinct has an original and strong influence on the decisions of the heart and the self, and it is an important gateway among the gateways of Satan at the same time. That sexual dissatisfaction is a main cause of psychological disorder, anxiety, and constant stress, and that what is called romantic love is instincts prevented from discharge that reflect inside the mind and affect its natural decisions. There are poetic witnesses in this regard that are too great for this research. The poet al-Sarri ibn Ahmad al-Kindi said: 'Love has afflicted me from you with what it afflicted me, so my business is that my tearful eyes overflow. I spend the night sleepless, leaning, conversing with honest face and false hopes. So the Pleiades bear witness for me of sleeplessness, and the Two Guards [stars] know what I conceal. So leave me, O blame of the blamers, and take the reins, O passion of love.'
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage discusses the sexual instinct from a psychological and Quranic perspective. It first describes the instinct as a strong behavioral drive that activates at puberty, though some scientists see it emerging in childhood. It includes not just sexual acts but all related inclinations. The danger lies in educational difficulties. Then it presents the Quranic view: the sexual instinct strongly influences the heart and self and is a gateway for Satan. Sexual dissatisfaction causes psychological issues. Romantic love is presented as repressed instincts that affect rational thought. The passage ends with a poem illustrating the suffering of love.

How Desires Affect Knowledge: An Epistemological Analysis
Author discusses how desires affect human knowledge and decision-making

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how desires (shahwa) can distort knowledge acquisition and decision-making according to Islamic epistemological sources.

Core Idea
Desires corrupt the process of knowledge itself, not just ethics.

This lesson explores a fundamental epistemological principle in Islamic thought: that human desires, particularly the sexual instinct, are not merely ethical concerns but directly impact the acquisition and application of knowledge. The passage draws on prophetic warnings, a saying of Imam Ali, and poetic wisdom to emphasize that desires can lead to misguidance even after one possesses knowledge. Understanding this helps us see why Islamic disciplines like Akhlaq and Kalam insist on purification of the soul as a prerequisite for sound knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desires mentioned
The passage names 'shahwa al-batn wal-faraj' – desires of the belly and private parts, i.e., gluttony and sexual appetite. Notice that these are specifically singled out as sources of misguidance after knowledge.
2
Analyze the contrast between truth and falsehood
The author states that truth (al-haqq) is 'heavy and bitter' while falsehood (al-batil) is 'light and sweet'. This psychological observation explains why desires incline people toward falsehood: it is easier and more pleasurable in the moment.
Compare this with Quranic descriptions of truth vs. falsehood.
3
Reflect on Imam Ali's metaphor
Imam Ali says: 'The slave of desire is more humiliated than the slave of slavery.' This means that internal enslavement to one's own desires is worse than external slavery, because it corrupts the intellect and will.
Consider how this relates to the concept of 'aql (intellect) in Islamic epistemology.
4
Synthesize the epistemological conclusion
The passage concludes that the sexual instinct (al-ghariza al-jinsiyya) is part of the system of knowledge (nizam al-ma'rifa). This means that any complete theory of knowledge must account for how desires affect judgment, not just ignore them as external factors.
This challenges purely rationalist epistemologies that ignore the role of passions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضلالة بعد المعرفة ، ومضلات الفتن ، وشهوة البطن والفرج (الحق) ثقيل مر والباطل خفيف حلو ورب شهوة ساعة تورث حزنا طويلا ( وعن الامام علي بن ابي طالب (ع ) ( عبد الشهوة أرق من عبد الرق (ما أصعب على من استعبدته الشهوات أن يكون فاضلا) ، اقتصر من شهوة خالفت عقلك بالخلاف عليها قال الشاعر لا شيئ أجمل من عفاف زانه ورع ومن لبس العفاف تجملا طبعت سرائرنا على التقوى ومن طبعت سريرته على التقوى علا) الخلاصة : ان الغريزة الجنسية كما واضح في القرآن الكريم والسنة النبوية المطهرة لها تأثير قوي في كثير من قرارات النفس الانسانية وهي جزء من نظام المعرفة.
Misguidance after knowledge, and misleading trials, and the desire of the belly and the private parts. (Truth) is heavy and bitter, and falsehood is light and sweet. Perhaps a momentary desire brings long-lasting grief. And from Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib (as): 'The slave of desire is more humiliated than the slave of slavery.' (How difficult it is for one whom desires have enslaved to be virtuous). Restrain from a desire that opposes your intellect by opposing it. The poet said: 'Nothing is more beautiful than chastity adorned with piety, and one who wears chastity becomes elegant. Our innermost natures are imprinted with piety, and whoever's innermost nature is imprinted with piety rises.' Summary: The sexual instinct, as is clear in the Holy Quran and the purified prophetic tradition, has a strong influence on many decisions of the human soul and is part of the system of knowledge.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the obstacles to sound knowledge. The author begins by warning about misguidance after knowledge, citing the Prophet's concern. He lists misleading trials and desires of the belly and private parts as factors. He contrasts truth (heavy and bitter) with falsehood (light and sweet), and notes that a momentary desire can bring lasting grief. The quote from Imam Ali emphasizes that enslavement to desire is worse than physical slavery. The poet's lines underscore the value of chastity and piety. The summary concludes that the sexual instinct, according to Quran and Sunnah, strongly influences human decisions and is part of the knowledge system. This shows that desires are not merely ethical concerns but directly impact epistemology.

D) Desires are irrelevant to the system of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Desires are not external to the pursuit of knowledge; they are integral to the psychological process and can lead to error if unchecked.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Created Nature of Devils
Author describes creation and subordination of devils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the created nature and subordination of devils to Allah as a foundation for understanding their role in distorting knowledge.

Core Idea
Devils are not independent agents; their deceptive power is contingent on Allah's permission as a trial.

This lesson explores the author's description of devils—their origin, leadership, and attributes from Qur'an and hadith. Understanding their dependency on Allah is crucial because it defines the limits of their influence over human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creatures
Find the Arabic terms for devils (الشياطين), their leader (إبليس), and their species (الجن). Notice the plural and singular forms.
These terms recur in Islamic theology.
2
Extract their source of power
The text states 'جميع نفوذهم وقدرتهم من الله' (all their influence and power is from Allah). What does this imply about their independence?
3
Note their purpose
The author says their enmity is 'بعنوان عقاب أو امتحان' (as punishment or trial). How does this frame their role?
4
Examine the creation substance
They are created from fire (مَارِجٍ مِن نَار), which differentiates them from humans (clay). This affects their nature.
Refer to the Qur'anic verse quoted.

14. Devils: plural of shaytan. Their leader is al-Shaytan or Iblis, and he is from the jinn. And the devils, like the rest of existents, are purely submissive, and all their influence and power is from Allah who gives them authority, mission, and enmity towards humans, as a punishment or trial. We will address these creatures in a brief study in light of the verses of the Holy Quran, and then we will indicate their role in knowledge. A. Their substance of creation: He said, exalted: 'He created man from clay like pottery, and He created the jinn from a smokeless flame of fire.' The devils marry and reproduce. And [mention] when We said to the angels, 'Prostrate to Adam,' and they prostrated except Iblis; he was of the jinn and departed from the command of his Lord. So do you take him and his offspring as allies instead of Me, while they are an enemy to you? Wretched is the exchange for the wrongdoers. And Iblis is a name of the shaytan. It is narrated from Imam Ali (as) his saying: 'Behold! Indeed, the shaytan has gathered his party and called his cavalry and infantry, and indeed with me is my insight.' (O people! The beginning of the occurrence of trials is desires followed... and if truth were pure, there would be no disagreement, but a handful is taken from this and a handful from that, then they are mixed together, they both become turbid. Then at that point the shaytan takes control of his allies, and those for whom good has preceded from Allah are saved.) C. Created before humans: 'And indeed, We created man from dried clay of altered mud, and the jinn We created before from the fire of Samum.'
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion that identifies devils as factors that obstruct sound knowledge. The author defines devils as a collective whose leader is Iblis, from the jinn. He emphasizes that all their power is from Allah, and their enmity towards humans serves as punishment or trial. The author then begins a study on their role in knowledge. First, he discusses their substance: created from smokeless fire, and they reproduce. He quotes the Quranic verse about Iblis refusing to prostrate and highlights that they are enemies to humans. The author quotes Imam Ali saying the shaytan gathers his forces, and a hadith that trials arise from desires mixed with truth, allowing the shaytan to take control. Finally, he mentions that jinn were created before humans. The overall message is that devils actively mislead humans, affecting their knowledge.

Devils as Obstacles to Knowledge
Author explains the epistemological role of devils: mixing truth with falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how devils obstruct knowledge by mixing truth with falsehood, as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The greatest epistemological danger from devils is their ability to blend truth with falsehood, creating confusion.

This lesson focuses on the mechanism of devilish deception in knowledge. The author quotes a hadith about trials arising from desires mixed with truth, allowing shaytan to take control. We will unpack how this applies to distinguishing sound knowledge from corruption.

Guided Path
1
Locate the epistemological claim
Find the section where the author discusses 'دورهم في المعرفة' (their role in knowledge). The hadith about mixing truth and falsehood appears right after the quote from Imam Ali.
The hadith begins with 'يا أيها الناس إنمـا بـدء وقوع الفتن أهواء تتبع...'
2
Extract the mixing process
The hadith says: 'يؤخذ من هذا ضغث ومن هذا ضغث فيمزجان فيجللان معا'. What does this describe? It describes taking a handful from truth and a handful from falsehood and mixing them together.
'ضغث' means a handful or a bundle.
3
Identify the result
When truth and falsehood are mixed, what happens? The text says 'فهنالك استحوذ الشيطان على أوليائه' (then the shaytan takes control of his allies). Who are the saved? Those for whom good has preceded from Allah.
4
Connect to knowledge acquisition
How does this mixing affect a person's ability to know the truth? The devil exploits desires (أهواء) to blend error, leading to fitna (trial). This shows that epistemological confusion arises from a moral failing.

Assessing Scriptural Evidence for Claims about Jinn and Angels
Author enumerates Quranic and hadith evidence on jinn, Iblis, and angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scriptural basis and logical inference in a theological description of unseen beings.

Core Idea
Quran and hadith are authoritative for knowledge of the unseen, requiring careful textual analysis.

This passage from al-Fatlawi compiles Quranic verses and hadith to describe the origins and nature of jinn, Iblis, and angels. While not a logical argument, it demonstrates how Islamic epistemology relies on textual evidence. Our focus is on learning to trace claims back to their sources and evaluate the inferences drawn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claims
Read the passage and list the key claims made about jinn, Iblis, angels, and devils. For example, 'jinn are created from fire', 'Iblis is a jinn', 'angels are created from light'.
Use the enumeration A-E as a guide.
2
Locate the Scriptural Evidence
For each claim, find the Quranic verse or hadith cited as evidence. Note the exact source (surah, verse, or hadith collection).
Some claims have multiple evidences.
3
Analyze the Inference
Determine how the author moves from the scriptural text to the claim. Is it a direct statement (e.g., the verse says He created jinn from fire) or an indirect inference (e.g., Iblis was of jinn because the verse says he was from jinn and he disobeyed)?
In this passage, most inferences are direct quotes.
4
Evaluate Consistency
Check if any claims contradict each other or if the evidence is coherent. For example, the passage says both that jinn were created before man and that Iblis refused to prostrate, which are consistent.
This is a check for internal coherence.

Abd al-Zahra Turki al-Fatlawi: We will discuss these creatures in a brief research in light of the verses of the Noble Quran, and then we will indicate their role in knowledge. A - Their material of creation: He said, the Most High: (He created man from clay like pottery, and He created the jinn from a smokeless flame of fire). The devils copulate and reproduce. (And [mention] when We said to the angels, 'Prostrate to Adam,' and they prostrated, except Iblis. He was of the jinn and departed from the command of his Lord. Then will you take him and his descendants as allies instead of Me while they are to you an enemy? Wretched is the exchange for the wrongdoers.) Iblis is one of the names of the devil. It is narrated from Imam Ali, peace be upon him, his saying: (Indeed, the devil has gathered his party and summoned his cavalry and infantry, and indeed I have my insight... O people, the beginning of the occurrence of trials is desires followed... And if the truth were purely followed, there would be no disagreement; but a handful is taken from this and a handful from that, then they are mixed and become intertwined; so there the devil gains mastery over his allies, and those for whom the good has preceded from Allah are saved.) C - Created before man: (And indeed, We created man from sounding clay of altered black smooth mud. And the jinn We created before from scorching fire.) For angels: al-Hijr 26-27. It is narrated from the Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, in Kanz al-'Ummal: (Angels were created from light, jinn from a smokeless flame of fire, and Adam was created from what was described to you.) And in the same source, he said, peace be upon him: (Allah created the angels from light, and among them is one smaller than a fly.) And it is narrated from Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq, peace be upon him, in al-Bihar, vol. 59, p. 176: (By Him in Whose hand is my soul, the angels of the heavens are more than the number of dust on the earth. There is no place for a foot in the heaven except that there is an angel glorifying and sanctifying Him. And there is no tree or clod on the earth except that there is an angel appointed over it.) And from him, peace be upon him, in Tafsir al-Qummi, vol. 2, p. 206: (The angels do not eat nor drink nor marry; they live by the breeze of the Throne.) D - They are obligated: (And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me.) And among them are righteous and among them are rebels: (And among us are righteous and among us are less than that; we are of diverse ways.) E - Their leader refused to implement the divine command: Allah, the Exalted, wanted to appoint a vicegerent on earth, so He created man from clay and asked the angels to prostrate to this new creature: (So the angels prostrated, all of them together. Except Iblis; he was arrogant and was of the disbelievers. He said, 'O Iblis, what prevented you from prostrating to that which I created with My hands? Were you arrogant, or were you among the high ones?' He said, 'I am better than him. You created me from fire and created him from clay.' He said, 'Then get out of it, for indeed you are accursed. And indeed upon you is My curse until the Day of Recompense.') Iblis is from the jinn, but he became arrogant and thus became the leader over the angels. (He said, 'My Lord, because You have misled me, I will surely adorn [evil] for them on the earth and will surely mislead them all. Except Your chosen servants among them.' He said, 'This is a path [that leads] straight to Me. Indeed, My servants – no authority will you have over them, except those who follow you of the deviators. And indeed, Hell is the promised place for them all.')
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is part of the author's broader epistemological inquiry into the role of various created beings in the acquisition of knowledge. The author explicitly states that this is a brief discussion on these creatures (jinn and shayatin) based on Quranic verses, and that he will subsequently indicate their role in knowledge. He lists several points: (A) Their material of creation: Humans from clay, jinn from fire, as per Surah al-Rahman. He also notes that shayatin (devils) reproduce, citing the story of Iblis from Surah al-Kahf, where Iblis is identified as being from the jinn. (B) He then quotes a tradition from Imam Ali about the devil's gathering and the nature of trials, emphasizing that mixing truth with falsehood leads to confusion. (C) Jinn were created before humans, as per Surah al-Hijr. The author then includes several hadith about the creation of angels from light, their abundance, and their non-material nature (no eating, drinking, etc.). (D) Jinn and humans are obligated to worship Allah, and among jinn there are both righteous and rebellious groups, citing Surah al-Jinn. (E) The leader of the devils, Iblis, refused to prostrate to Adam, claiming superiority based on being created from fire. His expulsion and subsequent enmity are described in full from Surah Sad. The passage uses these scriptural references to lay the foundation for understanding the role of these beings in knowledge, presumably as sources of temptation or inspiration. The author follows a systematic enumeration of points, making this a didactic exposition.

Scriptural evidence from the Quran and hadith provides the basis for Islamic claims about the unseen world, requiring careful identification of the source and logical inference.

Shaytan's Actions: Incitement, Plotting, and Sorcery
Defines shaytan's three main actions: incitement, plotting, and sorcery as illusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three actions of Shaytan (tahrid, kayd, sihr) using Quranic and prophetic evidence, and explain why sorcery is considered an illusion rather than a transformation of reality.

Core Idea
Shaytan's three tools: incitement, plotting, and sorcery—all aim to mislead, but sorcery is mere illusion.

In this lesson, we explore the three primary actions attributed to Shaytan according to Islamic teachings: incitement (tahrid), plotting (kayd), and sorcery (sihr). Each is defined with reference to Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith. Understanding these actions helps us recognize the deceptive nature of Shaytan's influence, especially the illusory nature of sorcery, which does not change reality but only deceives the senses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three actions
From the passage, extract the three actions of Shaytan: incitement (tahrid), plotting (kayd), and sorcery (sihr). Note the Arabic terms and their approximate meanings.
The passage presents them in a numbered list-like structure.
2
Locate the evidence for each action
For each action, find the supporting evidence: a prophetic hadith for incitement, a Quranic verse (Surah Yusuf) for plotting, and a Quranic verse (Surah Al-Baqarah) plus the story of Musa for sorcery.
The hadith is about anger being from Shaytan.
3
Analyze the definition of sorcery (sihr)
Focus on the explicit definition: sorcery does not change the nature of things nor create a new reality; it only makes the senses and feelings imagine what the sorcerer desires. This is the key logical distinction.
Contrast with the magicians' illusion in the story of Musa.
4
Distinguish sorcery from reality transformation
Explain why the author emphasizes that sorcery is an illusion. Use the example of ropes appearing as snakes to illustrate that no physical change occurs.
This refutes the misconception that sorcery is supernatural causation.

الشَّيْطَان إنّهُ عَدُوٌّ مُضلَّ مُبِينٌ ( وقال النبي الاكرم محمد ( ص ) ( إن الغضب من الشيطان) ١٠ ) التحريض والكيد : ان الشيطان يحرض الانسان على النيل من الناس بغير الحق ( قَالَ يَا بَني لَا تَقصص رؤيَاكَ عَلَى إِخْوَتِكَ فَيَكِيدُوا لَكَ كَيْدًا إِنْ الشَّيْطَانَ لِلْإِنْسَانِ عَدُوٌّ مُبِينٌ) ١١ ) السحر : وهو بضاعة للشيطان يروجها للتضليل والشر والاعراض عن الحق والهدى . يقول تعالى ( واتبعوا ما تتلو الشياطينُ عَلَى مُلْكِ سُلَيْمَانَ وَمَا كَفَرَ سُلَيْمَانُ وَلَكنُ الشَّيَاطِينَ كَفَرُوا يُعَلِّمُونَ النَّاسَ السِّحْرَ)؛ (والسحر لا يغير من طبيعة الاشياء ولا ينشيء حقيقة جديدة لها . ولكنه يخيل للحواس والمشاعر بما يريده الساحر . وهذا هو السحر الذي صوره القرآن الكريم في قصة موسى عليه السلام عندما خيل الى الناس ان الحبال والعصي كأنها حيات تسعى وهو بهذه الطبيعة يؤثر في الناس وينشيء لهم مشاعر على وفق ايحائه مشاعر تخيفهم وتؤذيهم وتوجههم الوجهة التي يريدها الساحر) فالسحر ضرب من التضليل واسلوب واداة يحاول الشيطان من خلاله خلط بصائر الخير والشر وقد يستطيع خداع بعض الناس لبعض الوقت ولكنه لا يستطيع خداع كل الناس كل الوقت.
Satan is an enemy, a clear misleader. And the Prophet Muhammad (s) said: 'Anger is from Satan.' 10) Incitement and plotting: Satan incites man to unjustly harm people. (He said: O my son, do not relate your vision to your brothers, lest they plot against you a plot; indeed Satan is to man a clear enemy.) 11) Sorcery: It is merchandise of Satan which he promotes for deception, evil, and turning away from truth and guidance. Allah Most High says: 'And they followed what the devils recited over the kingdom of Solomon. And Solomon did not disbelieve, but the devils disbelieved, teaching people sorcery.' Sorcery does not change the nature of things nor create a new reality for them. Rather, it makes the senses and feelings imagine what the sorcerer desires. This is the sorcery depicted in the Holy Quran in the story of Moses (upon him peace) when he made people imagine that ropes and staffs were moving snakes. In this nature, it affects people and creates for them feelings according to its suggestion, feelings that frighten them, harm them, and direct them to the direction the sorcerer wants. Sorcery is a kind of deception and a method and tool by which Satan attempts to confuse the insights of good and evil. He may be able to deceive some people for some time, but he cannot deceive all people all the time.

Commentary
This passage defines three actions of Shaytan: incitement (tahrid), plotting (kayd), and sorcery (sihr). The author cites Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith to support each. For sorcery, the author clarifies that it does not alter reality but only creates illusions, using the example of Moses and the magicians whose ropes and staffs appeared to be snakes. The key point is that sorcery is deceptive, not transformative.

Imam Ja'far's Three Types of Sorcery
Classification of sorcery into three kinds by Imam Ja'far.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify sorcery into its three types as explained by Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq and distinguish between them.

Core Idea
Sorcery is not monolithic; it includes natural contrivances, sleight of hand, and demonic influence.

In this passage, a heretic asks Imam Ja'far about the origin and workings of sorcery. The Imam provides a clear classification that reveals the different mechanisms behind what is commonly called magic. This classification helps us understand that not all sorcery is supernatural; some is based on deception and some on demonic pacts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The heretic asks: 'What is its origin? How does the sorcerer perform its wonders? And what does it do?' Notice the three parts of the question.
The Imam's answer addresses these implicitly through the classification.
2
Extract the first type
The Imam compares sorcery to medicine: just as physicians have remedies for diseases, sorcerers contrive ailments for health and tricks for every situation. This type is based on natural cause-effect manipulation.
Example: creating an illness to then 'cure' it, gaining trust.
3
Extract the second type
This type involves snatching, speed, deceptions, and lightness. It is essentially sleight of hand and agility, without supernatural power.
Example: street magicians who appear to make objects disappear.
4
Extract the third type
The third type is what the allies of the devils take from the demons. This is directly demonic, involving spiritual alliance.
Example: claiming to foretell the future through jinn.
5
Compare the three types
Reflect on what each type requires: the first requires knowledge (like medicine), the second requires skill and practice, the third requires spiritual submission. This helps distinguish them in real situations.
In Islamic rulings, the first may be fraud, the second deception, the third shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سأل زنديق الامام جعفر الصادق عليه السلام فقال : اخبرني عن السحر ما أصله ؟ وكيف يقدر الساحر على ما يوصف من عجائبه ؟ وما يفعل ؟ قال : ان السحر على وجوه شتى وجه منها بمنزلة الطب كما ان الاطباء وضعوا لكل داء دواء فكذلك علم السحر احتالوا لكل صحة آفة ولكل عافية عاهة ولكل معنى حيلة . ونوع منه آخر خطفة وسرعة ومخاريق وخفة . ونوع منه ما يأخذ أولياء الشياطين منهم
A heretic asked Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): 'Tell me about sorcery: what is its origin? How does the sorcerer perform what is described of its wonders? And what does it do?' He said: 'Sorcery is of various kinds. One kind is comparable to medicine: just as physicians have appointed a remedy for every disease, similarly the science of sorcery has contrived an ailment for every health, a defect for every well-being, and a trick for every meaning. Another kind is snatching, speed, deceptions, and lightness. Another kind is what the allies of the devils take from them.'
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage records a question from a heretic (zindiq) to Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq about the origin and workings of sorcery. The Imam responds by dividing sorcery into three types. The first type is analogous to medicine: just as doctors prescribe cures, sorcerers devise ailments and tricks for every situation. The second type involves sleight of hand, speed, and deception. The third type involves direct demonic influence where the sorcerer receives power from devils. The classification is concise and categorical.

Key Terms
سحر
طب
خطفة
أولياء الشياطين

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Imam Ja'far divides sorcery into three distinct categories: medical-like contrivances, sleight of hand, and demonic acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Three Quranic Meanings of 'Sihr' (Sorcery)
The exegetes explain that the word 'sihr' in the Quran has three distinct meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three distinct meanings of the word 'sihr' in the Quran and parse them using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
The Quran uses 'sihr' in three senses: illusion, demonic alliance, and rejected transformation.

The word 'sihr' (sorcery) appears in the Quran with multiple meanings. Exegetes have classified them into three categories: deception and illusion, seeking demonic assistance, and a claimed physical transformation. This lesson will teach you to distinguish these meanings by examining the Quranic verses cited in the tafsir and understanding which meanings are accepted by the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first meaning: deception/illusion
Find the part of the passage that describes 'khida' and 'takhayyul' with no reality. Note the examples: sleight of hand and false speech. Reference the verses about the magicians of Pharaoh (7:116, 20:66).
This is the most common meaning in stories of Moses.
2
Identify the second meaning: seeking demonic help
Look for the term 'istijlāb' and the reference to devils teaching sorcery. The passage cites verse 2:102 about devils teaching sorcery.
This meaning involves actual contact with jinn.
3
Identify the third meaning: physical transformation (claimed)
Read the part about 'al-aghtam' who claim sorcery can change forms and natures. Note that the author dismisses this as having no reality for the muhaqqiqun.
This meaning is rejected by the verified scholars.
4
Analyze the difference between meanings
Compare the three: the first is purely illusory, the second involves real demonic interaction, the third is a false claim. The Quran uses 'sihr' in all three senses, but the exegetes emphasize the first two as valid interpretations.
Understanding the context of each verse is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشرح المفسرين مفردة { السحر على معان؛ الأول ؛ الخداع وتخييلات لا حقيقة لها نحو ما يفعله المشعوذ بصرف الابصار عما يفعله لخفة يد . وما يفعله النمام بقول مزخرف عائق للاسماع وعلى ذلك قوله تعالى (قَالَ أَلْقُوا فَلَمَّا أَلْقُوا سَحَرُوا أَعْيُنَ النَّاسِ وَاسْتَرهَبُوهُ وَجَاءُوا بِسِحْرٍ عَظِيمٍ ) ( قَالَ بَلْ أَلْقُوا فَإِذَا حِبَالَهُمْ وَعِصِيهِم يُخَيلُ إِلَيْهِ مِنْ سِحرِهِمْ أَنْهَا تَسْعَى) وبهذا النظر سموا موسى عليه السلام ساحرا) وَقَالُوا يَا أَيُّهَا السَّاحِرُ ادْعُ لَنَا رَبِّكَ ) الثاني ؛ استجلاب معاونة الشياطين بضرب من التقرب اليه كقوله تعالى هَلْ أَنبِّئُكُمْ عَلَى مَن تَنَزَّلُ الشَّيَاطِينُ ، تَنَزَّلُ عَلَى كُلِّ أَفَاكِ أثيم وعلى ذلك قوله تعالى (وَلَكنَّ الشَّيَاطِينَ كَفَرُوا يُعَلِّمُونَ النَّاسَ السِّحْرَ) الثالث ؛ ما يذهب اليه الاغتام وهو اسم لفعل يزعمون أنه من قوته يغير الصور والطبائع فيجعل الانسان حمارا ولاحقيقة لذلك عند المحصلين.
And the exegetes explain the word 'sorcery' as having meanings: First: deception and illusory things with no reality, like what the trickster does by diverting eyes from what he does due to hand lightness, and what the tale-bearer does with embellished speech that hinders hearing. And accordingly Allah's saying: 'He said: Throw. And when they threw, they bewitched the eyes of the people and terrified them and produced great sorcery.' 'He said: Rather, throw. And suddenly their ropes and staffs seemed to him from their sorcery to be moving.' And with this view they called Moses (peace be upon him) a sorcerer, and said: 'O sorcerer, call upon your Lord for us.' Second: seeking the assistance of devils through a kind of drawing close to them, like Allah's saying: 'Shall I inform you upon whom the devils descend? They descend upon every sinful liar.' And accordingly Allah's saying: 'But the devils disbelieved, teaching people sorcery.' Third: what the 'aghtam' hold, which is a name for an action they claim has power to change forms and natures, making a man into a donkey, but there is no reality for that among those who achieve the truth.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage from the exegetes (mufassirun) explains that the word 'sihr' in the Quran has three distinct meanings. The first meaning is deception and illusions that have no reality, exemplified by sleight of hand and false speech. The Quranic story of Moses and the magicians is cited, where the magicians' ropes and staffs appeared to be snakes, and thus Moses was called a sorcerer. The second meaning is seeking help from devils through drawing close to them, supported by verses about devils teaching sorcery. The third meaning is the claim of some (referred to as 'al-aghtam') that sorcery can change forms and natures, such as turning a man into a donkey, but the author dismisses this as having no reality for the 'muhaqqiqun' (those who achieve verification).

Key Terms
سحر (sihr): In this tafsir, sihr has three meanings: illusion, demonic alliance, and claimed transformation (dismissed as unreal). [Note: The third meaning is rejected by verified scholars; this text aligns with the majority view that sorcery is illusory.]
خداع (khida'): Deception; the first meaning of sihr, involving illusion and trickery with no reality. [Note: No significant shift.]
تخييل (takhayyul): Making something appear as something else; imaginary effects. [Note: No significant shift.]
استجلاب (istijlāb): Seeking to bring; specifically, seeking assistance from devils through proximity or supplication. [Note: No significant shift.]
اغتام (aghtam): A group or individuals who hold that sorcery can physically transform beings; their view is rejected. [Note: This term is rare; possibly a misspelling of 'أوغام' or similar. The passage dismisses their claim.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the exegetes, which meaning of 'sihr' is considered to have no reality by those who achieve the truth?
A) Deception and illusions
B) Seeking demonic assistance
C) Changing forms and natures (e.g., man to donkey)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The word 'sihr' in the Quran has three distinct meanings: illusion, demonic alliance, and a physically transformative claim which is rejected by verified scholars.

Satan's Corruption of Inner Perceptions
Author concludes that devils corrupt the system of knowledge and the soul's decisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the cognitive faculty (al-basair) targeted by Satan according to the author and explain how its corruption leads to faulty decision-making.

Core Idea
Satan corrupts not just will but the very cognitive ability to distinguish truth from falsehood.

This passage concludes the author's discussion on the destructive role of devils, emphasizing that beyond leading to disbelief, Satan's greatest harm is corrupting the inner perceptions (basair). This faculty is essential for discerning truth, morality, and beauty. When Satan destroys it, human concepts become confused, and the soul makes evil decisions. Understanding this claim is crucial for Islamic epistemology because it highlights that knowledge is not merely intellectual but also spiritual—protected only through divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target faculty
Locate in the Arabic the term 'al-basair' (البصائر). This refers to inner perceptions or spiritual insight—the faculty that distinguishes truth from falsehood and good from evil. Note that the author explicitly says Satan destroys the system of these perceptions.
Compare with physical sight (basar); basair is deeper insight.
2
Describe the destructive effect
The author says Satan 'demolishes it from its foundation' and causes confusion: concepts of good and evil, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness become mixed. This is not merely intellectual error but a fundamental disorientation of the soul’s cognitive/moral compass.
This goes beyond mere temptation; it is epistemological sabotage.
3
Connect corruption to decision-making
The passage concludes that this destruction manifests in 'the evil decisions of the soul' (qararat al-nafs al-sayyi'a) and the soul that commands to evil (al-nafs al-ammara bi al-su'). The corruption of basair directly causes faulty judgments and immoral choices.
The soul's decisions are tied to its perceptual clarity.
4
Summarize the author's conclusion
The author states that devils have a 'vital destructive role' (دور حيوي تخريبي) in two domains: the system of knowledge (nizam al-ma'rifa) and the type of decision the soul makes. This is a unified claim tying epistemology to moral psychology.
This conclusion is central to understanding the Islamic view of spiritual warfare.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينسفه من اساسه كما في حالة الجنون الذي يَتَخَبْطُهُ الشَّيْطَانُ مِنَ الْمَسَ) وكني بالمس عن الجنون ولعل أعظم تخريب يحققه الشيطان بعد ايصال الانسان الى الكفر هو هذا المس الذي يحطم نظام البصائر. فتختلط في ذات الانسان مفاهيم الخير والشر ، الصواب والخطأ ، الجمال والقبح الخ الخلاصة : إن الشياطين لهم دور حيوي تخريبي في نظام المعرفة وفي نوع القرار الذي تتخذه النفس . يتجلى في قرارات النفس السيئة او الامارة بالسوء
And he demolishes it from its foundation as in the case of madness with which Satan confounds from the touch. And by 'touch' madness is meant. Perhaps the greatest destruction that Satan achieves after bringing man to unbelief is this touch which destroys the system of inner perceptions. So within man, the concepts of good and evil, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, etc., become mixed. Conclusion: The devils have a vital destructive role in the system of knowledge and in the kind of decision that the soul makes. It manifests in the evil decisions of the soul or the soul that commands to evil.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is the author's conclusion regarding the role of devils in epistemology. It opens by describing how Satan destroys knowledge from its foundation, likening it to madness resulting from his 'touch'. The author notes that Satan's greatest destruction after leading to disbelief is this 'touch' that shatters the system of inner perceptions (basair). As a result, within the human self, the distinctions between good and evil, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness become confused. The author then summarizes that devils have a vital destructive role in the knowledge system and in the type of decision the soul makes, manifesting in the soul's evil decisions or the soul that commands to evil (al-nafs al-ammara bi al-su'). This is a direct epistemological claim: the devil corrupts not just will but the cognitive capacity to distinguish truth from falsehood and good from evil.

Key Terms

المعرفة

D) Leading man to commit major sins

Key Takeaway
Satan's primary destructive act is corrupting the inner perceptions (basair), leading to moral and epistemic confusion that manifests in evil decisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Experience and Rational Judgment in Islamic Epistemology
Author argues for the role of experience in knowledge and its Islamic roots.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological relationship between experience and rational judgment as presented in the passage, and identify the historical claim that Islamic civilization preceded the West in using experimental methods.

Core Idea
Experience without rational judgment is sterile; Muslims pioneered the experimental method.

This passage addresses the epistemological status of experience. The author argues that while experience is crucial, its conclusions depend on rational judgment. Moreover, the author contests the Western monopoly on experimental science, asserting that Muslims and Greeks preceded the West. This lesson unpacks these claims and their significance for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim about experience
Read the first sentence: 'Experience is one of the main tools of knowledge.' Note its high status in the West. Then find the qualification: 'the conclusion from experience depends on a rational judgment; without it, experience would be sterile.' This is the central epistemological point.
2
Analyze the condition attached to experience
The author explicitly states that experience alone is insufficient. The inference from empirical data requires a rational judgment (ḥukm ʿaqlī). Ask yourself: why would experience be 'sterile' without reason? Reason interprets data, formulates hypotheses, and draws conclusions.
3
Recognize the historical claim
The passage asserts that the West is not the originator of the experimental method; rather, the Greeks and early Muslims preceded them. This challenges a common narrative. Note the mention of 'great giants in natural sciences' who followed experimental paths.
4
Connect to Islamic epistemology
Consider how this relates to the Islamic intellectual tradition. In Islamic sciences like kalām and fiqh, empirical observation (like ʿurf or natural phenomena) is combined with rational reasoning to produce knowledge. This passage validates that approach as historically grounded.
This step helps you see why the tool matters for Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التجربة أحدى أدوات المعرفة الرئيسة وقد احتلت مكانة سامية في الغرب وتربعت على عرش المعرفة وشكلت حجر الاساس لكثير من الاختراعات والاكتشافات ولكن يجب ملاحظة ان الاستنتاج من التجربة يتوقف على حكم عقلي ولولاه لكانت التجربة عقيمة كما ينبغي القول ان الغرب ليس مبتكرا للتجربة بل سبقه اليها الاغريق والاسلاميون في العصور الاسلامية الاولى ان الاسلام انتج فطاحل وأساطين عظاما في العلوم الطبيعية سلكوا مسالك التجارب والاختبارات العلمية فخرجوا بنتائج باهرة واكتشافات عظيمة
Experience is one of the main tools of knowledge, and it has occupied a lofty position in the West and sat on the throne of knowledge and formed the cornerstone for many inventions and discoveries. But it must be noted that the conclusion from experience depends on a rational judgment; without it, experience would be sterile. Likewise, it should be said that the West is not the innovator of experience; rather, the Greeks and Muslims preceded it in the early Islamic centuries. Indeed, Islam produced great giants and masters in the natural sciences who followed the paths of scientific experiments and tests, and brought out magnificent results and great discoveries.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the role of experience in knowledge. The author begins by acknowledging the high status of experience in Western civilization, but then immediately qualifies this by stating that the inference from experience depends on a rational judgment; without it, experience is barren. This is a critical point: experience alone does not yield knowledge unless interpreted by reason. The author then asserts that the West is not the originator of the experimental method; rather, the Greeks and early Muslims preceded them. The passage concludes by praising Islamic civilization for producing great scientists who advanced experimental science and achieved remarkable discoveries.

Key Takeaway
The passage emphasizes that experience requires rational interpretation and that Muslims were pioneers in experimental science.

The Soul's Connection to the Body via the Brain
Author discusses the soul's control over the body and its center in the brain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's argument that the soul's connection to the body occurs through the brain, citing the Quranic and philosophical evidence used.

Core Idea
The brain is the central hub connecting the soul to the body.

This lesson examines a key philosophical position in Islamic thought: the soul's connection to the body via the brain. The author grounds this claim in Quranic verses and a hadith, then elaborates with Mulla Sadra's metaphor of the soul as a kingdom. You will learn to analyze the evidence and the structure of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that explicitly states the soul's connection to the body occurs through the brain. This is the thesis of the passage.
Look for the phrase 'لا مناص من القول ان اتصال النفس بالبدن يتم عن طريق الدماغ'
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
List the Quranic verses and hadith cited. Note how they relate to the soul's existence and connection to the body. Are they direct proof or illustrative?
Verses include 6:93, 39:42 and the hadith about assembled spirits.
3
Analyze the empire analogy
The author compares the soul to a leader and the body to an empire. Describe how this analogy supports the idea that the brain is the center of connection.
The soul appears in different faculties but its essential center is the brain.
4
Assess Mulla Sadra's contribution
Read the quote from Sadr al-Muta'allihin. How does his statement that the soul is a kingdom resembling the Creator's reinforce the author's position?
It emphasizes the soul's comprehensive dominion over both abstract and material aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظَّالِمُونَ فِي غَمَرَاتِ الْمَوْتِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةُ بَاسِطُو أَيْدِيهِمْ أَخْرِجُوا أَنْفُسَكُمْ الله يتوفى الأنفُس حين مَوتِهَا وَالَّتِي لَم تَمُتْ فِي مَنَامِهَا فَيُمْسِكَ الَّتِي قَضَى عَلَيْهَا الْمَوْتَ وَيُرْسِلُ الْأَخْرَى إِلَى أَجَلٍ مُسَمَّى إِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَآيَاتِ لِقَوْمٍ يَتَفَكَّرُونَ) وفي الحديث الشريف الارواح جنود ) ولا مناص من القول ان اتصال النفس بالبدن يتم عن طريق الدماغ الذي له وجهان وجه مادي كما يشاهده الاطباء وآخر روحي كما يدرسه الفقهاء. ان النفس تسيطر على جميع أجزاء الجسد المادية والمعنوية. فامبراطورية الجسد خاضعة لقرار النفس وهي تتركز بالشأن الذي تبغيه فتارة تظهر في العقل واخرى في القلب وثالثة في الحواس كالزعيم الذي يزور أجزاء امبراطوريته عند الحاجة ولكن مركز اتصالها الجوهري هو الدماغ . يقول صدر المتألهين الشيرازي ان النفس مملكة تشبه مملكة بارئها تشمل على أنواع الجواهر والاعراض المجردة والمادية)؛
'The wrongdoers are in the agonies of death, and the angels are stretching out their hands, "Bring out your souls." Allah takes the souls at the time of their death, and those that do not die [He takes] during their sleep. Then He keeps those for which He has decreed death and releases the others for a specified term. Indeed in that are signs for a people who give thought.' (Quran 6:93, 39:42) And in the noble hadith: 'Spirits are assembled troops.' There is no escape from saying that the connection of the soul to the body occurs through the brain, which has two aspects: a material aspect as seen by physicians, and a spiritual aspect as studied by jurists. The soul controls all material and spiritual parts of the body. The empire of the body is subject to the decision of the soul, and it concentrates on the matter it desires, sometimes appearing in the intellect, sometimes in the heart, and thirdly in the senses, like a leader who visits parts of his empire when needed, but its essential center of connection is the brain. Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi says: The soul is a kingdom resembling the kingdom of its Creator, encompassing types of substances and accidents, both abstract and material.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage begins by citing Quranic verses and a hadith to establish the reality of the soul and its connection to the body. The author then asserts that the soul's connection to the body is through the brain, which has both a material aspect (studied by medicine) and a spiritual aspect (studied by fiqh). The soul governs all parts of the body, appearing in different faculties (intellect, heart, senses) as needed, but the central hub is the brain. The author supports this with Mulla Sadra's statement that the soul is a kingdom similar to that of its Creator, containing all types of substances and accidents, both abstract and material. This establishes the soul's comprehensive dominion.

The Soul's Essential Movement in Sadrian Philosophy
Author describes the soul's essential movement from material to abstract reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key characteristics of the soul's essential movement (al-haraka al-jawhariyya) and distinguish between the spirit (ruh) and soul (nafs) as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
The soul is not static; it is in constant essential movement from matter to spirit.

This passage introduces a central concept in Mulla Sadra's metaphysical system: the essential movement of the soul. Understanding this movement is crucial for grasping how the soul evolves from a material state to an abstract reality, using the body as a tool for perfection. The author quotes Hasan Ibrahimiya to elaborate on this intrinsic dynamic.

Guided Path
1
Identify key terms
Locate the terms 'al-ruh' (spirit) and 'al-nafs' (soul) in the Arabic. Note how the author describes the spirit 'donning the garment' of the soul. This suggests a relationship or stage.
The spirit is the starting point; the soul is the embodied stage that undergoes movement.
2
Extract the description of movement
Find the phrase 'haraka jawhariyya mustamirra' (continuous essential movement). What does the author say accompanies this movement? How is the soul described as developing and perfecting?
The movement is intrinsic (dhatiyya) and perfectional (takamuliyya).
3
Understand the endpoint
Identify what the soul rids itself of at the end of the journey. What does it cross into? Also note the role of the body as a means.
The soul sheds matter and its laws, entering the world of abstract realities (al-mujarradat).
4
Relate to the concept of essential movement
Connect this passage to Mulla Sadra's doctrine of al-haraka al-jawhariyya, which holds that all substances are in continuous change. How does this apply specifically to the soul?
This movement is not accidental but essential to the soul's very substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الروح عندما تلبس ثوب النفس تصاحبها حركة جوهرية مستمرة تتطور فيها وتتكامل ويشرح الاستاذ حسن ابراهيميان هذه الحركة فيقول ان النفس تمثل نتاجا لحركة تكاملية لجوهر المادة بمعنى ان النفس بخصوصياتها تسعى نحو الكمال بحركة ذاتية جوهرية وفي نهاية هذا السير تتخلص من المادة وقوانينها وتعبر الى عالم المجردات مستخدمة البدن في طي مداخل الكمال
Indeed, when the spirit dons the garment of the soul, it is accompanied by a continuous essential movement in which it develops and perfects itself. Professor Hasan Ibrahimiya explains this movement, saying: 'The soul represents a product of a perfectional movement of the substance of matter, meaning that the soul with its particularities seeks perfection through an essential intrinsic movement, and at the end of this journey it rids itself of matter and its laws and crosses into the world of abstract realities, using the body as a means for traversing the stages of perfection.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage explains a key concept in Sadrian philosophy: the soul's essential movement (al-haraka al-jawhariyya). The author states that when the spirit (ruh) assumes the form of the soul (nafs), it begins a continuous essential movement of development and perfection. Hasan Ibrahimiya elaborates: the soul is the outcome of a perfectional movement inherent in the substance of matter. The soul, with its particular characteristics, seeks perfection through an intrinsic movement. At the end of this journey, it sheds matter and its laws and enters the world of abstract realities, using the body as a vehicle to traverse the stages of perfection. This illustrates the dynamic nature of the soul in its journey from material to spiritual.

Key Terms
الروح
الحركة الجوهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Matter and its laws
C) The spirit
D) The intellect

Key Takeaway
The soul undergoes a continuous essential movement from matter to abstract reality, using the body as a means for perfection.

Tabatabai's Three Stages of the Soul
Tabatabai's three-stage model of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ontological stages of the soul according to Tabatabai and explain how the soul transitions from identity with the body to complete independence.

Core Idea
The soul begins as the body itself, then becomes distinct, then fully independent.

This passage from Tafsir al-Mizan introduces a dynamic view of the soul's relationship with the body. Tabatabai outlines three stages: identity, distinction, and independence. Understanding these stages helps in analyzing Islamic discussions on the soul's nature, especially in philosophical exegesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three stages
Read the Arabic and locate the three phrases marking the stages: 'عين البدن', 'تمتاز بالإنشاء منه', and 'تستقل عنه بالكلية'.
Each corresponds to a distinct relationship: identity, distinction, independence.
2
Understand the first stage ('ayn al-badan)
The soul is identical to the body. This implies that at the moment of initial existence, the soul has no separate reality; it is the body itself.
This is a strong form of psychophysical unity.
3
Understand the second stage (distinction via creation)
The soul becomes distinct by being 'created from' the body. This suggests a generated, emergent relationship rather than pre-existence.
The word 'insha' implies a new creation.
4
Understand the third stage (complete independence)
The soul becomes completely independent: it neither mixes with nor consumes the body but surrounds it like a garment (kall).
This final stage is one of transcendence and encompassing.
5
Synthesize the progression
Consider how this three-stage model explains the soul's evolution from material dependence to spiritual independence.
This framework is used in later Islamic philosophy to discuss the soul's immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما صاحب تفسير الميزان فيقول (النفس في أول وجودها عين البدن ، ثم تمتاز بالإنشاء منه ، ثم تستقل عنه بالكلية فتصير بحال (لا) تمازج البدن ولا تواكله وإنما هي كلل للبدن محيطة به)
As for the author of Tafsir al-Mizan, he says: 'The soul in its first existence is identical to the body, then it becomes distinct by being created from it, then it becomes completely independent from it, so it comes to be in a state where it neither mixes with the body nor consumes it, but rather it is a garment for the body encompassing it.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage quotes the contemporary exegete Allama Tabatabai's view on the ontological stages of the soul. According to him, the soul initially is identical to the body. Then it becomes distinct through being generated from the body. Finally, it becomes completely independent, such that it does not mix with or consume the body, but rather surrounds it as a garment. This gradual independence is a key concept in understanding the soul's relationship with the body.

Key Terms
عين البدن

Checkpoint

B) The soul is identical to the body
C) The soul is a garment for the body
D) The soul is created from the body

Key Takeaway
Tabatabai's three-stage model shows the soul evolving from being identical to the body to being completely independent and encompassing it.

The Unity of the Soul and the Commanding Soul
Author explains the unity of the soul and introduces the commanding soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of the unity of the soul and identify the first rank of the soul as the commanding soul (nafs ammara) according to the Quranic tradition.

Core Idea
The soul is one; its many names reflect its manifestations, not multiple souls.

This passage from an Islamic philosophy text clarifies a key misunderstanding: the soul is not multiple but one, with different designations capturing its states. The author uses analogies to physical harmony and Quranic concepts to introduce the first rank, the commanding soul.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the thesis
The author explicitly states that the human soul is one. Note that this is a corrective against interpretations that treat terms like 'plant soul' and 'animal soul' as separate entities.
The soul is a single spiritual substance.
2
Understand the analogy of predomination
The author compares naming the soul to describing a person as 'truthful' because their truthfulness predominates over lying. Similarly, the soul is named by its predominant manifestation at a given time.
This is a key insight: labels reflect predominance, not essence.
3
Follow the body comparison
Islamic scholars compare the soul to the body: harmony equals health, imbalance equals sickness. A 'sick soul' is one out of balance, but the soul was created balanced originally.
The soul's primordial state is sound (fitra).
4
Identify the first rank
The first rank mentioned is the commanding soul (al-nafs al-ammara), which by nature inclines to evil. Man cannot claim purity but can restrain it through Allah's mercy.
This is directly from Quran 12:53.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن النفس الانسانية واحدة وان تسميات النفس النباتية والنفس الحيوانية وغيرها من المسميات انما هو من باب تسجيل تجليات النفس كما يقال ان فلانا صادق فيقصد إن صدقه برز أكثر من كذبه أو إن فلانا شريف ويقصد إن شرفه برز اكثر من فجوره وقد شبه علماء المسلمين النفس بالجسم فاذا كان الجسم متناسقا في أعضائه يكون سليما من الامراض أما إذا اختل عضو فيه فان الجسم يفقد توازنه ويصبح سقيما ولهذا يقال ان نفس فلان مريضة وهذا المفهوم لا يتعارض مع كون النفس عند بداية خلقها مستوية ( متكاملة الخلقة) وقد عبر القرآن الكريم عن النفس بمعان عدة تمثل حالاتها أو تجلياتها سنتطرق اليها بخطوطها العامة التي يمكن تسميتها بانها مراتب النفس ١ ) النفس الأمارة : ان النفس بطبعها تدعو الى مشتهياتها من السيئات فليس للانسان ان يبرىء نفسه من الميل الى السوء وانما له ان يكف عن أمرها بالسوء ودعوتها الى الشر وذلك برحمة من الله سبحانه (وَمَا أَبَرَى نَفْسِي إِنْ
The human soul is one, and the names 'plant soul', 'animal soul', and other designations are only ways of recording manifestations of the soul, just as it is said 'so-and-so is truthful' meaning his truthfulness appears more than his lying, or 'so-and-so is noble' meaning his nobility appears more than his immorality. Muslim scholars have compared the soul to the body: if the body is harmonious in its parts, it is healthy; if a part becomes disturbed, the body loses its balance and becomes ill. Hence it is said 'so-and-so's soul is sick'. This concept does not contradict that the soul at the beginning of its creation is balanced (perfectly created). The Holy Quran has expressed the soul with several meanings representing its states or manifestations. We will touch upon them in general outlines, which can be called the ranks of the soul. First: The commanding soul (al-nafs al-ammara): The soul by its nature calls to its desires of evil deeds. Man should not absolve himself from inclination to evil, but he can restrain it from commanding evil and calling to evil, and that is by the mercy of Allah Almighty. (Quran: 'And I do not absolve myself, indeed...')
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage discusses the unity of the soul despite its various designations. The author argues that terms like 'plant soul' and 'animal soul' refer to different manifestations of the same soul, not distinct entities. He uses the analogy of describing a person as truthful or noble, meaning that quality predominates. Similarly, the soul can be described by its predominant characteristic. The author compares the soul to the body: a healthy body has harmony, a sick body has imbalance. A 'sick soul' means one that is unbalanced. This does not contradict that the soul was created balanced originally. Then the author introduces the Quranic ranks of the soul, beginning with the nafs ammara (the commanding soul), which naturally inclines towards evil. Man cannot claim innocence from evil inclination, but can restrain it through Allah's mercy. The passage ends with a partial Quranic verse.

Key Terms
النفس الأمارة
تجليات النفس

Key Takeaway
The soul's oneness is preserved despite varied manifestations; the commanding soul is its initial state inclined to evil.

The Three Types of Soul (Nafs) in the Quran
Author defines the three types of nafs with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of nafs (ammara, lawwama, mutma'inna) based on their Quranic descriptions and functions in Islamic psychology.

Core Idea
The soul is not monolithic: it can command evil, blame itself, or find tranquility.

The Quran speaks of the soul (nafs) in three distinct states, each reflecting a different spiritual condition. Understanding these states is crucial for self-purification (tazkiyah). This lesson will walk you through the definitions and evidence for each type as presented by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Read the Arabic passage and list the three types of nafs: al-ammara, al-lawwama, and al-mutma'inna. Note the Quranic verses used for each.
The types are not separate souls but states of one soul.
2
Extract the defining characteristic of each type
For each type, find the key phrase that describes its function: ammara commands evil; lawwama blames and reproaches; mutma'inna is tranquil and pleased.
The author defines lawwama as the conscience (ضمير) that reproaches after sin.
3
Note the supporting Quranic evidence
For lawwama, Quran 75:1-2 is cited; for mutma'inna, Quran 89:27-30. For ammara, a saying of Imam Ali is used. Understand how these proofs ground the definitions.
4
Understand the progression and relationship
The author implies a progression: the ammara can lead to lawwama through reproach, and lawwama can lead to mutma'inna through correction. Reflect on how this maps to the spiritual journey.
The lawwama is the turning point from sin to repentance.
5
Analyze the exegetical debate on lawwama
The author mentions two interpretations: one that it is the disbelieving soul blaming itself on Judgment Day, and another that it is the believing soul repenting in this world. Decide which view seems stronger based on the passage.
The author favors the second view because God swears by it, indicating high station.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الامام علي عليه السلام إن النفس لامارة بالسوء والفحشاء فمن ائتمنها خانته ومن استنام اليها اهلكته ومن رضي عنها أوردته شر المورد. والدعوات الأخلاقية جاءت لتعديل ذلك الميل وتحفيز النفس لكي تتجه في مسير السعادة والابتعاد عن الافراط والتفريط. إن النظريات المادية نادت بالانصياع لرغبات اللذة في حين نادت الرهبانية بكبح جماح اللذات والشهوات والعزوف عن الحياة في الكهوف والأديرة أما الاسلام فقد راح يدعو الى منهج وسط بينهما (قل من حرم زينة الله التي اخرج لعباده والطيبات من الرزق) وفي الوقت نفسه يأمر بكبح جماح النفس عن ارتكاب المعاصي والسيئات التي توجب الفوضى في المجتمع وتسوقه الى الانحلال الاخلاقي. النفس اللوامة: وهي الضمير الذي يؤنب الانسان على ما اقترفه من السيئات والآثام ولاسيما بعدما يفيق من سكراتها وينحدر في دوامة الندم على ما ارتكبه وهذا يدل على ان النفس ممزوجة بالميل الى الشهوات وفي الوقت نفسه فيها ميل الى الحق والعدل ولكل تجل ظروفه فان غلبة الشهوات يحول دون ظهور نور الحق فيقترف المعاصي والآثام ولكنه ما ان تخمد شهوته يصفو أمامه جمال الحياة وتنكشف مضرات اللذة فتستيقظ النفس وتأخذ باللوم والعذل الى حد ربما تدفع صاحبها الى الانتحار لعدم تحمله وطأة تلك الجريمة. والخلاصة ان النفس اللوامة هي تلك التي إذا فعل الانسان سوءا لامته وانبته على فعله واليها اشار القرآن الكريم بقوله (لَا أُقْسِمُ بِيَوْمِ الْقِيَامَةِ ، وَلَا أُقْسِمُ بالنفس اللوامة). واختلف المفسرون في المراد من النفس اللوامة على آراء أهمها: انها النفس الكافرة الفاجرة التي تلوم نفسها يوم القيامة إن كانت عملت خيرا قالت : هلا ازددت . وإن كانت عملت سوءا قالت ليتني لم افعل . (۲) : ان المراد نفس المؤمن التي تلومه في الدنيا على ارتكاب المعصية وتحفزه على اصلاح ما بدا منه . والظاهر ان القول الثاني هو المتعين فالآية الكريمة تحكي عن المنزلة العظيمة التي تتمتع بها النفس اللوامة الى حد أقسم بها سبحانه. النفس المطمئنة: وهي النفس التي توصلها النفس اللوامة الى حد لا تعصف بها عواصف الشهوة وتطمئن برحمة الرب وتحس بالمسؤولية الموضوعة على عاتقها أمام الله وامام المجتمع يقول سبحانه (يَا أَيَّتُهَا النَّفْسُ الْمُطْمَئِنَّةُ . ارْجِعِي إِلَى رَبِّك رَاضِيَةً مَرْضِيّةً ، فَادْخُلي في عبادي ، وادخلي جنتي)؛ فصاحب هذه النفس يمتلىء بالسرور والفرح عند الطاعة وتجد في صميمها لذة للطاعة وحلاوة.
Imam Ali (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed the soul is a persistent enjoiner of evil and immorality; whoever trusts it, it betrays him; whoever relies on it, it destroys him; whoever is pleased with it, leads him to an evil outcome.' Moral exhortations came to modify that inclination and motivate the soul to proceed on the path of happiness and avoid excess and negligence. Materialistic theories called for submission to the desires of pleasure, while monasticism called for curbing the reins of pleasures and desires and abstaining from life in caves and monasteries. But Islam called for a middle path between them: 'Say, who has forbidden the adornment of Allah which He has produced for His servants and the good [lawful] things of provision?' (7:32) and at the same time commands curbing the soul from committing sins and evils that cause chaos in society and lead it to moral decay. The blaming soul (al-nafs al-lawwama): It is the conscience that reproaches a person for the evils and sins he has committed, especially after he awakens from their intoxication and descends into the whirlpool of regret for what he has done. This indicates that the soul is mixed with inclination to desires, and at the same time has an inclination to truth and justice. Each has its own conditions. If desires overcome, it prevents the emergence of the light of truth, so he commits sins and evil deeds. But when his desire subsides, the beauty of life becomes clear before him and the harms of pleasure are revealed, so the soul awakens and begins to blame and reproach to the point that it may drive its owner to suicide due to inability to bear the weight of that crime. In summary, the blaming soul is the one that, when a person does evil, blames him and rebukes him for his action. The Holy Quran referred to it with His saying: 'I swear by the Day of Resurrection, and I swear by the blaming soul' (75:1-2). The exegetes differed concerning the meaning of the blaming soul into views, the most important of which: (1) it is the disbelieving, immoral soul that blames itself on the Day of Resurrection; if it had done good it says: why did I not increase? and if it had done evil it says: would that I had not done it. (2) it is the believing soul that blames him in this world for committing sin and urges him to reform what has occurred. The apparent view is the second, for the noble verse speaks of the great station that the blaming soul enjoys to the extent that He swore by it. The tranquil soul (al-nafs al-mutma'inna): It is the soul to which the blaming soul leads to the point that the storms of desire do not overwhelm it, and it is tranquil through the mercy of the Lord and feels the responsibility placed upon its shoulders before Allah and before society. He says, exalted: 'O tranquil soul, return to your Lord, well-pleased and pleasing [to Him], and enter among My servants, and enter My Paradise' (89:27-30). The possessor of this soul is filled with joy and happiness at obedience, and finds in its core the pleasure of obedience and sweetness.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the three types of soul (nafs) as described in the Quran and Islamic tradition. The author begins with the first type, al-nafs al-ammara (the soul that commands evil), citing a saying of Imam Ali that emphasizes its treacherous nature. He then contrasts materialistic and monastic approaches with the Islamic middle path, referencing Quran 7:32. Next, he defines al-nafs al-lawwama (the blaming soul) as the conscience that reproaches a person for sin, explaining its dual inclination toward desire and truth. He notes that when desire subsides, the soul blames itself, potentially leading to extreme regret. He then discusses two exegetical interpretations of the 'blaming soul' in Quran 75:1-2, favoring the view that it refers to the believer who repents in this world. Finally, he defines al-nafs al-mutma'inna (the tranquil soul) as the result of the blaming soul's work, where the person attains peace, responsibility, and joy in obedience, citing Quran 89:27-30. Throughout, the author uses Quranic verses and scholarly opinions to support the definitions.

Key Terms
النفس
النفس الأمارة
النفس اللوامة
النفس المطمئنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Al-nafs al-lawwama
C) Al-nafs al-mutma'inna
D) Al-nafs al-kafira

Key Takeaway
The three types of nafs represent a spiritual progression: the soul that commands evil, the soul that blames itself, and the soul that finds peace through obedience.
