The Tranquil and Satisfied Soul: Two Stages of Spiritual Perfection
Author defines two soul types and their characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the tranquil soul from the satisfied and pleasing soul and explain the relationship between servant satisfaction and divine pleasure as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
The satisfied and pleasing soul is the perfect stage of the tranquil soul, where the servant's satisfaction earns God's pleasure, uniquely honored in the Qur'an.

In this passage, the author defines two pivotal terms in Islamic spiritual psychology: the tranquil soul and the satisfied and pleasing soul. Understanding their distinct characteristics and the progression from one to the other is essential for grasping the concept of servitude ('ubudiyya) and divine pleasure. Let's unpack the definitions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two soul types
Locate the names of the two soul types in the Arabic passage: النفس المطمئنة and النفس الراضية المرضية. Notice that the second is introduced as a completion of the first.
The second soul type is described as 'التي' (the one which) indicating an elaboration.
2
Extract characteristics of the tranquil soul
From the first part, list traits: it rests content with its Lord, sees itself as a servant owning nothing, views worldly conditions as divine tests, stays balanced between excess and deficiency.
The tranquil soul is the foundation.
3
Extract characteristics of the satisfied and pleasing soul
From the second part, list traits: it is perfect (متكاملة), satisfied with the Lord, pleasing to the Lord, its tranquility necessitates satisfaction with all divine decrees (creative and legislative), it does not fall into anger or sin.
This soul has both راضية (satisfied) and مرضية (pleasing).
4
Analyze the relationship between the two
Note that the passage states that tranquility leads to satisfaction, and satisfaction leads to being pleasing. The author says: 'When the servant is satisfied with his Lord, he adheres to the path of servitude, and that merits his Lord's satisfaction.'
The coupling of 'satisfied' and 'pleasing' is deliberate.
5
Connect to the Quranic verse
The author references the verse 'And enter My Paradise' (ادخلي جنتي) and explains that the attribution of Paradise to God's self in this verse is unique, indicating special honor for the satisfied and pleasing soul.
This verse is the climax of Surah al-Fajr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفس المطمئنة هي التي تسكن الى ربها وترضى بما رضي به فترى نفسها عبدا لا يملك لنفسه شيئا من خير او شر أو نفع أو ضر ويرى الدنيا دار مجاز وما يستقبله فيها من غنى او فقر أو أي نفع وضر ابتلاء وامتحانا إلهيا فلا يدعوه تواتر النعم عليه الى الطغيان واكثار الفساد والعلو والاستكبار ولا يوقعه الفقر والفقدان في الكفر وترك الشكر بل هو في مستقر من العبودية لا ينحرف عن مستقيم صراطه بافراط او تفريط. ثم النفس الراضية المرضية: وهي النفس المتكاملة الراضية من ربها والتي رضى الرب منها واطمئنانها الى ربها يستلزم رضاها بما قدر وقضى تكوينا أو حكم به تشريعا فلا تسخطها سانحة ولا تزيغها معصية واذا رضى العبد من ربه لزم طريق العبودية استوجب ذلك رضى ربه ولذا عقب قوله (راضية) بقوله (مرضية) وفي قوله تعالى : وادخلي جنتي تعيين لمستقرها وفي إضافة الجنة الى ضمير المتكلم تشريف خاص ولا يوجد في كلام الله تعالى إضافة الجنة الى نفسه تعالى إلا في هذه الآية.
The tranquil soul is the one that rests content with its Lord and is pleased with what He is pleased with, so it sees itself as a servant that owns nothing for itself of good or evil, benefit or harm, and it sees the world as a realm of transience, and what it encounters therein of wealth or poverty or any benefit or harm as a divine trial and test. Thus, the succession of blessings does not drive it to tyranny, corruption, arrogance, and pride, nor does poverty and loss cause it to fall into disbelief and ingratitude. Rather, it remains in the station of servitude, not deviating from its straight path by excess or deficiency. Then the satisfied and pleasing soul: it is the perfect soul that is satisfied with its Lord and with whom the Lord is pleased. Its tranquility towards its Lord necessitates its satisfaction with what He has decreed and ordained, whether regarding creation or legislation. No misfortune makes it angry, nor does any sin cause it to stray. When the servant is satisfied with his Lord, he adheres to the path of servitude, and that merits his Lord's satisfaction. Hence, He followed His statement (satisfied) with (pleasing). And in His saying: 'And enter My Paradise' there is an assignment of its abode, and in the attribution of Paradise to the first-person pronoun there is a special honor. There is no mention in God's speech of Paradise attributed to Himself except in this verse.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines two levels of the soul: the tranquil soul (al-nafs al-mutma'inna) and the satisfied and pleasing soul (al-nafs al-rāḍiyya al-marḍiyya). The author first describes the tranquil soul as one that rests content with its Lord, seeing itself as a servant devoid of ownership, and viewing worldly circumstances as divine tests. This soul remains balanced, neither corrupted by plenty nor driven to despair by poverty. Then the author elaborates on the satisfied and pleasing soul, which is a perfected version: it is fully satisfied with God's decree and with whom God is pleased. Its satisfaction is comprehensive, covering both creative and legislative decrees. The author notes that when a servant is satisfied with God, he follows the path of servitude, which in turn earns God's pleasure. The author explains that the Quranic phrase 'satisfied and pleasing' couples the servant's satisfaction with God's pleasure, and the honor of Paradise being attributed to God in 'Enter My Paradise' is unique to this verse.

Key Terms
النفس المطمئنة
النفس الراضية المرضية
العبودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the tranquil soul see the world as?
A) A permanent abode
B) A realm of transience
C) A place for accumulation of wealth
D) A source of endless pleasure

Key Takeaway
The satisfied and pleasing soul is the perfected stage of the tranquil soul, where the servant's satisfaction with God results in God's pleasure, uniquely honored in the Qur'an.

The Greater Jihad: Internal Struggle
Author presents sayings about jihad against the self as the greater struggle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key differences between the lesser jihad and the greater jihad as presented in the passage, and recognize self-admiration as a disease of the self.

Core Idea
The greater jihad is the internal struggle against one's own desires, not external combat.

This passage presents a fundamental Islamic teaching: the internal spiritual struggle (jihad al-nafs) is considered greater than external combat. It quotes the Prophet Muhammad and Imams Ali, al-Baqir, and al-Sadiq to emphasize the primacy of self-purification and the dangers of self-admiration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of jihad mentioned
Notice that the Prophet distinguishes between the 'lesser jihad' (al-jihad al-asghar) and the 'greater jihad' (al-jihad al-akbar). The lesser jihad refers to physical combat, while the greater jihad is internal struggle.
2
Understand the definition of preaching (al-wa'z)
The passage defines al-wa'z as a rebuke accompanied by intimidation and a reminder of good. It is presented as an influential factor in souls, aiding in the greater jihad.
This definition comes from al-Khalil ibn Ahmad and is linked to Quranic usage.
3
Analyze Imam al-Baqir's description of the believer's struggle
Read the detailed description: the believer is constantly striving against his self, sometimes succeeding and sometimes falling, but God rescues him. This illustrates the ongoing nature of jihad al-nafs.
The phrase 'a believer is preoccupied with striving against his self' highlights its centrality.
4
Recognize self-admiration as a disease
The passage concludes by identifying 'al-'ujb bil-nafs' (self-admiration) as a disease of the self that leads to destruction, quoting Imam al-Sadiq.
This distinguishes self-admiration from other spiritual ailments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهاد النفس لا شك ان الوعظ من العوامل المؤثرة في النفوس { الوعظ : زجر مقترن بتخويف . قال الخليل بن احمد الفراهيدي هو التذكير بالخير كقوله تعالى : يعظكم الله لعلكم تذكرون} روي ان رسول الله محمد صلى الله عليه وآله رأى بعض اصحابه منصرفا من بعث كان بعثه وقد انصرف بشعثه وغبار سفره وسلاحه يريد منزله فقال : انصرفوا من الجهاد الاصغر الى الجهاد الاكبر فقيل له : او جهاد فوق الجهاد بالسيف ؟ قال : نعم جهاد المرء نفسه . وعن امير المؤمنين علي عليه السلام : مجاهدة النفس شيمة النبلاء . وينبغي للعاقل ان لا يخلو في كل حال من طاعة ربه و مجاهدة نفسه . وعن الامام محمد بن علي الباقر عليه السلام قال : ان المؤمن معنى بمجاهدة نفسه ليغلبها على هواها فمرة يقيم اودها ويخالف هواها في محبة الله ومرة تصرعه نفسه فيتبع هواها فينعشه الله فينتعش ويقيل الله عثرته فيتذكر . ومن امراض النفس العجب بالنفس روي عن الامام الصادق عليه السلام : من اعجب بنفسه هلك ومن اعجب برأيه هلك .
The striving against the self (jihad al-nafs). There is no doubt that preaching is among the factors that influence souls. Preaching: a rebuke accompanied by intimidation. Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi said: it is reminding of good, as in His saying: 'God preaches to you that you may remember.' It is narrated that the Messenger of God, Muhammad, peace be upon him and his family, saw some of his companions returning from a mission he had sent them, and they were returning with dust and travel gear and weapons, heading to their homes. He said: 'You have returned from the lesser jihad to the greater jihad.' It was said to him: 'Is there a jihad greater than jihad with the sword?' He said: 'Yes, the jihad of a person against his own self.' And from the Commander of the Faithful, Ali, peace be upon him: 'Striving against the self is the characteristic of the noble.' And it is befitting for the intelligent person not to be, in any situation, free from obeying his Lord and striving against his self. And from Imam Muhammad ibn Ali al-Baqir, peace be upon him, he said: 'Indeed, the believer is preoccupied with striving against his self to overcome its desires. Sometimes he straightens its crookedness and opposes its desire out of love for God, and sometimes his self overthrows him, so he follows its desire, then God rescues him so he recovers, and God overlooks his slip so he remembers.' And among the diseases of the self is self-admiration. It is narrated from Imam al-Sadiq, peace be upon him: 'Whoever admires himself perishes, and whoever admires his opinion perishes.'
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'jihad al-nafs' (striving against the self) as the greater jihad. It begins by affirming the importance of preaching as an influential factor in souls, defining preaching as a rebuke with intimidation and a reminder of good. The core teaching is attributed to the Prophet Muhammad: after a military expedition, he told his companions that they had returned from the lesser jihad to the greater jihad, and clarified that the greater jihad is the struggle against one's own self. The author then quotes Imam Ali stating that striving against the self is the trait of the noble. Next, the passage advises that the intelligent person should always engage in obeying God and striving against his self. A detailed description from Imam al-Baqir illustrates the believer's ongoing struggle against his own desires: sometimes he overcomes it, sometimes he falls, but God rescues him. Finally, the passage identifies self-admiration as a disease of the self, quoting Imam al-Sadiq that one who admires himself perishes. Thus, the passage establishes the primacy of internal spiritual struggle over external combat.

The Greater Jihad and Self-Conceit
Defines jihad al-nafs and warns against self-conceit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the lesser and greater jihad and identify the signs and dangers of self-conceit ('ujb) using Islamic narrations.

Core Idea
The greater struggle is against the self; self-conceit is a blind disease that destroys the soul.

This passage from the Islamic ethical tradition clarifies that the true 'greater jihad' is not physical combat but the inner struggle against one's own desires. It also warns against self-conceit ('ujb), a spiritual disease that makes a person see all virtue in himself and no fault. By analyzing the narrations, we learn how to recognize and cure this disease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two jihads
Find the hadith where the Prophet says you have returned from the lesser jihad to the greater jihad. What are these two jihads?
Lesser jihad is physical struggle; greater jihad is struggling against one's own soul.
2
Understand the definition of wa'z
The author defines wa'z (admonition) as a deterrent with fear. Note the Quranic verse cited and the definition by al-Khalil.
Wa'z is a tool for the greater jihad.
3
Extract the characteristics of the self-conceited person
From Imam al-Sadiq's narration, identify the fool's traits: conceited about his opinion and himself, sees all virtue for himself, obliges all right to himself.
These are core signs of 'ujb.
4
Analyze the consequences of 'ujb
From Imam Ali's sayings, note how self-conceit leads to perdition and how to cure it by reflecting on Allah's power and one's own weakness.
Cure involves humility and remembering one's lowly origin and end.

There is no doubt that admonition (wa'z) is among the factors affecting souls. {Admonition: a deterrent accompanied by intimidation. Al-Khalil ibn Ahmad al-Farahidi said: It is reminding of good, as His saying: 'Allah admonishes you so that you may remember.'} It is narrated that the Messenger of Allah Muhammad (peace be upon him) saw some of his companions returning from a mission he had sent them on, and they had returned with their disheveled state, travel dust, and weapons, intending to go to their homes. He said: 'You have returned from the lesser jihad to the greater jihad.' It was said to him: 'Is there a jihad above jihad with the sword?' He said: 'Yes, a man's jihad against his own soul.' (Struggle against yourselves regarding your desires so that wisdom may settle in your hearts.) And from the Commander of the Faithful, Ali (peace be upon him): 'Struggling against the soul is the characteristic of the noble.' (It is fitting for the intelligent person never to be free, in any state, from obedience to his Lord and struggling against his soul.) Sometimes he straightens its deviation and opposes its whims for the love of Allah, and sometimes his soul throws him down so he follows its whims, then Allah raises him so he rises, and Allah excuses his slip so he remembers.' And among the diseases of the soul is self-conceit. It is narrated from Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him): 'Whoever is conceited about himself perishes, and whoever is conceited about his opinion perishes. And Jesus son of Mary (peace be upon him) said: I treated the sick and healed them by Allah's permission, and I cured the blind and the leper by Allah's permission, and I treated the dead and brought them to life by Allah's permission, and I treated the fool and could not reform him.' So it was said: 'O Spirit of God, and what is the fool?' He said: 'The one who is conceited about his opinion and himself, who sees all virtue for himself and none against him, and who obliges all right for himself and does not oblige any right against himself.' Imam Ali (peace be upon him) says: 'What is the son of Adam and conceit? His beginning is a filthy drop, and his end is a filthy corpse, and between that he carries filth.' (If you are amazed by what you have of your authority, and arrogance or pride befalls you, then look at the greatness of Allah's kingdom and His power regarding what you cannot do for yourself; for that will soften your wing, restrain your arrogance, and return to you what had left your intellect.)

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of jihad al-nafs (struggle against the soul) and identifies self-conceit ('ujb) as a spiritual disease. The author begins by defining wa'z (admonition) as a deterrent with fear, citing al-Khalil and a Quranic verse. He then presents the famous hadith about the lesser and greater jihad, where the Prophet declares fighting one's own soul as the greater struggle. This is followed by narrations from Imam Ali and Imam al-Baqir emphasizing the importance of mujahadat al-nafs. The discussion then shifts to diseases of the soul, focusing on 'ujb. The author provides a narration from Imam al-Sadiq, including a story of Jesus healing the sick but being unable to reform the fool, defined as one conceited about his opinion and himself. Finally, Imam Ali's sayings condemn conceit, advising humility by contemplating Allah's power. All examples are drawn from Islamic sources, reinforcing the theological and spiritual framework.

The Soul's Journey After Death in Islamic Philosophy
The soul's transition to barzakh and its embodiment in hayula

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the stages of the soul's descent and ascent as described in the passage, and define the key terms barzakh and hayula in the context of Islamic eschatology.

Core Idea
The soul descends from the immaterial world and ascends wearing the garment of its deeds.

This passage from a work of Islamic philosophy outlines the soul's trajectory from its immaterial origin, through earthly life, to its post-mortem state in barzakh. The author integrates traditions from Imam al-Sadiq, Quranic exegesis, and even modern medicine to describe the subtle body (hayula) that houses the soul in the intermediate realm. Understanding this journey is essential for grasping the Islamic philosophical account of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stages of the soul's journey
Look for the three key moments: descent into the body (ensoulment at four months), life in the world with transformations, and ascent after death to the immaterial world.
The passage mentions both a descent 'from the world beyond matter' and an ascent 'to the world from which it came'.
2
Examine the concept of barzakh
Note that the author describes barzakh as the intermediate realm where the soul dwells after death, not merely a barrier as in some Quranic interpretations.
In Quran, barzakh is a barrier; here it becomes a realm of subtle bodies.
3
Analyze the nature of the hayula body
The passage states the soul inhabits a 'body called hayula' in barzakh, which is a subtle, immaterial vehicle resembling the worldly form.
This shifts from Aristotelian prime matter to a spiritual body.
4
Interpret the metaphor of the garment
The soul descends 'pure spirit' and ascends 'wearing the garment of the soul and what it earned in the worldly life' — indicating deeds shape the post-mortem state.
This reflects the idea that the soul's moral and spiritual acquisitions accompany it.
5
Trace the source of ensoulment timing
The passage cites exegesis and modern medicine to assert that the spirit enters the fetus after four months of gestation.
This links the philosophical discussion to empirical observation and authoritative tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ان النفس بعد الموت تنتقل الى عالم البرزخ وتسكن جسما يعبر عنه بالهيولى وعلامة الموت كما يقول الامام الصادق جعفر بن محمد إذا جمد الدم فارق الروح (البدن ان الروح : النفس) تهبط من عالم ما وراء المادة الى الجسد وفي كتب التفسير (أن ولوج الروح الى الجسد بعد اربعة أشهر من حمل الجنين في بطن امه وهو ما تؤيده كتب الطب الحديث. وبعد ان تمر النفس بالتحولات تتجرد ثانية فتصعد الى العالم الذي جاءت منه . تنزل روحا مجردة وتصعد مرتدية ثوب النفس وما كسبت في الحياة الدنيا. قال الامام الصادق عل السلام (ان الجسم الهيولي الذي تسكن فيه الروح في البرزخ يشبه تماما في شكله
Indeed, after death the soul moves to the world of barzakh and dwells in a body which is called hayula. The sign of death, as Imam al-Sadiq Ja'far ibn Muhammad says: when the blood congeals, the spirit departs. (The body: the spirit is the soul.) It descends from the world beyond matter to the body. And in books of exegesis: that the insertion of the spirit into the body occurs after four months of the fetus's gestation in its mother's womb, which is supported by modern medical books. And after the soul undergoes transformations, it becomes disembodied again and ascends to the world from which it came. It descends as a pure spirit and ascends wearing the garment of the soul and what it earned in the worldly life. Imam al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the hayula body in which the spirit dwells in barzakh completely resembles in its form...'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage discusses the soul's journey after death. The author states that the soul passes to the barzakh (intermediate realm) and inhabits a subtle body called hayula. The sign of death, quoting Imam al-Sadiq, is the congealing of blood and departure of the spirit. The parenthetical note clarifies that spirit (ruh) is synonymous with soul (nafs) in this context. The soul descends from the immaterial world into the physical body at the time of ensoulment, which occurs four months after conception, as supported by both exegesis and modern medicine. After undergoing earthly transformations, the soul is divested of the physical body and ascends to its original world, carrying the 'garment' of its deeds. The quote from Imam al-Sadiq indicates that the barzakh body (jism hayuli) closely resembles the worldly form. This explanation blends theological, philosophical (Platonic/Neoplatonic) and medical perspectives typical of Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
البرزخ
الهيولى
الروح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the physical grave
B) In the world of barzakh
C) In the heavens immediately
D) In a new material body

Key Takeaway
The soul's journey after death involves descent into the body, earthly life, and ascent to the immaterial world, with a subtle barzakh body housing the soul in the interim.

The Epistemological Balance: Al-Mizan
Author introduces al-mizan as a comprehensive epistemological balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two types of al-mizan and describe how it functions as a comprehensive measure in human knowledge.

Core Idea
Al-mizan is the divine balance that measures all things: material, intellectual, and spiritual.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of al-mizan as a fundamental element in the birth of human knowledge. Unlike a mere physical scale, al-mizan encompasses all means of measurement, including beliefs, actions, and thoughts. We will break down its definition, types, and the Quranic evidence that supports its broader meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-mizan
Find where the author explicitly defines al-mizan. Look for the phrase 'what is meant by al-mizan is everything that weighs, i.e., measures a thing'.
The author expands the definition beyond physical scales.
2
List the elements of knowledge al-mizan receives
The passage enumerates multiple sources: senses, intellect, heart, insights, inspiration, innate nature, revelation, guidance, dreams, and memory. List them in order.
This shows the comprehensive role of al-mizan in human cognition.
3
Distinguish the two types of al-mizan
The author classifies al-mizan into two types: the balance of weights and commerce (physical) and the balance of actions and sayings (moral). Identify the supporting Quranic verses.
The second type is broader and includes ethical and legal standards.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Read the quoted verses from Surah al-Hadid (57:25) and Surah al-Rahman (55:7-9). Note how they link al-mizan to justice and the broader cosmic order.
The placement after 'raising the heaven' indicates a universal scope.
5
Summarize Sayyid Fadlallah's expansion
The author quotes Sayyid Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, who explains that al-mizan defines limits and ensures uprightness in thought, judgment, and value, based on divine revelation.
This reinforces the extension of al-mizan to all human affairs.

-١٧- الميزان : عنصر جوهري في ولادة المعرفة البشرية وله اهمية بالغة فهو يستلم جميع عناصر المعرفة ( من الحواس والعقل والقلب والبصائر والالهام والفطرة والايحاء والهداية والرؤيا المنامية والذاكرة ) ويقارن بين الصواب والخطأ ويقدم ما يشبه تقريرا موجزا متوازنا الى النفس لتتخذ قراراتها والميزان على نوعين ميزان الاثقال والتجارة وميزان الافعال والاقوال بدليل قوله تعالى ( وأَنْزَلْنَا مَعَهُمُ الْكِتَابَ وَالْمِيزَانَ لِيَقُومَ النَّاسُ بِالْقِسْطِ ) ( وَالسَّمَاءَ رَفَعَهَا وَوَضَعَ الميزان • ألا تَطْغَوا فِي الْمِيزَانِ، وَأَقِيمُوا الْوَزْنَ بِالْقِسْطِ وَلَا تُخْسِرُوا الْمِيزَانَ ) فالمراد من الميزان كل ما يوزن أي يقدر به الشيء وهو أعم من ان يكون عقيدة أو قولا أو فعلا ومن مصاديقه الميزان الذي يوزن به الاثقال ) ( المیزان لغة المقياس وهو وسيلة لوزن الاجسام المادية والذي ذكر بعد خلق السماء له مفهوم واسع لا ينحصر بقياس الاوزان المادية فقط بل يشمل كل وسيلة للقياس بما في ذلك القوانين التشريعية والتكوينية ) وبعبارة السيد محمد حسين فضل الله الميزان الذي يحدد للناس حدود الاشياء وموازينها بالطريقة التي لا تنحرف في دائرة التصور عن الاستقامة في الفكر في الحكم وفي القيمة لان هناك قاعدة في الوحي الالهي تضبط ذلك كله من خلال الخطوط الموضوعية لتقدير القيم والقضايا والافكار كما تنطلق هذه القاعدة لتحدد خطوط السير في دائرة الحركة الى المواقع التي يريد الله للانسان أن يتحرك فيها وهكذا يمتد الامر الى العلاقات الانسانية التي جعل الله لها حدودا وركز لها ميزانا يزن
Al-Mizan (the balance) is an essential element in the birth of human knowledge and has great importance. It receives all the elements of knowledge (from the senses, intellect, heart, insights, inspiration, innate nature, revelation, guidance, dreams, and memory) and compares between right and wrong, presenting a kind of brief, balanced report to the soul for it to make its decisions. Al-mizan is of two types: the balance of weights and commerce, and the balance of actions and sayings, as evidenced by His statement: 'And We sent down with them the Book and the Balance so that people may uphold justice' (Quran 57:25) and 'And the heaven He raised and set the balance, that you not transgress in the balance. And establish weight in justice and do not diminish the balance' (Quran 55:7-9). So what is meant by al-mizan is everything that weighs, i.e., measures a thing, and it is more general than being a belief, a saying, or an action. Among its instances is the balance used to weigh physical weights. Al-mizan linguistically means the scale, and it is a means to weigh material objects, and its mention after the creation of the heavens has a broad meaning not limited to measuring physical weights but includes every means of measurement, including legislative and constitutional laws. According to Sayyid Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, al-mizan defines for people the limits of things and their scales, in a way that does not deviate in the sphere of conception from uprightness in thought, judgment, and value, because there is a basis in divine revelation that controls all of that through objective lines for estimating values, issues, and ideas. This basis also determines the lines of conduct in the sphere of action to the positions that God wants man to move towards. Thus the matter extends to human relations, for which God has set limits and established a balance that weighs.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of al-mizan (the balance) as a fundamental element in the generation of human knowledge. The author begins by asserting that al-mizan is an essential component: it receives all the elements of knowledge—senses, intellect, heart, insights, inspiration, innate nature, revelation, guidance, dreams, and memory—and compares truth and falsehood, presenting a balanced report to the soul. Then the author classifies al-mizan into two types: the physical balance used in commerce and the balance of actions and sayings. This classification is supported by two Quranic verses: one referring to the Book and Balance sent with prophets (Quran 57:25) and the other to the balance set in the heavens (Quran 55:7-9). The author then expands the meaning of al-mizan to encompass any measure or standard, not limited to physical scales but including beliefs, statements, and actions. Linguistically, mizan means scale, but its Quranic context after the creation of the heavens indicates a broader concept that includes all means of measurement, such as legislative laws and constitutional principles. The passage concludes with a quote from Sayyid Muhammad Husayn Fadlallah, who emphasizes that al-mizan sets limits for things and ensures uprightness in thought, judgment, and value, guided by divine revelation. This illustrates the extension of the concept to all aspects of human life, including ethics and social relations. The discussion is grounded in the Islamic intellectual tradition, using Quranic evidence to establish the metaphysical and epistemological function of balance.

Four Philosophical Criteria for Truth and Illusion
Epistemological critique of four philosophical answers to the criterion of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically evaluate four philosophical theories on the criterion of truth and identify their shortcomings using logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Popularity, usefulness, and relativity are not reliable measures of truth.

Philosophers have long debated how to distinguish truth from illusion. This lesson presents four famous answers, each accompanied by a concise logical critique. By understanding where these theories fail, you will strengthen your own ability to discern objective truth.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central question
Read the opening line: 'ما معيار كون الشيء حقيقة أو وهما؟' Ask yourself: what exactly is being sought?
The criterion (miʿyār) must be a reliable standard.
2
Examine Comte's social consensus theory
Identify the claim: truth is what society accepts. Note the example of geocentrism vs. heliocentrism. Then read the critique: popularity does not determine truth; eternal truths (e.g., math, ethics) are independent of opinion.
The critique uses counterexamples that are fixed across time.
3
Examine James' pragmatism
Identify the claim: truth is what is useful or beneficial. Then read the critique: this cannot apply to physical/chemical/mathematical laws, which are true regardless of utility.
The critique emphasizes that natural laws are objective.
4
Examine Fauconnie's relativism
Identify the claim: truth varies with time and place. Then read the critique: the example (changing description of a friend) confuses change in the subject with change in truth; contradictory truths cannot both be correct.
The critique points out a logical flaw in the example.
5
Examine Bacon's empiricism
Identify the claim: experience and experimentation are the criterion. Note that the author does not provide a critique here, but simply states it.
Consider why the author might not criticize this view.

معيار الحقيقة والوهم ٣ ما معيار كون الشيء حقيقة أو وهما ؟ تعددت الآراء والنظريات الفلسفية في محاولاتها الاجابة عن هذا السؤال . سنستعرض أهمها مع نقدها بايجاز شدید : ١) أجابة الفيلسوف الفرنسي أوغست كونت ۱۷۹۸ - ١٨٥٧ م الحق هو المقبول من المجتمع والوهم هو المرفوض منه ان فكرة كون الارض مركز الكواكب كانت مقبولة لدى المجتمع عامة فهي حق في زمانها وفكرة كون الشمس هي مركز الكواكب مقبولة اليوم فهي حق في زمانها. تعليق: ليس الكثرة دليل الحق ولا القلة دليل الباطل لان الحق والباطل عبارة عن قيم وجدانية لا علاقة لها بالكثرة أو القلة. ان الرأي العام ليس هو معيار الحق من الوهم دائما فالحقائق الرياضية مثلا او القوانين الاخلاقية والمنطقية لا يتبدل الرأي العام فيها فان ٢٢-٤ هذه حقيقة ثابتة سواءا اتفق افراد المجتمع ام لا . أو ان الخير محمود والشر مذموم وهكذا نجد جملة كثيرة جدا من الحقائق ثابتة خالدة لجميع البشر في جميع انحاء الارض ولكل مراحل التاريخ . ٢ ) أجابة الفيلسوف وليم جيمس ١٨٤٢ - ١٩١٥م الحق ما كان مفيدا او ناجحا او نافعا في الحياة . تعليق هذا القول لا يمكن الاخذ به لان هذه النظرية لا يمكن تعميمها على القوانين الفيزيائية والكيميائية والرياضية ولا علاقة للضرر والنفع بحقيقة ان الارض تدور حول الشمس) او (ان الذرة مكونة من الكترون وبروتون ونيوترون) ٣ ) أجابة الفيلسوف بول فورییه ۱۷۷۲ - ۱۸۳۷م الحقيقة أمر نسبي لا مطلق يختلف بحسب الظروف الزمانية والمكانية. فانت تصف احد الاصدقاء في فترة من الفترات بالاخلاص وتصفه في فترة اخرى بالخيانة وكلا الوصفين صحيح في مرحلته فلا توجد حقيقة ثابتة البتة. تعليق : ان ما ذكر من مثال لايمت بصلة الى النسبية فان الرأي الاول يرفض الرأي الثاني فلم تجتمع حقيقتان متناقضتان لتقويم الشخص بل ان الشخص نفسه غير تصرفاته من حال الى حال . في حين موضوعنا الاصل هو كيف نميز بين الحق والوهم . أي الصواب من الخطأ ٤ ( أجابة الفيلسوف الانجليزي فرانسيس بيكون ١٥٦١ - ١٦٢٦م التجربة هي معيار تمييز الحقيقة عن الاوهام أي ان العمل بالتجارب والتاكد من صحتها هو المقياس والمعيار.
The Criterion of Truth and Illusion. What is the criterion for something being truth or illusion? Philosophical opinions and theories have multiplied in their attempts to answer this question. We will review the most important of them with a brief, strong critique. 1) Answer of the French philosopher Auguste Comte (1798–1857): Truth is what is accepted by society, and illusion is what is rejected by it. The idea that the earth is the center of the planets was accepted by society in general, so it was truth in its time, and the idea that the sun is the center of the planets is accepted today, so it is truth in its time. Comment: Multiplicity is not evidence of truth, nor is scarcity evidence of falsehood, because truth and falsehood are emotional values that have no relation to multiplicity or scarcity. Public opinion is not always the criterion of truth from illusion. For example, mathematical facts or ethical and logical laws do not change public opinion about them. For 4-2=2? [reference possibly a math fact] is a fixed truth whether individuals of society agree or not. Or that good is praiseworthy and evil is blameworthy. Thus we find a great many truths that are fixed and eternal for all humans in all parts of the earth and for all stages of history. 2) Answer of the philosopher William James (1842–1915): Truth is whatever is useful, successful, or beneficial in life. Comment: This statement cannot be accepted because this theory cannot be generalized to physical, chemical, and mathematical laws. Harm and benefit have no relation to the fact that the earth revolves around the sun, or that the atom is composed of electron, proton, and neutron. 3) Answer of the philosopher Paul F[?] (1772–1837): Truth is relative, not absolute; it changes according to temporal and spatial circumstances. You describe a friend in one period as sincere and in another period as treacherous, and both descriptions are correct in their stage; therefore there is no fixed truth at all. Comment: The mentioned example is not related to relativism, because the first opinion rejects the second opinion; two contradictory truths did not come together to evaluate the person; rather, the person himself changed his behavior from one state to another. Our original topic is how to distinguish between truth and illusion, i.e., correctness from error. 4) Answer of the English philosopher Francis Bacon (1561–1626): Experience is the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusions, i.e., working with experiments and verifying their correctness is the measure and criterion.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique of four major theories on the criterion of truth (ḥaqīqa) and illusion (wahm). It begins by posing the central question: What is the criterion for something being truth or illusion? The author then enumerates four answers with concise refutations. (1) Auguste Comte's social consensus theory: truth is what society accepts. The critique points out that truth is not determined by popularity; eternal truths like mathematical facts (e.g., 4-2=2) or moral principles (good is praiseworthy) do not depend on public opinion. (2) William James' pragmatism: truth as usefulness. The critique argues that this cannot apply to scientific laws, such as the earth's rotation or atomic structure, which are true regardless of benefit. (3) Paul Fauconnie's (or similar) relativism: truth varies with time and place. The critique demonstrates that the example given (changing description of a friend) actually shows a change in the object, not a change in truth; contradictory truths about the same thing cannot both be correct. (4) Francis Bacon's empiricism: truth is determined by sensory experience and experimentation. The passage does not provide a critique of this view, but simply states it. The overall argument is that truth is objective, fixed, and independent of consensus, utility, or relative circumstances.

وهم
معيار
نقد

Key Takeaway
Objective truths such as mathematical and ethical laws are not determined by consensus, utility, or context, but are fixed and independent.

Truth Is Absolute and Unchanging
Author states truth is constant across mathematical, moral, and logical principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of fixed truths in Islamic sciences and explain why they are not subject to public opinion.

Core Idea
Truth does not depend on consensus; it is fixed across time and place.

The author opens by stating that the criterion distinguishing truth from illusion is constant. He gives examples from mathematics, ethics, and logic to show that truth does not change with societal agreement. This foundational point is crucial for understanding how Islamic sciences treat divine commands and logical principles as objective realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the criterion of truth is constant. Underline it.
2
List the examples given

These are not subject to change by public opinion.
3
Understand the basis of constancy
Why are these truths fixed? Because they hold regardless of societal agreement and across all time and place.
This refutes relativistic views that truth changes with culture.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Think of an example from Islamic law or theology that is similarly absolute, e.g., the prohibition of shirk or murder. Does it depend on majority opinion?
The same logic applies: such rulings are fixed by revelation.

Refutation of the Pragmatist Criterion of Truth
Author presents and refutes William James' view that truth is what is useful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the pragmatic criterion of truth and explain why it fails for objective scientific facts using Islamic science examples.

Core Idea
Truth is not defined by utility; some truths are true regardless of benefit or harm.

This lesson examines a modern philosophical claim about truth: that it is defined by usefulness. The author of our text presents and refutes this view using examples from physics and chemistry. By analyzing this critique, you will learn how to evaluate theories of truth against objective reality, a skill essential in Islamic sciences where the truth of revelation is not subject to pragmatic considerations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find William James' definition of truth in the text. What exactly does he claim truth is?
2
Understand the author's objection
On what basis does the author reject this definition? Look for the phrase 'لا يمكن الاخذ به' and the reasons given.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
The author gives examples from physics and chemistry. Why do these disprove the pragmatic criterion? Consider whether these facts could ever become 'false' due to lack of benefit.
4
Assess the logical structure
The objection shows that the pragmatic criterion is too narrow: it fails to cover truths that are independent of human utility. Is this a valid refutation? Why?

Refuting Relativism: The Fallacy of Changing Circumstances
Author presents and refutes the relativist view that truth varies with time and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in a common relativist argument and explain why changing circumstances do not imply relative truth.

Core Idea
Truth is not relative; changing conditions affect the subject, not truth itself.

The philosopher Paul F. argued that truth is relative because a friend can be described as sincere at one time and treacherous at another. However, the author points out that the friend himself changed, so the two descriptions do not conflict. This lesson unpacks the fallacy and shows why a fixed criterion of truth is necessary for Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relativist claim and example
The passage presents the claim that truth varies with time and place. The example describes a friend who is sincere in one period and treacherous in another, concluding both descriptions are correct in their stage.
2
Analyze the example for contradiction
Ask: Are the two descriptions true of the same situation? The friend changed his behavior, so the descriptions apply to different states. No two contradictory truths are asserted about the same state.
3
Spot the logical flaw
The flaw is that the example confuses change in the subject with relativity of truth. The truth 'the friend was sincere at time T1' and 'the friend was treacherous at time T2' are different propositions, not contradictory.
This is a fallacy of irrelevant example.
4
Apply to Islamic context: abrogation
In usul al-fiqh, some rulings change due to naskh (abrogation). Does this imply truth is relative? No, because the ruling changed because the condition or effective cause changed, not because truth itself is relative.
For example, the prohibition of alcohol was revealed in stages; each ruling was true for its time given the community's readiness.
5
Conclude on the criterion of truth
The passage insists on a fixed criterion to distinguish truth from falsehood. Relativism is rejected because it would make knowledge impossible and undermine divine revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ ) أجابة الفيلسوف بول فورییه ۱۷۷۲ - ۱۸۳۷م الحقيقة أمر نسبي لا مطلق يختلف بحسب الظروف الزمانية والمكانية. فانت تصف احد الاصدقاء في فترة من الفترات بالاخلاص وتصفه في فترة اخرى بالخيانة وكلا الوصفين صحيح في مرحلته فلا توجد حقيقة ثابتة البتة. تعليق : ان ما ذكر من مثال لايمت بصلة الى النسبية فان الرأي الاول يرفض الرأي الثاني فلم تجتمع حقيقتان متناقضتان لتقويم الشخص بل ان الشخص نفسه غير تصرفاته من حال الى حال . في حين موضوعنا الاصل هو كيف نميز بين الحق والوهم . أي الصواب من الخطأ
3) The answer of the philosopher Paul F. (1772-1837): Truth is relative, not absolute, varying according to temporal and spatial circumstances. So you describe a friend in one period as sincere and in another period as treacherous, and both descriptions are correct in their stage; thus there is absolutely no fixed truth. Comment: The mentioned example has no connection to relativity, because the first opinion rejects the second opinion; two contradictory truths did not come together to evaluate the person; rather the person himself changed his behavior from one state to another. While our original subject is how to distinguish truth from illusion, i.e., right from wrong.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents a relativist argument using an example of a friend whose behavior changes. The author refutes by noting that the two descriptions are not simultaneously true of the same state; the person changed. Thus no real contradiction, and relativism does not follow. The author insists on a fixed criterion.

Key Terms
الحقيقة أمر نسبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's refutation, why does the example of a friend changing from sincere to treacherous not prove relativism?
A) Because one cannot describe a friend as both sincere and treacherous
B) Because the friend changed his behavior, so the two descriptions apply to different times, not the same situation
C) Because sincerity is always absolute
D) Because the example is unrelated to truth

Key Takeaway
Relativism fails because it confuses change in the subject with change in truth; a fixed criterion of truth is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement: 'Lying is always forbidden, but in some cases it is permitted to save a life.' Does this contradict the relativist claim? Explain using the author's reasoning.
?
Find an example from Islamic law where a ruling changed due to changing circumstances (e.g., the permissibility of using certain financial instruments). Argue why this does not imply relativity of truth.

Refutation of Empiricism as a Criterion of Truth
Author refutes empiricist criterion of truth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key objections to empiricism as a criterion of truth and apply them to defend the priority of rational knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Experience cannot be the final judge of truth because rational principles are its foundation.

Francis Bacon proposed that experience is the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion. However, this view collapses when we consider that primary rational knowledge—such as mathematical axioms and moral principles—is not derived from experience but rather shapes how we interpret it. The author argues that these a priori truths are prior to and judge over empirical data, making experience insufficient as a foundational criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the empiricist claim
Bacon asserts that experiment and verification through experience is the measure of truth. Locate this claim in the Arabic text: 'التجربة هي معيار تمييز الحقيقة عن الاوهام'.
Empiricism reduces all knowledge to sensory experience.
2
Find the author's counterexample
The author lists three domains of knowledge that are not subject to experiment: primary rational knowledge (المعارف العقلية الأولية), mathematical principles (المباديء الرياضية), and moral-emotional principles (الأخلاقية والعاطفية). Recognise that these are known a priori.
A priori knowledge is independent of experience.
3
Analyse the objection
The author argues that these rational principles are 'سابقة على التجربة وحاكمة عليها'—they precede experience and judge over it. This means experience itself must be interpreted using rational categories.
If experience presupposes reason, then reason cannot be derived from experience.
4
Assess the author's position
The author does not reject experience entirely but denies it the status of ultimate criterion. Consider how this aligns with Islamic epistemology, where both reason and revelation precede and guide empirical observation.
Classical Islamic thought integrates reason and revelation, not mere empiricism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ ( أجابة الفيلسوف الانجليزي فرانسيس بيكون ١٥٦١ - ١٦٢٦م التجربة هي معيار تمييز الحقيقة عن الاوهام أي ان العمل بالتجارب والتاكد من صحتها هو المقياس والمعيار. تعليق ان هذه الاجابة لا يمكن قبولها لان المعارف العقلية الاولية سابقة على التجربة وحاكمة عليها كما ان المباديء الرياضية والاخلاقية والعاطفية غير خاضعة للتجربة
4) The answer of the English philosopher Francis Bacon (1561-1626): Experience is the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusions, i.e., working with experiments and verifying their correctness is the measure and criterion. Comment: This answer cannot be accepted because primary rational knowledge precedes experience and judges over it, and also mathematical, moral, and emotional principles are not subject to experiment.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage critiques empiricism. The author argues that a priori knowledge (like mathematics and ethics) is not derived from experience but rather informs it. Thus experience cannot be the foundational criterion; rational principles are prior.

Key Terms
التجربة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject experience as the criterion of truth?
A) Because experience is always unreliable
B) Because primary rational knowledge precedes and governs experience
C) Because Islamic revelation is the only source
D) Because experiments are too difficult

Key Takeaway
Primary rational knowledge is prior to and governs experience, so experience cannot be the ultimate criterion of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a self-evident rational principle in Islamic theology (e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part') and explain why it cannot be derived from sensory experience. Then contrast it with an empirical truth like 'fire burns'.
?
Pick one of the author's examples—mathematical, moral, or emotional principles—and find a parallel in Islamic sacred law (e.g., the universal moral principle that justice is good). Argue why this principle is not subject to experimental verification.
